Why God's Knowledge Cannot Be Accidental
Refutation of knowledge as accident through logical impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's knowledge cannot be an accidental attribute requiring a substrate, based on the impossibility of changing essences and the limits of divine power.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is not an accident; accidents need substrates, and power cannot alter essential realities.

The author presents a logical refutation of the view that God's knowledge is an accident inherent in a substrate. The argument hinges on two key points: the nature of accidents requires a substrate, and divine power does not extend to changing the essence of things. This passage uses the concepts of substance (jawhar), accident (ʿaraḍ), and power (qudra) to establish that attributing accidental knowledge to God leads to impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice the logical flow and the technical terms used.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The author considers two options: either knowledge is self-sufficient (not needing a substrate) or it requires an additional factor (an agent). Understand why both lead to problems.
3
Understand the agent argument
If knowledge were caused by an agent, the agent's act does not determine the essential nature of the effect. An agent cannot turn a substance into an accident or vice versa.
This is crucial to the conclusion.
4
Relate to divine power
The argument concludes that making a substance not need a substrate is impossible, and divine power only applies to possible things. Therefore, such a change is impossible.
5
Reflect on the outcome
Synthesize the argument: God's knowledge cannot be an accident because that would require an impossible change in essence. Thus, it must be essential and not in a substrate.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذات كونه علمًا فيجب أن يكون كل علم غير محتاج إلى محل، وإما أن يكون لأمر زائد على ذات كون، علما، فيجب أن يكون فعلا لفاعل، فوجب أن يكون فعل الفاعل يوجب نفي الاحتياج في كل عرض وكل معنى، وليس الأمر كذلك، ثم فعل الفاعل لا يوجب أن ينتسب إليه المفعول بأخص وصفه الذاتي، بل إنما ينتسب إليه من حيث كونه فعلا فقط حتى يسمى فاعلا صانعا، إما أن يضاف إليه حكم العلمية حتى يصير عالما فمحال، وأيضًا فإن فعل الفاعل لا يخرج الشيء عن حقيقته فلا يجوز أن يقلب الجوهر عرضا والعرض جوهراً، فإن القدرة إنما تتعلق بما يمكن وجوده وهذا من المستحيل، فنفي الاحتياج إلى محل في حق الجوهر لا يجوز أن يثبت بالقدرة كما أن إثبات الاحتياج إلى المحل في حق العرض لا يجوز أن يثبت بالقدرة، وما ليس يمكن لا يكون مقدورًا، وما ليس بمقدور يستحيل أن يوجد.
The essence of it being knowledge necessitates that every knowledge is not in need of a substrate, and either it is due to an additional matter beyond the essence of being knowledge, then it must be an act of an agent (fāʿil). So it is necessary that the act of the agent negates the need for a substrate in every accident (ʿaraḍ) and every meaning (maʿnā), but the matter is not so. Moreover, the act of the agent does not necessitate that the object is attributed to it by its most specific essential description; rather it is attributed to it only insofar as it is an act, such that it is called an agent and maker. Either the judgment (ḥukm) of knowledge is attributed to it such that it becomes knowing (ʿālim), that is impossible. Also, the act of the agent does not remove the thing from its reality, so it is not permissible to turn a substance (jawhar) into an accident or an accident into a substance. For power (qudra) only pertains to what is possible of existence, and this is impossible. So the negation of need for a substrate with respect to substance cannot be established by power, just as the affirmation of need for a substrate with respect to an accident cannot be established by power. And what is not possible is not within power (maqdūr), and what is not within power is impossible to exist.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God's knowledge cannot be an accident inherent in a substrate. The author argues: (1) If knowledge were an accident, it would need a substrate. (2) If it were something additional to the essence, it would require an agent to create it. (3) But the act of an agent does not determine the essential nature of the effect; it only attributes the effect as an act. (4) An agent cannot change the essence of a thing (e.g., turning a substance into an accident). (5) Power (qudra) only applies to possible things; making a substance not need a substrate is impossible because it would change its essence. Therefore, divine knowledge cannot be an accidental attribute that depends on a substrate or is created by power.

Key Terms
علم
محل
جوهر
عرض
قدرة
فاعل
معنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge is an accident similar to human knowledge, inherent in a substrate.
Correction: Accidents require substrates, but God is not a substrate. Moreover, changing the essence of a substance is impossible, so divine knowledge cannot be an accident caused by power.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why cannot God's knowledge be an accident (ʿaraḍ)?
A) Because knowledge is always essential, not accidental.
B) Because accidents require a substrate, and making a substance not need a substrate is impossible for power.
C) Because God's act as agent cannot create knowledge.
D) Because knowledge is a meaning (maʿnā) that is not in a substrate.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is not an accident because accidents require a substrate and divine power cannot change essential properties.

?
Since God's knowledge is not dependent on a substrate, it is not subject to change or imperfection. How does this truth impact your trust in God's promises? Do you ever doubt God's knowledge of your situation because you think He might not fully know? How can you strengthen your reliance on His essential knowledge?

Hisham's Objection on Divine Knowledge Change
Hisham objects that God's knowledge must change after creation, and the author responds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Hisham's objection that God's knowledge must change after creation, and the author's defense that divine knowledge is not in a substrate and thus not subject to temporal change.

Core Idea
God's knowledge does not change because it is not located in a substrate.

This passage presents a classic theological objection from Hisham regarding divine knowledge. He argues that if God knew the world eternally, His knowledge must either become obsolete or change, implying a change in God. The author responds by affirming that divine knowledge is a meaning not in a substrate, thereby avoiding the dilemma. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read Hisham's objection in the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how he sets up a dilemma: after creation, God's knowledge must either remain as future knowledge (which would be ignorance) or change.
Pay attention to the logical structure: if it changes, where does the new knowledge occur?
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Hisham argues that if knowledge changes, the new knowledge must occur either in God's essence, in a substrate, or not in a substrate. He shows the first two are impossible, leaving the third, which he implies is absurd.
He also adds an argument from the occurrence of the judgment 'knowing the world as existing' and from human experience.
3
Understand the author's response
The author accepts that meanings need substrates generally, but by necessity divine knowledge is not in a substrate. He argues that denying the distinction between the two states is impossible, and a new meaning cannot inhere in the eternal or in a body.
The author concludes that divine knowledge is a meaning specifically related to the agent who created it for Himself.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Consider the human analogy: our knowledge of a future event differs from knowledge of its present occurrence. This shows that the known changes, but the knower's knowledge may be of a different kind. For God, His knowledge is not subject to change because it is not in a substrate.
This helps clarify why the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال هشام : فقد قام الدليل على أن الباري سبحانه وتعالى عالم في الأزل بما سيكون من العالم، فإذا وجد العالم هل بقي علمه علما بما سيكون أم لا ؟ فإن لم يكن علما بما سيكون، فإذا قد تجدد له حكم أو علم فلا يخلو أن يحدث ذلك المتجدد في ذاته أو في محل، أو لا في ذاته ولا في محل ، ولا يجوز أن يحدثه في ذاته كما استحالة كونه محلا للحوادث، ولا يجوز أن يحدثه في محل : لأن المعنى إذا قام حكمه إليه، فبقي أنه يحدثه لا في محل، وإن كان علمه بما سيكون باقيا على تعلقه الأول فكان جهلا ولم يكن علمًا.
ثم قال: وأيضا فإنه قد تجدد له حكم بالاتفاق وهو كونه عالما بوجود العالم وحصوله في الوقت الذي حصل، وتجدد الحكم يستدعي تجدد الصفة، كما أن تحقق الحكم يستدعي تحقق الصفة، ألستم تلقيتم كونه ذا علم من كونه عالما، فلذلك نتلقى تجدد العلم من تجدد كونه عالما حتى لو قيل : كان عالما في الأزل بكون العالم كان محالا، وكان العلم جهلا ، بل لو لم يكن العالم معلوم الكون في الأزل، فصار معلوم الكون في الوقت الذي حصل، فلم يكن الباري عالما بالكون في الأزل، ثم صار عالم الكون في الوقت المحصل ، فدل ذلك على تجدد العلم.
قال : ولا نشك بأن علمنا بأن سيقدم زيد غدًا ليس علمًا بقدومه، بل العلم بأن سيقدم غير والعلم بقدومه غير ويجد الإنسان تفرقة ضرورية بين حالتي علميه ، وهذه التفرقة راجعة إلى تجدد علم في حال القدوم ولم تكن قبل ذلك.
وأما قولكم : إن العلم من جملة المعاني وهي لذواتها محتاجة إلى محل، وأنها في ذواتها مختصة بمن له أحكامها فصحيح إلا أنا بضرورة التقسيم التزمنا كونه لا في محل ؛ إذ لا وجه لنفي التفرقة بين الحالتين، ولا وجه لتجدد المعنى في الأزلي القديم ولا في الجسم الحادث، فبالضرورة قلنا: هو معنى لا في محل له ولا ينحا به نحو الجوهر من كل وجه حتى يقال : هو قائم بالنفس ومتحيز، أو قابل للعرض، وإنما يختص حكمه بالفاعل ؛ لأن الفاعل هو الذي أوجده علما لنفسه
Hisham said: The proof has been established that the Exalted Creator is eternal knower of what will come to be of the world. When the world comes into existence, does His knowledge remain knowledge of what will be or not? If it is not knowledge of what will be, then a new judgment (ḥukm) or knowledge has occurred for Him. Then that new thing must occur either in His essence, or in a substrate (maḥall), or neither in His essence nor in a substrate. It is not permissible for it to occur in His essence, as it is impossible for Him to be a locus of events. And it is not permissible for it to occur in a substrate, because if a meaning (maʿnā) attaches to a substrate, its judgment reverts to it. So it remains that He causes it to occur not in a substrate. And if His knowledge of what will be remains upon its first relation, then it would be ignorance (jahl) and not knowledge.
Then he said: Also, a judgment has occurred for Him by agreement, namely His being knower (ʿālim) of the existence of the world and its occurrence at the time it occurred. The occurrence of judgment necessitates the occurrence of the attribute (ṣifa), just as the realization of judgment necessitates the realization of the attribute. Did you not derive His being possessor of knowledge from His being knowing? So we derive the occurrence of knowledge from the occurrence of His being knowing. Even if it were said: He was knowing in eternity of the world's being, that would be impossible, and the knowledge would be ignorance. Rather, if the world was not known to exist in eternity, then it became known to exist at the time it occurred, then the Creator was not knower of existence in eternity, then He became knower of existence at the realized time, so that indicates the occurrence of knowledge.
He said: And we do not doubt that our knowledge that Zaid will come tomorrow is not knowledge of his coming; rather knowledge that he will come is different from knowledge of his coming, and a person finds an essential distinction (tafriqa ḍarūriyya) between the two states of his knowledge. This distinction is due to the occurrence of knowledge at the time of coming which was not present before.
As for your saying: Knowledge is among the class of meanings (maʿānī), and they by their essences are in need of a substrate, and they are in their essences specific (mukhtaṣṣa) to the one who has their judgments — that is correct. However, by the necessity of division (ḍarūrat al-taqsīm) we have committed to it being not in a substrate. For there is no way to deny the distinction between the two states, and no way for the occurrence of a meaning in the eternal ancient (azalī qadīm) nor in the originated body (jism ḥādith). So by necessity we say: It is a meaning not in a substrate for it, and it is not directed (yanḥā) towards substance (jawhar) in every respect such that it is said: it is self-subsistent (qāʾim bi-l-nafs) and extended (mutaḥayyiz), or receptive of accident (qābil li-l-ʿaraḍ). Rather, its judgment is specific to the agent (fāʿil), because the agent is the one who brought it into existence as knowledge (ʿilman) for Himself.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage contains an objection from Hisham and the author's response. Hisham argues: (a) If God knew the world eternally, then after creation His knowledge must either remain as future knowledge (which would be erroneous) or change. (b) If it changes, the new knowledge must occur either in God's essence (impossible as locus of events), in a substrate (impossible because the meaning's judgment would revert to that substrate), or not in a substrate (which they claim is absurd). (c) Further, the judgment 'knowing the world as existing' occurs after non-existence, so a new attribute must occur. (d) Human experience shows a distinction between knowledge of a future event and knowledge of its present occurrence, implying new knowledge. The author responds: (1) He accepts the premise that meanings need substrates in general, but by necessity we affirm that divine knowledge is not in a substrate because denying the distinction between the two states is impossible, and a new meaning cannot inhere in the eternal or in a body. (2) Thus, divine knowledge is a meaning that is not in a substrate, not like a substance or accident in all respects, but specifically related to the agent who created it as knowledge for Himself.

Key Terms
علم
تجدد
محل
صفة
حكم
جاهل
معنى
فاعل
تفرقة ضرورية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a change in the known necessarily implies a change in God's knowledge.
Correction: The author argues that divine knowledge is not in a substrate and therefore not subject to temporal change; the change is in the object known, not in God's attribute.

Checkpoint
According to Hisham's objection, what would happen if God's knowledge remained knowledge of what will be after the world exists?
A) It would be considered complete knowledge.
B) It would be ignorance (jahl) and not knowledge.
C) It would imply God's essence changed.
D) It would require a new substrate.

Key Takeaway
The author concludes that divine knowledge is a meaning not in a substrate, created by God for Himself, and therefore not subject to change.

Change in God's Knowledge? An Argument from Renewed Judgment
Argues that the renewal of God's being knowledgeable implies renewal of His knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that if God's being knowledgeable about the world changes, then His attribute of knowledge must also change, and why this poses a challenge to traditional views of divine immutability.

Core Idea
A change in God's being knowledgeable implies a change in His knowledge.

This passage presents a logical argument rooted in the relationship between a judgment (ḥukm) and the attribute it reflects. The author contends that just as the existence of a judgment implies the existence of its corresponding attribute, so too does a renewal of the judgment imply a renewal of the attribute. Using the example of God's knowledge of the world's existence, the argument moves to an analogy with human knowledge of future events, aiming to show that the attribute of knowledge must change if what is known changes. This challenges the Ashʿarī position that God's knowledge is eternal and unchanging.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the core premise
The passage begins with the principle that the renewal of a judgment necessitates the renewal of the attribute from which it derives. Understand this causal link before proceeding.
The judgment (ḥukm) is 'being knowledgeable'; the attribute (ṣifa) is knowledge itself.
2
Apply the premise to God's case
The argument applies the premise to God: His judgment of being knowledgeable about the world's existence is renewed at the moment the world comes into existence. This must mean His attribute of knowledge is renewed.
In eternity, the world did not exist, so God was not knowledgeable of its existence in the same sense.
3
Examine the human analogy
The author draws an analogy with human knowledge: our knowledge that Zayd will come tomorrow is distinct from our knowledge that he has come. We experience a necessary distinction between these two states, indicating a renewal of knowledge.
This analogy is meant to illustrate that change in the object of knowledge entails change in knowledge itself.
4
Consider the theological implication
If the same reasoning applies to God, then His knowledge must change when what is known changes. This conflicts with the classical Ashʿarī doctrine that God's knowledge is eternal, unchanging, and encompasses all things immutably.
The argument is presented as a challenge to be refuted; it does not represent the author's own view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا فإنه قد تجدد له حكم بالاتفاق وهو كونه عالما بوجود العالم وحصوله في الوقت الذي حصل، وتجدد الحكم يستدعي تجدد الصفة، كما أن تحقق الحكم يستدعي تحقق الصفة، ألستم تلقيتم كونه ذا علم من كونه عالما، فلذلك نتلقى تجدد العلم من تجدد كونه عالما حتى لو قيل : كان عالما في الأزل بكون العالم كان محالا، وكان العلم جهلا ، بل لو لم يكن العالم معلوم الكون في الأزل، فصار معلوم الكون في الوقت الذي حصل، فلم يكن الباري عالما بالكون في الأزل، ثم صار عالم الكون في الوقت المحصل ، فدل ذلك على تجدد العلم. قال : ولا نشك بأن علمنا بأن سيقدم زيد غدا ليس علما بقدومه، بل العلم بأن سيقدم غير والعلم بقدومه غير ويجد الإنسان تفرقة ضرورية بين حالتي علميه ، وهذه التفرقة راجعة إلى تجدد علم في حال القدوم ولم تكن قبل ذلك.
And also, a judgment has been renewed for Him by consensus, which is His being knowledgeable of the existence of the world and its occurrence at the time it occurred. The renewal of the judgment necessitates the renewal of the attribute, just as the actualization of the judgment necessitates the actualization of the attribute. Did you not derive His being possessor of knowledge from His being knowledgeable? Therefore we derive the renewal of knowledge from the renewal of His being knowledgeable, such that if it were said: He was knowledgeable in eternity about the world being impossible, that would be ignorance. Rather, if the world was not known of existence in eternity, then it became known of existence at the time it occurred, then the Creator was not knowledgeable of existence in eternity, then He became knowledgeable of existence at the time of occurrence, that indicates the renewal of knowledge. He said: We do not doubt that our knowledge that Zayd will come tomorrow is not knowledge of his coming; rather, the knowledge that he will come is other than the knowledge of his coming. A person finds a necessary distinction between the two states of his knowledge, and this distinction goes back to a renewal of knowledge at the time of coming that was not there before.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage presents an argument that the change in God's being knowledgeable about the world (from not knowing its existence in eternity to knowing it at the time of its occurrence) implies a change in His attribute of knowledge. The author uses an analogy with human knowledge: our knowledge that something will happen is distinct from our knowledge that it has happened, and we perceive a necessary difference between these two states. This difference indicates that knowledge is renewed at the moment of occurrence. The same reasoning is applied to God: if His judgment of being knowledgeable changes, then His knowledge itself must change, which would contradict divine immutability. The argument is presented as a challenge to the Ash'ari position, using the consensus that God is said to be knowledgeable after the world exists.

Key Terms
تجدد الحكم
تجدد الصفة
تجدد العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that God was ignorant in eternity because He did not know the world's existence.
Correction: The argument does not claim ignorance; rather, it states that if the world was not known to exist in eternity, then God's knowledge of its existence must have been added at creation.

Checkpoint
According to the argument presented, why does the change in God's being knowledgeable imply a change in His knowledge?
A) Because knowledge is a created attribute that changes with time.
B) Because the renewal of the judgment necessitates the renewal of the attribute, as with human knowledge.
C) Because God's knowledge is identical with His essence and thus cannot change.
D) Because the world's existence causes a new attribute in God.

Key Takeaway
The argument shows that for the opponent, a change in God's being knowledgeable logically entails a change in His attribute of knowledge, challenging the doctrine of divine immutability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When something unexpected happens in your life, do you ever catch yourself thinking, 'I didn't see that coming'? How does that moment of surprise test your belief in God's eternal knowledge that included that event from all eternity?
?
You know that one day you will stand before God. But is your knowledge of that future judgment as real and present to you as the chair you are sitting on? What would change in your life if you made that future knowledge as vivid as your present awareness?

Affirming Divine Knowledge Without a Locus
Responds to objection that knowledge must reside in a locus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine knowledge cannot be in a locus and how the author defends this position using logical division.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge is a meaning without a locus, fitting its Creator who is without locus.

The author now addresses a critical objection: if knowledge is a meaning that requires a locus, how can God have knowledge without being a body or accident? He accepts the premise but argues that logical necessity forces us to conclude that divine knowledge is not in a locus. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the passage to see the objector's claim: knowledge is a meaning and meanings need a locus. This seems to conflict with God's transcendence.
The author does not reject the premise outright.
2
Follow the author's reply
The author accepts the premise but invokes 'necessity of division' (ḍarūrat al-taqsīm). He argues that neither the eternal nor the originated body can accommodate a new meaning, so the only option left is that divine knowledge has no locus.
This is a key logical move.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Divine knowledge is a meaning with no locus, not like substance or accident. Its ruling (ḥukm) is specific to the agent (the Creator) who brought it into existence as knowledge for Himself. Since the Creator is not in a locus, knowledge is also not in a locus.
This preserves God's transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولكم : إن العلم من جملة المعاني وهي لذواتها محتاجة إلى محل ، وأنها في ذواتها مختصة بمن له أحكامها فصحيح إلا أنا بضرورة التقسيم التزمنا كونه لا في محل ؛ إذ لا وجه لنفي التفرقة بين الحالتين، ولا وجه لتجدد المعنى في الأزلي القديم ولا في الجسم الحادث، فبالضرورة قلنا: هو معنى لا في محل له ولا ينحا به نحو الجوهر من كل وجه حتى يقال : هو قائم بالنفس ومتحيز، أو قابل للعرض، وإنما يختص حكمه بالفاعل ؛ لأن الفاعل هو الذي أوجده علما لنفسه فصار علما به ولأن الباري تعالى لا في محل والعلم لا في محل، فاختصاص ما لا محل له بما لا محل له أولى من اختصاص بما له مكان ومحل.
And as for your statement: 'Knowledge is among the meanings, and they in themselves need a locus, and they in themselves are specific to one who has their rulings' — it is correct, except that we, by necessity of division, have committed to it being not in a locus. For there is no way to deny the distinction between the two states, and no way for the renewal of a meaning in the eternal ancient nor in the originated body. So by necessity we said: it is a meaning that has no locus, nor is it directed towards substance in every respect such that it is said: it is self-subsisting and extended, or receptive of accident. Rather, its ruling is specific to the agent, because the agent is the one who brought it into existence as knowledge for Himself, so it became knowledge through Him. And because the Exalted Creator is not in a locus, and knowledge is not in a locus, so the specification of what has no locus to what has no locus is more appropriate than specification to what has place and locus.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection: if knowledge is a 'meaning' (ma'na) that inherently requires a locus (mahall), how can God have knowledge without a body or accident? The author accepts the premise that meanings need a locus, but argues that by logical division we must conclude that divine knowledge is not in a locus. This is because neither the eternal nor the originated body can accommodate a new meaning without implying change. Therefore, divine knowledge is a meaning with no locus, not like a substance (jawhar) or accident ('arad). Its ruling is attached solely to the agent (the Creator) who originated it as knowledge for Himself. Since the Creator is not in a locus, and knowledge is also not in a locus, it is more fitting for what is not in a locus to be associated with what is not in a locus than with what has place and locus.

Key Terms
معنى
محل
جوهر
عرض
فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if knowledge is a meaning, it must be an accident residing in a substance, making God composite.
Correction: The author affirms that divine knowledge is a meaning without a locus, not an accident. It is not like human knowledge which inheres in a mind or body.

Checkpoint
Why does the author conclude that divine knowledge is not in a locus?
A) Because knowledge cannot be attributed to God at all.
B) Because neither the eternal nor the originated body can accept a new meaning, so it must be without locus.
C) Because knowledge is an accident, and accidents cannot exist without a substance.
D) Because the objector's premise is false.

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge is a meaning without a locus, because neither eternal nor temporal can receive new meaning, and it belongs to the Creator who is without locus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that God's knowledge is not like your knowledge—it does not reside in a mind or brain. Does this truth change how you think about being known by God? How does it feel to be known by a knowledge that has no locus, no limitation? Commit tonight to sit alone for five minutes and repeat silently, 'You know me with a knowledge unlike any other, and I cannot escape it.'
?
The passage says it is 'more appropriate' for what has no locus (knowledge) to be associated with what has no locus (the Creator). Are you comfortable with a God whose attributes are so beyond your categories? Or do you secretly wish He were more 'relatable'—like a human with human-like knowledge? Honestly, what in your heart resists a God whose knowledge is not in a locus? Repent of any desire to shrink Him down.
?
The author insists that divine knowledge is a meaning with no locus, attributing its ruling solely to the agent—God. Do you live as if God's knowledge of you is an impersonal force, or as if it is a personal, agentive knowledge that He brought into existence for Himself? If it is personal, how should your prayers change tonight? Choose one word to describe the knowledge God has of you, and pray that word back to Him.

Al-Ash'ari's Doctrine of God's Immutable Knowledge
Presents al-Ash'ari's view that God's knowledge is one eternal attribute that does not change with changing objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ash'ari argues that God's knowledge is one eternal attribute that relates to all temporal events without undergoing change, answering the objection that divine knowledge would otherwise be mutable.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is one and eternal, relating to all events without change.

Al-Ash'ari addresses the theological objection from Hisham and Jahm that if God knows temporal events, His knowledge must change. He argues instead that God's knowledge is one eternal attribute that relates to all known things without itself being affected by change. This passage is a key defense of divine immutability in Ash'ari theology, relying on the nature of knowledge and an analogy with God's eternal existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Note al-Ash'ari's claim about God's knowledge not changing.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the core claim
Al-Ash'ari states that God has one eternal knowledge that encompasses all things without renewal of aspect, relation, or state for Him. Find this statement and note its key terms.
The key terms are 'علم واحد قديم' and 'تعلق'.
3
Understand the analogy with existence
Al-Ash'ari compares the relation of eternal knowledge to temporal things to the relation of God's eternal existence to temporal existents. Just as God's existence does not change with changing times, so His knowledge does not change with changing known objects.
This analogy shows how an eternal attribute can relate to temporal things without change.
4
Grasp the nature of knowledge
Al-Ash'ari explains that knowledge follows the known object as it is, without acquiring or imparting a property. The multiplicity of known things does not affect the unity of knowledge; differences are in the objects, not in the relation of knowledge.
This explains why God's knowledge remains one despite diverse objects.
5
Resolve the final objection
Al-Ash'ari addresses the claim that God would know existence and non-existence simultaneously. He clarifies that God knows each at its proper time, and the knowledge that something will be is the same as knowledge of its existence when it occurs, implying prior knowledge of non-existence.
This preserves divine immutability while accounting for temporal events.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمتكلمين في جواب شبهة هشام وجهم طرق وللفلاسفة طرق أيضا، قال الشيخ أبو الحسن الأشعري رضي الله عنه على طريقته : لا يتجدد الله تعالى حكم، ولا يتعاقب عليه حال، ولا تتجدد له صفة، بل هو تعالى متصف بعلم واحد قديم متعلق بما لم يزل ولا يزال، وهو محيط بجميع المعلومات على تفاصيلها من غير تجدد وجه العلم، أو تجدد تعلق أو تجدد حال له لقدمه والقدم لا يتغير ولا يتجدد له حال وإضافة العلم الأزلي إلى الكائنات فيما لا يزال كإضافة الوجود الأزلي إلى الكائنات الحاصلة في الأوقات المختلفة، وكما لا تغير ذاته بتغير الأزمنة لا يتغير علمه بتجدد المعلومات، فإن العلم من حقيقته أن يتبع المعلوم على ما هو به من غير أن يكتسب منه صفة ولا يكسبه صفة والمعلومات وإن اختلفت وتعددت فقد تشاركت في كونها معلومة ولم يكن اختلافها لتعلق العلم بها، بل اختلافها لأنفسها وكونها معلومة ليس إلا تعلق العلم بها، وذلك لا يختلف، وكذلك تعلقات جميع الصفات الأزلية فلا نقول : يتجدد عليها حال بتجدد حال المتعلق، فلا نقول : الله تعالى يعلم العدم والوجود معا في وقت واحد، فإن ذلك محال، بل يعلم العدم في وقت العدم، ويعلم الوجود في وقت الوجود، والعلم بأن سيكون هو بعينه علم بالكون في وقت الكون، إلا أن من ضرورة العلم بالوجود في وقت الوجود العلم بالعدم قبل الوجود، ويعبر عنه بأنه علم بأن سيكون.
And for the theologians there are methods in answering the objection of Hisham and Jahm, and for the philosophers there are also methods. Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, may God be pleased with him, said according to his way: No judgment is renewed for God, no state succeeds upon Him, and no attribute is renewed for Him. Rather, He is described with one eternal knowledge related to what has never ceased and will never cease, and it encompasses all known things in their details without renewal of the aspect of knowledge, or renewal of relation, or renewal of state for Him, due to His pre-eternity. Pre-eternity does not change, and no state is renewed for it. The relation of eternal knowledge to existent things in what will not cease is like the relation of eternal existence to existent things occurring in different times. Just as His essence does not change with the change of times, His knowledge does not change with the renewal of known things. For knowledge, from its reality, is to follow the known as it is, without acquiring a description from it nor giving it a description. Even if known things differ and multiply, they share in being known. Their difference is not due to the relation of knowledge to them, but rather their difference is due to themselves. Their being known is nothing but the relation of knowledge to them, and that does not differ. Likewise the relations of all eternal attributes — we do not say that a state is renewed for them with the renewal of the state of the related. So we do not say: God knows non-existence and existence together at one time, for that is impossible. Rather, He knows non-existence at the time of non-existence, and He knows existence at the time of existence. The knowledge that something will be is exactly the knowledge of its existence at the time of existence, except that it is a necessary consequence of knowledge of existence at the time of existence to have knowledge of non-existence before existence, and it is expressed as knowledge that it will be.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari response to the objection of Hisham and Jahm (who argued that God's knowledge changes). Al-Ash'ari states that God does not undergo any renewal of judgment, state, or attribute. Instead, God has one eternal knowledge that encompasses all things without change. He uses the analogy of God's eternal existence: just as God's existence is one and relates to things that exist at different times without change, so too His knowledge is one and relates to things as they occur. The nature of knowledge is to follow the known object, not to acquire or impart a property. The multiplicity of known things does not affect the unity of knowledge; their differences are in themselves, not in the relation of knowledge. Therefore, we do not say that God knows existence and non-existence simultaneously, but He knows each at its proper time. The knowledge that something will be is the same knowledge as when it exists, but it implies a prior knowledge of non-existence. This preserves God's immutability while accounting for temporal objects.

Key Terms
علم واحد قديم
تعلق
وجه العلم
قدم
المعلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge changes as events occur.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari teaches that God's knowledge is one eternal attribute that relates to events without change. The relation appears to vary with the object, but the attribute itself remains unchanged.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, how does God's knowledge relate to temporal events without changing?
A) God's knowledge changes as events occur.
B) God knows all events at once in a single eternal act.
C) God's knowledge is one and relates to each event when it occurs, but the relation does not change God.
D) God has multiple knowledges for different times.

Key Takeaway
God's one eternal knowledge relates to all temporal events without change, preserving divine immutability while accounting for temporal objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge of your every sin, past and future, is eternally present to Him, does your repentance today carry the weight of eternity? What will you change about how you approach Him knowing He has always seen it all?
?
You live as though your choices surprise God, but this passage says His knowledge follows the known as it is—without change. When you plan a sin, whose will are you trying to override? Will you let this truth crush your arrogance today?
?
If God's knowledge of your existence before you were born is the same as His knowledge of you now, what does that say about the significance of your current moment? Does your anxiety about tomorrow accuse God of ignorance? How will you trust His eternal knowledge with your next hour?

Ash'ari on God's Unchanging Eternal Knowledge
Ash'ari answers the objection about God knowing changing things without change in Himself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari defends God's unchanging eternal knowledge against objections that it must change with changing known things.

Core Idea
God's eternal knowledge encompasses all things without change, just as His existence is unchanging through time.

The objection that God's knowledge would have to change as the world changes is a classic challenge. In this passage, Ash'ari presents his method: a single eternal knowledge that relates to all things at their proper times without any change in God. Let's examine his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense style of theological argumentation.
2
Identify the key principle
The passage begins by establishing that the Creator is not in a location and knowledge is not in a location. Ask yourself why this is relevant to the argument for unchanging knowledge.
This principle lays the groundwork for rejecting any change in God.
3
Understand Ash'ari's claim
Ash'ari states that no new ruling, state, or attribute occurs in God. He is described by one eternal knowledge related to all things without any new aspect or relation. Compare this to the objection's assumption.
4
Reflect on the analogy
The passage compares eternal knowledge's relation to future beings to eternal existence's relation to beings at different times. Consider how this analogy clarifies that God's knowledge does not change.
Just as God's existence is unchanging, so is His knowledge.
5
Note the clarification about knowledge's nature
The text explains that knowledge follows the known as it is without acquiring or imparting qualities. Differences among known things are in themselves, not in the relation of knowledge. This seals the argument.
This prevents the misconception that knowledge changes with different known objects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصار علمًا به ولأن الباري تعالى لا في محل والعلم لا في محل، فاختصاص ما لا محل له بما لا محل له أولى من اختصاص بما له مكان ومحل. وللمتكلمين في جواب شبهة هشام وجهم طرق وللفلاسفة طرق أيضا، قال الشيخ أبو الحسن الأشعري رضي الله عنه على طريقته : لا يتجدد الله تعالى حكم، ولا يتعاقب عليه حال، ولا تتجدد له صفة، بل هو تعالى متصف بعلم واحد قديم متعلق بما لم يزل ولا يزال، وهو محيط بجميع المعلومات على تفاصيلها من غير تجدد وجه العلم، أو تجدد تعلق أو تجدد حال له لقدمه والقدم لا يتغير ولا يتجدد له حال وإضافة العلم الأزلي إلى الكائنات فيما لا يزال كإضافة الوجود الأزلي إلى الكائنات الحاصلة في الأوقات المختلفة، وكما لا تغير ذاته بتغير الأزمنة لا يتغير علمه بتجدد المعلومات، فإن العلم من حقيقته أن يتبع المعلوم على ما هو به من غير أن يكتسب منه صفة ولا يكسبه صفة والمعلومات وإن اختلفت وتعددت فقد تشاركت في كونها معلومة ولم يكن اختلافها لتعلق العلم بها، بل اختلافها لأنفسها وكونها معلومة ليس إلا تعلق العلم بها، وذلك لا يختلف، وكذلك تعلقات جميع الصفات الأزلية فلا نقول : يتجدد عليها حال بتجدد حال المتعلق، فلا نقول : الله تعالى يعلم العدم والوجود معًا في وقت واحد، فإن ذلك محال، بل يعلم العدم في وقت العدم، ويعلم الوجود في وقت الوجود، والعلم بأن سيكون هو بعينه علم بالكون في وقت الكون، إلا أن من ضرورة العلم بالوجود في وقت الوجود العلم بالعدم قبل الوجود، ويعبر عنه بأنه علم بأن سيكون.
Thus it became known by it, and because the Exalted Creator is not in a location and knowledge is not in a location, so the specification of what has no location to what has no location is more appropriate than specification to what has a place and a location. The theologians have methods in answering the doubt of Hisham and Jahm, and the philosophers also have methods. Sheikh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, may God be pleased with him, said according to his method: No ruling becomes new for God Exalted, nor does a state befall Him successively, nor does an attribute become new for Him. Rather, He Exalted is described with one eternal knowledge, related to what has not ceased and will not cease, and it encompasses all known things in their details without a new aspect of knowledge, or a new relation, or a new state for Him due to His eternity, and eternity does not change nor does a state become new for Him. The relation of eternal knowledge to beings in the future is like the relation of eternal existence to beings occurring at different times. And just as His essence does not change with the change of times, His knowledge does not change with the renewal of known things. For knowledge, by its reality, is to follow the known as it is, without acquiring a quality from it nor giving it a quality. The known things, even if they differ and multiply, share in being known, and their difference is not due to the relation of knowledge to them, but their difference is in themselves, and their being known is nothing but the relation of knowledge to them, and that does not differ. Similarly, the relations of all eternal attributes: we do not say that a state becomes new for them due to the renewal of the state of the related object. So we do not say: God Exalted knows non-existence and existence together at one time, for that is impossible. Rather, He knows non-existence at the time of non-existence, and knows existence at the time of existence. And the knowledge that it will be is exactly the same as knowledge of its existence at the time of existence, except that it is necessary for the knowledge of existence at the time of existence to have knowledge of non-existence before existence, and it is expressed as knowledge that it will be.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the Ash'ari position on God's knowledge against the objections of Hisham and Jahm. It begins by establishing an important principle: since the Creator (al-Bari) is not in a location, and knowledge is not in a location, it is more fitting that what has no location be specified by what has no location. This sets the stage for rejecting any change in God's attributes. The text then presents Ash'ari's method: God does not acquire new rulings, states, or attributes. Instead, He is described by a single eternal knowledge that encompasses all things, past and future, without any new relation or state arising from the renewal of known objects. The key analogy is that eternal knowledge's relation to future beings is like eternal existence's relation to beings occurring in different times: just as God's existence does not change with time, so His knowledge does not change. The text emphasizes that knowledge follows the known as it is; it does not acquire a quality from the known nor does it impart a quality to it. Differences among known things are in themselves, not in knowledge's relation. Consequently, we cannot say God knows existence and non-existence simultaneously, but rather He knows each at its proper time. The knowledge that something will be is identical to the knowledge of its occurrence at the time of occurrence, with the necessary implication that knowledge of existence after non-existence presupposes knowledge of prior non-existence. This is expressed as 'knowledge that it will be'.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God knows all things eternally, He must know them as present simultaneously.
Correction: Ash'ari clarifies that God knows each thing at its own time, e.g., non-existence at the time of non-existence and existence at the time of existence, and the knowledge does not change.

C) By forgetting the event temporarily and then recalling it.
D) By knowing only the present, not the future.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is eternal and unchanging; it relates to each thing at its appropriate time without any change in God.

Eternal Knowledge and the Zayd Example
Sanusi clarifies eternal knowledge with Zayd's example and refutes renewed knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's eternal knowledge does not require renewal, as demonstrated by the Zayd example, and why the human experience of change does not apply to Him.

Core Idea
Eternal knowledge suffices; renewal implies imperfection.

This lesson explores a powerful analogy used by theologians to clarify the nature of God's knowledge. By reflecting on how human knowledge works in a simple scenario—knowing that Zayd will arrive tomorrow—we can better understand why God's knowledge does not need to be renewed with each event. The passage also addresses a common objection and refutes the possibility of temporally renewed knowledge in the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Zayd analogy carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the scenario: a person knows Zayd will arrive tomorrow, that knowledge persists, and then Zayd arrives. What happens?
The key point is that no new knowledge is needed because the prior knowledge suffices.
2
Understand the objection about distinction
The text presents an objection: humans feel a difference between before and after an event, which is attributed to renewed knowledge. Consider why this objection fails for God.
The response is that the distinction arises from human limitations (sensations), not from divine reality.
3
Grasp the refutation of renewed knowledge
Analyze the final part where the text argues that if renewed knowledge occurs a moment before an event, it is still knowledge of the future, just like eternal knowledge. This leads to the conclusion that renewed knowledge would be eternal, which contradicts its temporary nature.
This refutes the possibility of temporally renewed knowledge in God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يوضح الحق في ذلك هو أنا لو علمنا قدوم زيد غيره، وقدرنا بقاء هذا العلم على مذهب من يعتقد جواز البناء عليه، ثم قدم زيد ولم يحدث له علم بقدومه لم يفتقر إلى علم آخر بقدومه إذا سبق له العلم بقدومه في الوقت المعين، وقد حصل ما علم وعلم ما حصل ; إذ لو قدرنا أنه لم يتجدد له علم ولم تتجدد له غفلة وجهل استحال أن يقال : لم يعلم قدومه، بل يجب أن يقال : تنجز ما كان متوقعا، وتحقق ما كان مقدرًا، وحصل ما كان معلوما. وقولهم : إن الإنسان يجد تفرقة بين حالتيه قبل قدومه وحال قدومه، فتلك التفرقة ترجع إلى تجدد العلم فليس ذلك على الإطلاق بل يرجع في حق المخلوقين إلى إحساس وإدراك لم يكن فكان وفي حق الخالق لا تفرقة بين المقدر والمحقق والمنجز والمتوقع، بل المعلومات بالنسبة إلى علمه تعالى على وتيرة واحدة، وقال : هذا العلم المتجدد يحصل عندكم قبل الوجود المتجدد بلحظة، فإذا تقدمه بلحظة كان أيضًا علمًا بما سيكون لا علمًا بالكائن، فهو والعلم الأزلي بما سيكون سواء، وإذا جاز تقدمه جاز قدمه.
And what clarifies the truth in that is that if we knew Zayd's arrival tomorrow, and we supposed the continuation of this knowledge according to the doctrine of one who believes in the permissibility of building upon it, then Zayd arrived and no new knowledge of his arrival occurred for him, he would not need another knowledge of his arrival because he had prior knowledge of his arrival at the specified time, and what he knew occurred and he knew what occurred. For if we supposed that no new knowledge occurred for him and no heedlessness nor ignorance occurred for him, it would be impossible to say that he did not know his arrival. Rather, it must be said: what was expected came to pass, what was estimated was realized, and what was known occurred. And their statement: 'Man finds a distinction between his two states—before his arrival and at the state of his arrival'—that distinction goes back to renewed knowledge. That is not absolute, but rather applies to creatures: it goes back to a sensation and perception that was not and then came to be. And in the case of the Creator, there is no distinction between the estimated, the realized, the accomplished, and the expected. Rather, the known things are at one uniform level with respect to His knowledge, Exalted. And he said: This renewed knowledge occurs, according to you, before the renewed existence by a moment. If it precedes it by a moment, it is also knowledge of what will be, not knowledge of the existent. So it and the eternal knowledge of what will be are the same. And if its precedence is permissible, then its eternity is permissible.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage provides a clarification and a refutation of the claim that God needs renewed knowledge for events. It begins with an analogy: if a human knows Zayd's arrival tomorrow and this knowledge persists, when Zayd actually arrives, the person does not need a new piece of knowledge—the prior knowledge suffices. If no new knowledge arises and no heedlessness or ignorance occurs, it is impossible to say he did not know the arrival. This demonstrates that eternal knowledge can cover future events without renewal. The text then addresses the objection that a human feels a distinction between the state before and after an event, which is attributed to renewed knowledge. The answer is that this distinction applies only to creatures, who experience new sensations and perceptions. For the Creator, there is no distinction; all known things are uniform to His knowledge. Finally, the passage refutes the idea of temporally renewed knowledge in the Creator by analyzing its hypothetical nature: if a renewed knowledge occurs a moment before an event, it is still knowledge of what will be, not of the present existent, so it is functionally identical to eternal knowledge. If such precedence is allowed, then there is no barrier to it being eternal—hence the claim that renewed knowledge is possible leads to the conclusion that it could have always existed, undermining the opponent's position.

Correction: The passage clarifies that the distinction humans feel is due to new sensations and perceptions, which are not applicable to God. God's knowledge is uniform and does not change.

Checkpoint
What does the Zayd example illustrate about God's knowledge of future events?
A) That God must acquire new knowledge at the time of the event.
B) That persistent prior knowledge can suffice without needing renewal.
C) That God forgets future events and later remembers them.
D) That human knowledge is identical to God's knowledge.

Key Takeaway
God's eternal knowledge encompasses all events without needing renewal, and the human experience of change does not apply to Him.

?
Sanusi says that for creatures, the feeling of new knowledge is due to sensation—you perceive something new with your senses. Have you ever mistaken your sensory experience of change for a change in Allah's knowledge? How can you remind yourself that your limited perception does not reflect His timeless nature?
?

God's Knowledge Does Not Change with Events
Argues that God's knowledge does not change with events, refuting the objection from experiential distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's knowledge does not change with the occurrence of events, and how the objection from human experiential distinction is refuted.

Core Idea
God's eternal knowledge suffices; no new knowledge arises with events.

This passage presents a key argument in Islamic theology that God's knowledge is eternal and unchanging. The author uses a simple example of knowing a future event to show that prior knowledge is sufficient. He then anticipates and refutes an objection based on human experience of anticipation and realization, clarifying that for God there is no sensory change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice the author's use of a concrete example (Zayd's arrival) to illustrate a theological point.
2
Understand the example of Zayd's arrival
Focus on the example: if someone has prior knowledge of Zayd's arrival tomorrow, when Zayd actually arrives, no new knowledge is needed. The prior knowledge suffices.
This shows that knowledge of a future event does not become different when the event occurs.
3
Analyze the objection and response
The author addresses the objection that humans feel a distinction between anticipation and realization. He responds that for creatures this distinction comes from sensory change, but for God there is no sensory change; all known things are equally known.
The key is that God is not subject to sensory perception.
4
Reflect on the further argument
The author adds that even if temporal knowledge were to precede an event by a moment, it would still be knowledge of what will be, not what is, so it is no different from eternal knowledge. This reinforces the conclusion that God's knowledge does not need to change.
This argument shows that temporal knowledge is essentially the same as eternal knowledge in terms of content.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يوضح الحق في ذلك هو أنا لو علمنا قدوم زيد غدا وقدرنا بقاء هذا العلم على مذهب من يعتقد جواز البناء عليه، ثم قدم زيد ولم يحدث له علم بقدومه لم يفتقر إلى علم آخر بقدومه إذا سبق له العلم بقدومه في الوقت المعين، وقد حصل ما علم وعلم ما حصل؛ إذ لو قدرنا أنه لم يتجدد له علم ولم تتجدد له غفلة وجهل استحال أن يقال: لم يعلم قدومه، بل يجب أن يقال: تنجز ما كان متوقعا، وتحقق ما كان مقدرًا، وحصل ما كان معلوما. وقولهم: إن الإنسان يجد تفرقة بين حالتيه قبل قدومه وحال قدومه، فتلك التفرقة ترجع إلى تجدد العلم فليس ذلك على الإطلاق بل يرجع في حق المخلوقين إلى إحساس وإدراك لم يكن فكان وفي حق الخالق لا تفرقة بين المقدر والمحقق والمنجز والمتوقع، بل المعلومات بالنسبة إلى علمه تعالى على وتيرة واحدة، وقال: هذا العلم المتجدد يحصل عندكم قبل الوجود المتجدد بلحظة، فإذا تقدمه بلحظة كان أيضًا علمًا بما سيكون لا علمًا بالكائن، فهو والعلم الأزلي بما سيكون سواء، وإذا جاز تقدمه جاز قدمه.
And what clarifies the truth in that is that if we knew Zayd's arrival tomorrow, and we assumed the permanence of this knowledge according to the school of one who believes in the permissibility of building upon it, then Zayd arrived and no knowledge of his arrival occurred for him, he would not need another knowledge of his arrival, since he had prior knowledge of his arrival at the specified time; and what was known occurred, and what occurred was known. For if we assumed that no new knowledge occurred for him and no forgetfulness and ignorance occurred, it would be impossible to say: he did not know his arrival; rather it must be said: what was anticipated came to pass, what was estimated was realized, and what was known occurred. And their statement: man finds a distinction between his two states before his arrival and at the moment of his arrival, that distinction reverts to the occurrence of new knowledge. That is not absolute, but in the case of creatures it reverts to a sensation and perception that was not then came to be, and in the case of the Creator there is no distinction between the estimated and the realized, the actualized and the anticipated; rather, relative to His knowledge, all known things are on one uniform level. And he said: this occurring knowledge happens according to you before the occurring existence by a moment, so if it precedes it by a moment, it is also knowledge of what will be, not knowledge of what is; so it and eternal knowledge of what will be are the same, and if its preceding is permissible, its eternity is permissible.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation. The author presents an argument that God's knowledge does not temporally change when future events occur. He uses the example of knowing Zayd's arrival tomorrow: if one has certain knowledge of the arrival, when the event occurs no new knowledge is needed; the prior knowledge is sufficient. This shows that for God, who has eternal knowledge, there is no need for temporal knowledge. The author then addresses an objection: humans experience a distinction between anticipation and realization, which seems to require new knowledge for God. The author responds that for creatures this distinction comes from sensory change, but for God there is no sensory change; all objects of knowledge are equally known eternally. Moreover, if temporal knowledge were to precede the event by a moment, it would still be knowledge of what will be, not what is, so it is no different from eternal knowledge. Thus, if such temporal knowledge is allowed, eternal knowledge is equally allowed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge of future events becomes actual only when the event occurs.
Correction: God's eternal knowledge is complete and does not become actual or change; prior knowledge of a future event is sufficient and does not require renewal.

Checkpoint

B) They do not need a new knowledge because the prior knowledge suffices.
C) They forget the prior knowledge.
D) The prior knowledge becomes invalid.

Key Takeaway
God's eternal knowledge is complete and does not change when events occur; any temporal knowledge would be redundant and equivalent to eternal knowledge.

?
When you anticipate a good outcome or fear a bad one, do you act as if God's knowledge of the outcome is incomplete until it happens? How can you live in a way that reflects your belief that all things are equally known to God from eternity?

Mu'tazila on Divine Knowledge and Abu al-Husayn's Position
Presents the Mu'tazila view and internal disagreement on divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila doctrine that God knows by His essence, their disagreement on whether the difference between anticipation and realization lies in the attachment or the object, and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's partial acceptance of Hisham's view on the renewal of states.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argued God knows all by His essence, but differed on how that knowledge relates to future vs. present.

This passage presents the Mu'tazila doctrine of divine knowledge and a notable internal development. The Mu'tazila assert that God knows eternally by His essence, and the relation to future events is like that to present ones. However, they disagreed on whether the difference between anticipation and realization lies in the relation (tacalluq) or the object. Then Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, a later Mu'tazili, partially adopted Hisham's view that God's states are renewed with contingent things, despite rejecting attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation to get an overall sense of the argument and the different positions mentioned.
Notice the technical terms like تقدير الوجود and تحقيق الوجود.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila position on divine knowledge
The Mu'tazila hold that God knows by His essence eternally, and the relation of His knowledge to future events is the same as to present events. They also allow one knowledge to attach to multiple knowns.
This is a foundational point: God's knowledge does not change.
3
Identify the three views on the difference between anticipation and realization
Some Mu'tazila say the difference lies in the attachment (relation), al-Ash'ari says it lies in the object, and others say it lies in two distinct states. Identify each view in the text.
This shows internal disagreement within the Mu'tazila and between schools.
4
Examine Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's position
He inclined to Hisham's view that God's states are renewed with contingent things, even though he was a denier of states. He considered the aspects of attachments as additional states.
Note the irony: he denies states yet posits additional states.
5
Reflect on the significance of this passage
This passage illustrates the diversity of opinion within the Mu'tazila and the influence of philosophical methods on later theologians like Abu al-Husayn.
It also shows how later Mu'tazila moved away from their predecessors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت المعتزلة على طريقتهم: الباري تعالى عالم لذاته أزلا بما سيكون، ونسبة ذاته أو وجه عالميته إلى المعلوم الذي سيكون كنسبته إلى المعلوم الكائن الموجود، والعالم منا بما سيكون عالم على تقدير الوجود، وبما هو كائن عالم على تحقيق الوجود، فالمعلومات بعلم واحد جائز تقديرًا أو تحقيقا، وعندهم يجوز تقدير بقاء العلم ويجوز تعلق العلم الواحد بمعلومين ولا استحالة فيه شاهدًا وغائبًا، ثم إن بعضهم يقول: يرجع الاختلاف في الحالتين إلى التعلق لا إلى المتعلق، بخلاف ما قال الأشعري: إن الاختلاف يرجع إلى المتعلق لا المتعلق والتعلق، وقال بعضهم يرجع الاختلاف في الحالتين إلى حالتين. وقد مال أبو الحسين البصري إلى مذهب هشام بعض الميل حتى قضى بتجدد أحوال الباري تعالى عند تجدد الكائنات مع أنه من نفاة الأحوال، غير أنه جعل وجوه التعلقات أحوالاً إضافية للذات العالمية، وهو في جميع مقالاته ينهج مناهج الفلاسفة، ويرد على شيوخه من المعتزلة بتصفح أدلتهم ردا شنيعا.
And the Mu'tazila said according to their method: The Creator, exalted, is knowing by His essence eternally of what will be, and the relation of His essence or aspect of His omniscience to the known that will be is like its relation to the known that is existing. And the knower among us of what will be is a knower on the estimation of existence, and of what is, a knower on the realization of existence. So known things by one knowledge are permissible by estimation or realization. And according to them, estimation of the permanence of knowledge is permissible, and the attachment of one knowledge to two knowns is permissible, and there is no impossibility in it by witness or by unseen. Then some of them say: the difference in the two states reverts to the attachment, not to the attached, contrary to what al-Ash'ari said: that the difference reverts to the attached, not to the attachment and the attached. And some of them said: the difference in the two states reverts to two states. And Abu al-Husayn al-Basri inclined somewhat to the school of Hisham until he ruled for the renewal of states of the Creator, exalted, at the renewal of contingent things, even though he is among the deniers of states, except that he made the aspects of attachments as additional states for the omniscient essence. And in all his articles he follows the methods of the philosophers, and he harshly refutes his shaykhs among the Mu'tazila by examining their proofs.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila position on divine knowledge and then discusses an offshoot by Abu al-Husayn al-Basri. The Mu'tazila hold that God knows by His essence eternally, and the relation of His knowledge to future events is the same as to present events. Human knowledge of future events is on estimation, while of present events on realization. They allow one knowledge to relate to multiple knowns. Some Mu'tazila say the difference between anticipation and realization lies in the attachment (relation) not the object, unlike al-Ash'ari who says it lies in the object. Others say it lies in two states. Then the author notes that Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, despite being a denier of attributes (states), inclined to Hisham's view and accepted that God's states renew with contingent things, making the aspects of attachments as additional states. He follows philosophical methods and severely refutes his Mu'tazila predecessors.

The Mu'tazila held that God knows by His essence, but disagreed on whether the difference between anticipation and realization lies in the attachment or the object, and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri introduced a novel view on the renewal of states.

What is the state of your heart when you remember that nothing is hidden from Him?

Refuting the 'Will is Not Willed' Excuse
Refutation of the claim that will is not willed to avoid infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument against originated wills not in a locus is refuted by pointing out the inconsistency with the principle that knowledge is known, and how this silences the Karramiyya regarding the locus of accidents.

Core Idea
A clever excuse fails when compared to knowledge.

This passage presents a key theological refutation against those who posit that God's will is originated (hadith) and not in a locus. They try to avoid the problem of infinite regress (tasalsul) by claiming that will itself is not willed. However, the author shows that this excuse is inconsistent with the principle of earlier theologians, because if will were not willed, then knowledge would also not be known, leading to a contradiction. This argument effectively silences the Karramiyya on the issue of the locus of accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Note the flow of the argument.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the opponents' claim and their excuse
The opponents are those who posit originated wills not in a locus. They claim that will is not willed (al-irada la turad) to avoid infinite regress.
3
Understand the refutation
The author refutes this by comparing will to knowledge. Since knowledge is known (ma'lum), if the same reasoning applied, knowledge would also lead to infinite regress. Therefore, the excuse is invalid.
The refutation relies on the principle of Hisham and Jahm.
4
Recognize the broader implication
The conclusion is that this objection silences the Karramiyya on the issue of the locus of accidents (mahall al-hawadith).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكانت محتاجة إلى علوم أخر، والكلام في تلك العلوم كالكلام في هذه، ويؤدي إلى التسلسل، وقد ألزم أصحاب الإرادات الحادثة لا في محل هذا الإلزام واعتذروا بأن قالوا: الإرادة لا تراد وهذا العذر لا يصح على قاعدة هشام وجهم، فإن الإرادة وإن كانت لا تراد فهي بخلاف العلم؛ لأن العلم يعلم فيلزم القول بالتسلسل، وهذا الإلزام قد أفحم الكرامية في مسألة محل الحوادث.
It was in need of other sciences, and the discussion about those sciences is like the discussion about these, leading to infinite regress. And he forced the proponents of originated wills not in a locus with this objection, and they excused themselves by saying: will is not willed. But this excuse is not valid according to the principle of Hisham and Jahm, for will, even if it is not willed, is unlike knowledge, because knowledge is known, so the statement of infinite regress would follow. And this objection has silenced the Karramiyya concerning the issue of the locus of accidents.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of those who posit originated wills not in a locus (al-iradat al-haditha la fi mahall), likely a Mu'tazili or other sect. The opponent argues that if one science depends on another, this leads to infinite regress (tasalsul). The defenders of originated wills try to avoid this by claiming that will is not willed (i.e., it does not require a prior will). However, the author counters that this excuse is invalid according to the principle of Hisham ibn al-Hakam and Jahm ibn Safwan, because will is unlike knowledge: knowledge is known (i.e., it is an object of knowledge). If the same reasoning were applied to knowledge, it would also lead to infinite regress. Thus, the objection effectively refutes the Karramiyya regarding the locus of accidents (mahall al-hawadith). The argument follows the order: a claim by opponents, their excuse, the refutation using a distinction between will and knowledge, and the conclusion that the objection silences the Karramiyya.

Key Terms
الإرادة
التسلسل
محل الحوادث
الكرامية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the claim 'will is not willed' successfully avoids infinite regress.
Correction: The author shows it fails because knowledge is known, which would also require infinite regress if the same logic applied.

Checkpoint
What is the excuse offered by the proponents of originated wills not in a locus to avoid infinite regress?
A) Will is not willed.
B) Knowledge is known.
C) The locus of accidents is eternal.
D) Infinite regress is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The assertion that 'will is not willed' fails to halt infinite regress because knowledge is known, leading to a contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used a clever excuse to avoid a difficult truth, only to find it doesn't hold under scrutiny? What truth are you avoiding right now that you know you must face?
?
The opponents here used an excuse that seemed logical but failed when compared to knowledge. What excuses do you make to avoid submitting to Allah's knowledge of your inner state—the thoughts, intentions, and habits you prefer to hide?
?
This passage shows that even sophisticated arguments cannot hide from the truth. Are you willing to let go of your own rationalizations and accept what Allah has revealed, even if it means admitting you were wrong?

Mu'tazila on God's Knowledge and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's Position
Mu'tazila doctrine on God's knowledge and al-Basri's leaning to Hisham.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila doctrine on God's knowledge of the future and present, and how Abu al-Husayn al-Basri diverged from his school's orthodoxy.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila debate God's knowledge as a single relation to all knowns.

The Mu'tazila held that God knows all things eternally through a single knowledge. This passage explores their nuanced discussion of how that single knowledge relates to both future and present events, and introduces Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's unorthodox view that God's 'states' renew with creation, despite rejecting states altogether. Understanding these positions clarifies the fault lines within early kalam about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic text and translation
Read the passage first, then the translation. Note the key terms like 'ta'alluq' (relation) and 'muta'allaq' (related).
2
Identify the main positions
The passage presents the Mu'tazila view, then a debate on where the difference lies (relation vs. related), and finally al-Basri's stance.
3
Compare with al-Ash'ari
Notice that al-Ash'ari is mentioned as holding a different view: the difference is in the related, not the relation.
4
Understand al-Basri's innovation
Al-Basri, though a Mu'tazili, adopted some of Hisham's ideas about renewing states, which contradicts his nominal rejection of states. He redefined relations as 'additional states'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت المعتزلة على طريقتهم: الباري تعالى عالم لذاته أزلا بما سيكون، ونسبة ذاته أو وجه عالميته إلى المعلوم الذي سيكون كنسبته إلى المعلوم الكائن الموجود، والعالم منا بما سيكون عالم على تقدير الوجود، وبما هو كائن عالم على تحقيق الوجود، فالمعلومات بعلم واحد جائز تقديرًا أو تحقيقا، وعندهم يجوز تقدير بقاء العلم ويجوز تعلق العلم الواحد بمعلومين ولا استحالة فيه شاهدًا وغائبًا، ثم إن بعضهم يقول: يرجع الاختلاف في الحالتين إلى التعلق لا إلى المتعلق، بخلاف ما قال الأشعري: إن الاختلاف يرجع إلى المتعلق لا المتعلق والتعلق، وقال بعضهم يرجع الاختلاف في الحالتين إلى حالتين. وقد مال أبو الحسين البصري إلى مذهب هشام بعض الميل حتى قضى بتجدد أحوال الباري تعالى عند تجدد الكائنات مع أنه من نفاة الأحوال، غير أنه جعل وجوه التعلقات أحوالاً إضافية للذات العالمية، وهو في جميع مقالاته ينهج مناهج الفلاسفة، ويرد على شيوخه من المعتزلة بتصفح أدلتهم ردا شنيعا.
And the Mu'tazila said according to their method: the Exalted Creator is knower by His essence eternally of what will be, and the relation of His essence or aspect of His knowledge to the known that will be is like its relation to the known that is and exists. And the knower among us of what will be is a knower on the assumption of existence, and of what is is a knower on the realization of existence. So the known things are with a single knowledge, permissible by estimation or realization. And according to them, it is permissible to suppose the remaining of knowledge, and it is permissible for a single knowledge to be related to two known things, and there is no impossibility in that, in the witnessed and the unseen. Then some of them say: the difference in the two states relates to the relation, not to the related. Contrary to what al-Ash'ari said: the difference relates to the related, not to the relation and the related. And some say: the difference in the two states relates to two states. And Abu al-Husayn al-Basri inclined somewhat to the doctrine of Hisham until he judged that the states of the Exalted Creator are renewed upon the renewal of created things, even though he is among the negators of states. However, he made the aspects of the relations additional states for the knowing essence. And in all his articles, he follows the methods of the philosophers, and he refutes his teachers among the Mu'tazila by scrutinizing their proofs, a severe refutation.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila doctrine on God's knowledge. They affirm that God is knower by His essence eternally of future events, and His knowledge of future and present is the same, differing only in the mode of existence (estimation vs. realization). According to them, a single knowledge can relate to multiple knowns, and they debate whether the difference between knowledge of the future and the present lies in the relation (ta'alluq) or the related (muta'allaq). Al-Ash'ari held the difference is in the related, while a Mu'tazili faction says it is in the relation. Then the text discusses Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, who, despite being a negator of states (ahwal), leaned towards Hisham ibn al-Hakam's doctrine that God's states are renewed with creation. He considered the aspects of relations as additional states for the knowing essence, showing the influence of philosophy. The passage ends with a note on his harsh refutation of his Mu'tazili teachers.

Misconception: Al-Ash'ari and some Mu'tazila agree on the difference between knowledge of future and present.
Correction: Actually, al-Ash'ari says the difference is in the related (muta'allaq), while some Mu'tazila say it is in the relation (ta'alluq). They disagree.

Key Takeaway
