Philosophers on Divine Knowledge: Universal and Unchanging
Philosophers argue God knows all from His essence universally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that God knows all things universally and unchangingly from His own essence, without change or passivity.

Core Idea
God knows all things from His essence, not from external things, to preserve His immutability.

The philosophers (Falasifa) present a rigorous argument about divine knowledge. They assert that if God knew things from external objects, His essence would be compromised—either constituted by or accidentally affected by what He knows. Instead, they propose that God knows all things from His own essence as the principle of existence, in a universal and active manner, without any change. This passage lays the foundation for their understanding of God's omniscience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical flow: negating one possibility, then affirming another.
2
Identify the philosophical problem
The passage begins by stating what is not allowed: God knowing things from external things. Ask why that would be problematic. The text gives two reasons: His essence would be constituted by (muttaqima) knowledge, or knowledge would be accidental to Him ('arid). Both are deemed impossible.
This sets up the need for an alternative explanation.
3
Understand the alternative: self-knowledge as source
The passage then presents the positive view: God knows from His own essence. Since He is the principle (mabda') of all existents, He knows complete existents in their individuals and corruptible ones by species and individuals. This avoids external dependency.
Note the distinction between types of existents.
4
Grasp the argument against changing knowledge
The text argues that if God knew changing things as they change, His knowledge would change, implying a change in His essence. Each state would require a separate mental form, and they cannot coexist, so His essence would be mutable. This is impossible for the Necessary Existent.
This is the core of the immutability argument.
5
Explore the nature of divine knowledge: universal and active
The passage concludes that God knows everything in a universal, active (fi'li) manner, not passive (infi'ali). It cites the Quranic verse that nothing escapes Him. Then it briefly mentions the mechanism: by knowing His essence as cause, He knows the entire chain of existence.
The word 'passive' indicates His knowledge does not receive from outside.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت الفلاسفة على طريقهم: واجب الوجود ليس يجوز أن يعقل الأشياء من الأشياء وإلا فذاته؛ إما متقومة بما يعقل، أو عارض لها أن يعقل وذلك محال، بل هو كما أنه مبدأ كل موجود فيعقل من ذاته ما هو مبدأ له مبدأ وهو للموجودات التامة بأعيانها، والموجودات الكائنة الفاسدة بأنواعها وأشخاصها، ولا يجوز أن يكون عاقلا لهذه التغيرات مع تغيرها حتى يكون تارة يعقل منها أنها موجودة غير معدومة وتارة يعلمها معدومة غير موجودة، ولكل واحد من الأمرين صورة عقلية على حدة، ولا واحد من الصورتين يبقى مع الثانية، فيكون واجب الوجود متغير الذات، بل هو إنما يعقل كل شيء على نحو كلي فعلي لا انفعالي، ولا يعزب عنه مثقال ذرة في السماوات ولا في الأرض [سبأ: ٢٣]. وأما كيفية ذلك فلأنه إذا عقل ذاته وعقل أنها مبدأ كل موجود عقل أوائل الموجودات وما يتولد منها، ولا شيء من الأشياء يوجد إلا وقد صار معلوما من جهة ما يكون واجبًا بسببه، فتكون الأسباب بمصادمات تتأدى إلى أن
And the philosophers said according to their method: the Necessary Existent is not allowed to know things from things, otherwise His essence would be either constituted by what He knows, or it would be accidental to Him to know, and that is impossible. Rather, just as He is the beginning of every existent, He knows from His essence what is His beginning, and He is the beginning for complete existents in their individuals, and for the generated and corruptible existents by their species and individuals. And it is not allowed that He be a knower of these changes with their change, such that sometimes He knows from them that they exist not non-existent, and sometimes He knows them as non-existent not existing. For each of the two states there is a separate mental form, and none of the two forms remains with the second, so the Necessary Existent would be of changing essence. Rather, He only knows everything in a universal, active, not passive manner. And not a particle's weight escapes Him in the heavens nor in the earth [Saba: 23]. As for the how of that: when He knows His essence and knows that it is the beginning of every existent, He knows the first of existents and what is generated from them. And nothing exists except that it becomes known from the aspect by which it is necessary due to Him. So the causes, with their collisions, lead to ...
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' (Falasifa) view on divine knowledge, likely influenced by Ibn Sina. They argue that the Necessary Existent (Wajib al-Wujud) cannot know things from external things because that would compromise the simplicity of His essence—making it either constituted by or accidentally affected by what He knows. Instead, He knows all things from His own essence, as He is the principle (mabda') of all existence. He knows complete existents (al-mawjudat al-tamma) in their individuals, and corruptible things by their species and individuals. If He knew things as they change, His knowledge would change, implying change in His essence, which is impossible. Hence, He knows everything in a universal, active (fi'li) manner, not passive (infi'ali). The Quranic verse confirms that nothing escapes Him. The mechanism is that by knowing His essence as the cause, He knows the entire chain of existents. The passage is incomplete, ending with 'so the causes, with their collisions, lead to ...' indicating a continuation about causal chains.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
عقل
صورة عقلية
كلي
فعلي
انفعالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's knowledge of changing things requires change in God's essence, making Him temporal.
Correction: The philosophers argue that God knows changing things in a universal, unchanging manner, so His essence remains immutable.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why is it impossible for the Necessary Existent to know things from external things?
A) Because He would need multiple knowledges.
B) Because His essence would become either constituted by or accidental to what He knows.
C) Because He knows only universals.
D) Because He is transcendent beyond creation.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers maintain that God knows all things from His own essence in a universal, active manner, without change or passivity, preserving His absolute simplicity and immutability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God knows every detail of your life in a way that does not change with your changing circumstances? How does that affect your trust in Him today?
?
If God's knowledge of your actions is active (causing them) and not passive (reacting to them), what does that imply about the sincerity of your worship? Are you trying to impress a God who already knows, or are you worshipping out of love and submission?
?
The philosophers argue that God's essence would change if He knew things as they happen. Does your conception of God allow for this perfect immutability, or do you unconsciously imagine God 'learning' about your sins? Commit to correcting that image.

God Knows from His Essence, Not from Things
The philosophers assert that God knows from His essence alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' foundational claim that the Necessary Being knows all things from His own essence, not by receiving knowledge from external objects.

Core Idea
God's knowledge originates from His essence, not from the things He knows.

Before addressing how God knows particulars without change, the philosophers establish a critical principle: God's knowledge is not derived from the objects of knowledge. If it were, His essence would depend on them. This lesson presents that foundational principle and its logical basis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get its flow. Then read the translation. Notice the negative and positive claims.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two impossible scenarios
The passage states that if God knew from things, His essence would either be constituted by that knowledge or knowledge would be an accident. Both are impossible. Identify why each is rejected.
This is the negative argument.
3
Understand the positive claim
The passage then states that God knows from His essence as the principle of all existence. He knows perfect beings in their identities and contingent beings by species and individuals.
God's knowledge is self-derived and comprehensive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت الفلاسفة على طريقهم واجب الوجود ليس يجوز أن يعقل الأشياء من الأشياء وإلا فذاته إما متقومة بما يعقل أو عارض لها أن يعقل وذلك محال بل كما أنه مبدأ كل موجود فيعقل من ذاته ما هو مبدأ له مبدأ وهو للموجودات التامة بأعيانها والموجودات الكائنة الفاسدة بأنواعها وأشخاصها ولا يجوز أن يكون عاقلا لهذه التغيرات مع تغيرها حتى يكون تارة يعقل منها أنها موجودة غير معدومة وتارة يعلمها معدومة غير موجودة ولكل واحد من الأمرين صورة عقلية على حدة ولا واحد من الصورتين يبقى مع الثانية فيكون واجب الوجود متغير الذات بل هو إنما يعقل كل شيء على نحو كلي فعلي لا انفعالي ومع ذلك لا يعزب عنه مثقال ذرة في السماوات ولا في الأرض وأما كيفية ذلك فلأنه إذا عقل ذاته وعقل أنها مبدأ كل موجود عقل أوائل الموجودات وما يتولد منها ولا شيء من الأشياء يوجد إلا وقد صار معلوما من جهة ما يكون واجبا بسببه فتكون الأسباب بمصادمات تتأدى إلى أن يوجد منها الأمور الجزئية فالأول يعلم الأسباب ومطابقاتها فيعلم ما يتأدى إليه وما بينها من الأزمنة وما لها من العودات فيكون مدركا للأمور الجزئية من حيث هي كلية أعني من حيث لها صفات أو أحوال تستعد بها لأن تكون كلية وإن تخصصت فبالإضافة إلى زمان ومكان وحال متشخصة فيعقل ذاته ونظام الخير والموجود في الكل منه ونفس مدركة الكل سبب لوجود الكل منه ومبدأ له وإبداع وإيجاب وإيجاد فلا يتبع علمه معلوما بل يسبقه ولا يكتسب عنه صفة بل يكسبه ولا يتغير بتغير المعلوم بل يغيره ولا يتعلق بأمر معين من حيث هو معين شخصي حتى لو زال الشخص زال العلم بوجوده وهذا كمن عرف أن القمر إذا اجتمع مع الذنب في برج كذا وكانت الشمس في برج مقابل وقع ثم الكسوف فعلمه هذا قبل وجود الكسوف وبعده وفي حال الكسوف على وجه ليس يتغير بحدوث الكسوف وكذا كل علم كلي ثم الجزئي مندرج تحت الكلي على سبيل التضمن فيصير الكل معلومًا له على هذا الطريق فمن قال منهم إنه لا يعقل إلا ذاته أراد به أنه يعقل كونه مبدأ وعقله ذلك هو الموجب لحصول ما يصدر عنه ومن قال منهم إنه
The philosophers said, according to their way: The Necessary Being (واجب الوجود) is not such that it is permissible for Him to know things from [other] things; otherwise His essence would either be constituted by what He knows, or [the act of] knowing would be an accident for it, and that is impossible. Rather, just as He is the principle of every existent, He knows from His essence what is a principle for it—He is a principle—and He [knows] the perfect existents in their very identities, and the generated and corruptible existents by their species and individuals. And it is not permissible that He be a knower of these changing things along with their change, such that He sometimes knows from them that they exist not non-existent, and sometimes knows them as non-existent not existent; for each of the two states there is a separate intelligible form, and neither of the two forms remains with the second, so the Necessary Being would be changing in essence. Rather, He only knows every thing in a universal, active manner, not a passive one; and with that, not the weight of an atom escapes Him in the heavens or the earth. As for the modality of that: because when He knows His essence and knows that it is the principle of every existent, He knows the first existents and what is generated from them; and nothing of things exists except that it has become known by virtue of being necessitated by its cause; so the causes, with their collisions, lead to the existence of particular things from them. So the First [i.e., God] knows the causes and their correspondences, and thus knows what they lead to, the times between them, and their returns; thus He is an apprehender of particular things insofar as they are universal—I mean, insofar as they have attributes or states by which they are disposed to become universal. And if they become particularized, it is by relation to a time, place, and individuating state; so He knows His essence, the system of good and the existent in the whole from Him, and the very self that apprehends the whole is the cause of the existence of the whole from Him and its principle and origination, necessitation, and creation. Thus His knowledge does not follow the known, but rather precedes it; nor does He acquire a description from it, but rather He causes it to acquire; nor does He change with the change of the known, but rather He changes it; nor does it [His knowledge] attach to a particular thing insofar as it is a particular individual, such that if the individual ceased, the knowledge of its existence would cease. This is like one who knows that when the moon conjuncts the [lunar] node in such-and-such a sign and the sun is in the opposite sign, an eclipse occurs—so his knowledge of this is before the existence of the eclipse, after it, and during the eclipse in a way that does not change with the occurrence of the eclipse. And likewise, every universal knowledge: then the particular is subsumed under the universal by way of inclusion, so the whole becomes known to Him in this way. Thus, whoever among them said that He only knows His essence meant by that that He knows His being a principle, and His knowing that is what necessitates the occurrence of what proceeds from Him; and whoever among them said that He... [text incomplete]
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage presents and defends the philosophers' (falasifa) view on divine knowledge, specifically how God knows particulars without change. It begins by stating that the Necessary Being (واجب الوجود) cannot know things from external objects, because that would make His essence dependent on them or make knowledge an accident in Him—both impossible. Instead, God knows everything from His own essence, as He is the principle of all existence. He knows perfect beings in their very identities and corruptible beings by their species and individuals. The passage then raises an objection: if God knew changing things directly, His knowledge would change with them, requiring different intelligible forms at different times, which would imply change in the divine essence. The answer is that God knows everything in a universal and active manner (على نحو كلي فعلي), not passive (لا انفعالي). This universal knowledge encompasses all particulars without change. The modality is explained: because God knows His essence as the principle, He knows the first existents and their generated outcomes. All things become known through their causes and the collisions of causes leading to particular events. God knows causes, their correspondences, times, and returns, thereby apprehending particular things insofar as they are universal—i.e., possessing attributes that make them universalizable. When they become particularized, it is by relation to time, place, and state. Thus God's knowledge precedes the known, does not acquire attributes from it, and does not change with change in the known; rather, it causes change. The example of the eclipse illustrates: one who knows the general rule of eclipses knows any particular eclipse before, during, and after its occurrence without change in knowledge. Similarly, all universal knowledge subsumes particulars. The passage concludes by interpreting two sayings among philosophers: those who say God knows only His essence mean He knows His being the principle, which necessitates all that proceeds from Him. The second saying is cut off.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
يعقل
مبدأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God learns about things by observing them or receiving information from them.
Correction: The philosophers insist that God's knowledge is self-derived; He knows all things from His own essence, not from the things themselves.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why can't God know things from other things?
A) Because things are too numerous.
B) Because it would make His essence dependent or knowledge accidental.
C) Because He only knows universals.
D) Because He is simple and cannot receive impressions.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers ground God's knowledge in His own essence, not in external objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if God's knowledge of you is incomplete until you tell Him, or do you trust that He knows you intimately from His essence, before you even existed? What would change if you truly believed that?
?
When you sin in secret, do you act as if God needs to discover it, forgetting that His knowledge is not acquired from your actions but from His own essence? How can this truth drive you to immediate repentance?
?
If God knows all things from His essence—including your deepest struggles and unspoken needs—why do you rely on your own plans more than on His perfect knowledge? What step can you take today to surrender that area to Him?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
God Knows Particulars Universally Without Change
The philosophers explain how God knows particulars universally and actively, using causality and an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers argue that God knows changing particulars without any change in His essence, through universal active knowledge and the modality of causality.

Core Idea
God knows all particulars universally, actively, and without change.

Having established that God's knowledge is from His essence, the philosophers now address the apparent problem of knowing changing particulars. If God knew each particular as it changes, His knowledge would change. Their solution: God knows everything in a universal, active manner. This lesson unpacks that solution and its modality through causality, culminating in the eclipse analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
This longer passage contains the problem, the solution, the causal explanation, and an analogy. Read it once without analyzing.
The passage is continuous; let the argument flow.
2
Understand the problem of change
The passage says that if God knew changing things with their changes, He would sometimes know them existing, sometimes nonexistent, requiring different intelligible forms. That would imply change in His essence, which is impossible.
This is the objection that must be avoided.
3
Grasp the solution: universal active knowledge
The solution is that God knows everything 'in a universal, active manner' (على نحو كلي فعلي), not passively. This universal knowledge does not change with particular changes.
This is the core of the philosophers' answer.
4
Explore the causal modality
The passage explains how God can know particulars universally: He knows His essence as the principle, the first existents, and the chain of causes and their collisions. He knows all causes and their correspondences, thus knowing all outcomes and times.
God's knowledge is of the causal nexus, not of fleeting particulars directly.
5
Reflect on the eclipse analogy
The analogy of an eclipse illustrates: one who knows the general rule of eclipses knows any particular eclipse without change in his knowledge. Similarly, God's universal knowledge includes all particulars.
The analogy clarifies how universal knowledge encompasses particulars.

Terminological Objection: ʿAql vs. ʿĀlim
The author objects to the use of 'aql and 'aqil for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author insists on using the term ʿālim instead of ʿāqil for God and why this terminological choice carries theological weight.

Core Idea
Revelation names God as 'Knower', not 'Intellectual'.

The author begins his objection by highlighting a crucial terminological issue. The philosophers describe God as 'intellectual' (ʿāqil), but Islamic revelation consistently uses the term 'knower' (ʿālim). This is not a mere verbal dispute; it sets the stage for demanding proper proof for God’s knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get the rhythm. Then read the translation.
Notice how the author contrasts 'aql and 'alim.
2
Identify the terminological claim
The author says that the terms 'aql and 'aqil are not used in revelation for God. Instead, we use 'alim. Underline these terms.
The author asserts a scriptural basis.
3
Understand the demand for proof
The author then demands that the philosophers provide evidence that God is 'alim, not just 'aqil. Ask yourself: why is this a challenge?
The burden of proof shifts to the philosophers.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this terminological shift affects the rest of the argument about divine knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحن نتكلم على ذلك بالاعتراض، ويتصفح على كل مسألة منها بالاستعراض. فنغير فنقول أولاً: إطلاق لفظ العقل والعاقل غير مصطلح عليه عندنا، لفظ العقل إلى العلم، ونطلق لفظ العالم بدل العاقل، إذ ورد السمع بكون الباري تعالى عالماً، ولم يرد بكونه عاقلاً. فنطالبكم بالدليل على كون الباري تعالى عالماً، فبم عرفتم ذلك؟ والدليل ما أرشدكم إليه، والبرهان لم يقم عليه. فإن المتكلم يستدل بحصول الإحكام والإتقان في الأفعال على كون الصانع عالماً، وأنتم ما سلكتم هذه الطريقة، ولا استقام ذلك على قاعدتكم. فإن العلم عندكم لم يتعلق بالجزئيات، أو تعلق على وجه كلي، فهو إذا متعلق بالكليات، والأحكام إنما يثبت في الجزئيات المحسوسة. أما الكليات المعقولة فهي مقدرة في الذهن، فما فيه الإحكام ليس بمعلوم على الوجه الذي يقتضيه الإحكام، وما هو معلوم فلم شاهد إحكام فيه، فبطل الاستدلال من هذا الطريق.
And we will discuss this with objection, and scrutinize each issue of them by review. So we change and say first: The application of the term 'intellect' (al-ʿaql) and 'intellectual' (al-ʿāqil) is not conventional among us; the term 'intellect' (al-ʿaql) belongs to knowledge, and we use the term 'knower' (al-ʿālim) instead of 'intellectual' (al-ʿāqil), since revelation has come that the Creator, exalted, is 'knower' (ʿālim), and it has not come that He is 'intellectual' (ʿāqil). So we demand from you the proof that the Creator, exalted, is 'knower'; by what did you know that? The evidence is what guided you to it, but the demonstration has not been established upon it. For the mutakallim (theologian) infers from the occurrence of precision and perfection in actions that the Maker is knowing, but you have not pursued this method, nor does it hold on your basis. For knowledge according to you is not attached to particulars, or it is attached in a universal manner, so it pertains to universals, while precision and perfection are only established in sensible particulars. As for intellectual universals, they are estimated in the mind. So that in which there is precision is not known in the way that precision requires, and that which is known has no witnessed precision in it, so the inference from this way is invalidated.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage begins the author's objection against the philosophers' view on divine knowledge. First, the author points out a terminological issue: the terms 'aql (intellect) and 'aqil (intellectual) are not used in Islamic revelation for God; rather, the term 'alim (knower) is used. So the author demands a proof that God is 'alim, not just 'aqil. The author then contrasts the method of the mutakallimun (theologians), who argue from the precision and perfection in created acts to the knowledge of the Maker. The author argues that the philosophers cannot use this method because, in their view, God's knowledge is only of universals, not particulars. But the observed precision and perfection are in sensible particulars. Thus, the very things that exhibit precision are not known by God in their particularity (according to the philosophers), and the universal concepts that God knows are only mental constructs without actual witnessed precision. Therefore, the design argument fails on the philosophers' premises.

العقل
العاقل
العالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the objection is merely about word preference, not substantive theology.
Correction: The terminological shift emphasizes that God's knowledge is affirmed by revelation, not by philosophical reasoning alone.

Checkpoint
Why does the author object to using the term ʿāqil for God?
A) Because ʿāqil implies a process of reasoning not befitting God
B) Because revelation uses ʿālim, not ʿāqil
C) Because ʿāqil is a term the philosophers invented
D) Because ʿāqil means 'intellectual' which is a human attribute

Key Takeaway
The author insists on scriptural terminology for God's attributes, challenging the philosophers to provide proof for their own terms.

Invalidating the Design Argument on Philosophical Grounds
The author challenges the design proof using the philosophers' own epistemology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author argues that the design proof for God's knowledge fails because the philosophers deny God's knowledge of particulars.

Core Idea
Design is in particulars; God knows only universals—so the argument collapses.

The author now turns to a substantive objection: the philosophers cannot use the design argument because their own view that God knows only universals undermines it. The mutakallimun (theologians) infer knowledge from the perfect order in the world, but if God does not know particulars, then the very things that show design are not known by God, and the universals He knows have no observable design.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the source
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'فإن المتكلم يستدل' and its translation.
Notice the contrast between the mutakallim and the philosophers.
2
Identify the design argument
Find the statement: the mutakallim infers the Maker's knowledge from the precision and perfection in acts. Underline 'الإحكام والإتقان'.
3
Understand the philosophers' position
The passage states that in the philosophers' view, knowledge does not attach to particulars or attaches only universally. So God knows universals, not particulars.
This is key to the objection.
4
See the invalidation
The author argues that precision is in sensible particulars, but God does not know them as particulars. The universals He knows are mental concepts without witnessed precision. Thus the design argument fails.
The conclusion: 'فبطل الاستدلال من هذا الطريق'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحن نتكلم على ذلك بالاعتراض، ويتصفح على كل مسألة منها بالاستعراض. فنغير فنقول أولاً: إطلاق لفظ العقل والعاقل غير مصطلح عليه عندنا، لفظ العقل إلى العلم، ونطلق لفظ العالم بدل العاقل، إذ ورد السمع بكون الباري تعالى عالماً، ولم يرد بكونه عاقلاً. فنطالبكم بالدليل على كون الباري تعالى عالماً، فبم عرفتم ذلك؟ والدليل ما أرشدكم إليه، والبرهان لم يقم عليه. فإن المتكلم يستدل بحصول الإحكام والإتقان في الأفعال على كون الصانع عالماً، وأنتم ما سلكتم هذه الطريقة، ولا استقام ذلك على قاعدتكم. فإن العلم عندكم لم يتعلق بالجزئيات، أو تعلق على وجه كلي، فهو إذا متعلق بالكليات، والأحكام إنما يثبت في الجزئيات المحسوسة. أما الكليات المعقولة فهي مقدرة في الذهن، فما فيه الإحكام ليس بمعلوم على الوجه الذي يقتضيه الإحكام، وما هو معلوم فلم شاهد إحكام فيه، فبطل الاستدلال من هذا الطريق.
And we will discuss this with objection, and scrutinize each issue of them by review. So we change and say first: The application of the term 'intellect' (al-ʿaql) and 'intellectual' (al-ʿāqil) is not conventional among us; the term 'intellect' (al-ʿaql) belongs to knowledge, and we use the term 'knower' (al-ʿālim) instead of 'intellectual' (al-ʿāqil), since revelation has come that the Creator, exalted, is 'knower' (ʿālim), and it has not come that He is 'intellectual' (ʿāqil). So we demand from you the proof that the Creator, exalted, is 'knower'; by what did you know that? The evidence is what guided you to it, but the demonstration has not been established upon it. For the mutakallim (theologian) infers from the occurrence of precision and perfection in actions that the Maker is knowing, but you have not pursued this method, nor does it hold on your basis. For knowledge according to you is not attached to particulars, or it is attached in a universal manner, so it pertains to universals, while precision and perfection are only established in sensible particulars. As for intellectual universals, they are estimated in the mind. So that in which there is precision is not known in the way that precision requires, and that which is known has no witnessed precision in it, so the inference from this way is invalidated.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage begins the author's objection against the philosophers' view on divine knowledge. First, the author points out a terminological issue: the terms 'aql (intellect) and 'aqil (intellectual) are not used in Islamic revelation for God; rather, the term 'alim (knower) is used. So the author demands a proof that God is 'alim, not just 'aqil. The author then contrasts the method of the mutakallimun (theologians), who argue from the precision and perfection in created acts to the knowledge of the Maker. The author argues that the philosophers cannot use this method because, in their view, God's knowledge is only of universals, not particulars. But the observed precision and perfection are in sensible particulars. Thus, the very things that exhibit precision are not known by God in their particularity (according to the philosophers), and the universal concepts that God knows are only mental constructs without actual witnessed precision. Therefore, the design argument fails on the philosophers' premises.

الإتقان
الجزئيات

Correction: The author affirms the design argument for the mutakallimun; he is showing that the philosophers cannot consistently use it.

Checkpoint

A) Because they do not believe in design
B) Because they deny that God has any knowledge
C) Because according to them, God only knows universals, but design is observed in particulars

The design argument for God's knowledge cannot be used by those who deny God's knowledge of particulars.

The Terminological Objection: Rejecting 'Aql and 'Aqil for God
Author rejects 'aql/'āqil terms for God based on revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author insists on using 'ālim rather than 'āqil for God and how this terminological choice is grounded in revelation.

Core Idea
Revelation, not reason, dictates the names we attribute to God.

This passage begins the author's series of objections to those who attribute intellect ('aql) to God. He first clarifies their position on divine knowledge, then immediately raises a terminological issue: the words 'aql and 'āqil are not found in the Qur'an or Sunnah for God. Instead, revelation uses 'ālim (knowing). This sets the stage for demanding a proof that God is knowing.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening clarification
Read the first part where the author summarizes the opponent's view on God's knowledge of universals and particulars. This sets the context for the objection.
Notice the distinction between knowing universals and particulars.
2
Identify the terminological objection
Locate where the author states that the terms 'aql and 'āqil are not conventionally used by us (the Ash'arites). He explains that they use 'ālim instead.
3
Understand the basis in revelation
The author argues that scripture (al-sam') came with 'ālim, not 'āqil. This is a key principle: divine attributes are derived from revelation.
Consider why the author prioritizes revelation over reason.
4
Reflect on the implication
Think about what this means for theological method: if names are determined by revelation, then rational speculation alone cannot ascribe names to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعقل الكليات دون الجزئيات أراد ما قررناه ومن قال منهم إنه يعقل الكليات والجزئيات أراد ما عقله مقصودا؛ أي مبدأ وما يصدر عنه إلا أن ذلك على وجه، فهذا كلام القوم، ونحن نتكلم على ذلك بالاعتراض، ويتصفح على كل مسألة منها بالاستعراض. فنقول أولاً: إطلاق لفظ العقل والعاقل غير مصطلح عليه عندنا، إنما نطلق لفظ العقل إلى العلم، ونطلق لفظ العالم بدل العاقل، إذ ورد السمع بكون الباري تعالى عالما، ولم يرد بكونه عاقلا. فنطالبكم بالدليل على كون الباري تعالى عالما، فبم عرفتم ذلك؟ والدليل ما أرشدكم إليه، والبرهان لم يقم عليه، فإن المتكلم يستدل بحصول الإحكام والإتقان في الأفعال على كون الصانع عالما، وأنتم ما سلكتم هذه الطريقة، ولا استقام ذلك على قاعدتكم، فإن العلم عندكم لم يتعلق بالجزئيات، أو تعلق على وجه كلي، فهو إذا متعلق بالكليات، والأحكام إنما يثبت في الجزئيات المحسوسة، أما الكليات المعقولة فهي مقدرة في الذهن، فما فيه الإحكام ليس بمعلوم على الوجه الذي يقتضيه الإحكام، وما هو معلوم فلم يشاهد إحكام فيه، فبطل الاستدلال من هذا الطريق.
He knows universals without particulars, intended what we established, and whoever among them said that He knows universals and particulars intended what He knows intentionally; that is, a principle and what proceeds from it, except that it is in a certain way. So this is the statement of the people, and we will speak about that by way of objection, and scrutinize each issue by review. We say first: The use of the terms intellect (ʿaql) and intelligent (ʿāqil) is not conventionally established among us. We only apply the term intellect to knowledge, and we apply the term knower (ʿālim) instead of intelligent, since the revealed text came that the Exalted Creator is knowing (ʿālim), and it did not come that He is intelligent (ʿāqil). So we demand from you the proof that the Exalted Creator is knowing: how did you know that? The proof is what guided you to it, and the demonstration (burhān) has not been established for it. For the theologian (mutakallim) infers from the existence of perfection and precision (iḥkām and itqān) in actions that the Maker is knowing, but you have not followed this method, nor has it been established on your foundation. For knowledge with you does not pertain to particulars, or if it does pertain in a universal manner, then it pertains to universals, while perfection and precision are only established in sensible particulars. As for intelligible universals, they are estimated in the mind. So that which contains perfection is not known in the way that perfection requires, and that which is known has not witnessed perfection in it. Thus the inference from this path is invalidated.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is an objection. The author first clarifies the opponent's position regarding God's knowledge of universals and particulars. Then he raises a terminological objection: the words 'aql and 'āqil are not used in revelation for God; instead, we use 'ālim. So the opponent must prove that God is 'ālim. He then criticizes the standard mutakallimūn argument from design (iḥkām and itqān in actions) because if God only knows universals, the perfection seen in particular sensible things does not prove that He knows them in the particular sense. The inference is thus invalid for those who deny particular knowledge.

عاقل

سمع

Misconception: One might think that reason alone can determine that God is 'āqil (intelligent).
Correction: The author argues that revelation does not use that term, and we are bound by revelation in attributing names to God.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why do we not use 'āqil for God?
A) Because 'aql is not an attribute of God.
B) Because revelation does not use that term for God.
C) Because the opponent denied it.
D) Because 'aql means knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The names we attribute to God must come from revelation, not rational speculation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Invalidation of the Design Argument Under the Opponent's Framework
Author critiques the design argument given the opponent's view on universal knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument from design (iḥkām/itqān) fails to prove God's knowledge if God only knows universals.

Core Idea
If God only knows universals, design in particulars cannot prove His knowledge.

Having established the terminological basis, the author now demands a proof that God is 'ālim. He anticipates that the opponent might use the argument from design (the perfection and precision in actions). However, the author argues that this proof is invalid for those who claim God knows only universals. The reason is that design (iḥkām) is observed in particular sensible things, but if God's knowledge is only universal, He does not know these particulars in their particularity. Thus, the inference collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the demand for proof

Note that the burden of proof is on the opponent.
2
Identify the design argument
Find where the author mentions the mutakallimūn's inference from perfection and precision in actions to the Maker being knowing.
3
See the critique
The author points out that this method does not work on the opponent's foundation because they deny knowledge of particulars. Perfection is in sensible particulars, but their God knows only universals.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that the inference is invalid (batala al-istidlāl).
Consider why the argument fails specifically for the opponent.

Avicenna's Proof: Abstract Essences as Intellects
Avicenna's proof that every essence abstracted from matter is an intellect by itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Avicenna's proof that every essence abstracted from matter is an intellect by its own essence, and how he defines intellection as comparison.

Core Idea
Abstract essences are intellects by their own essence.

Avicenna presents a dense but elegant argument that any essence not tied to matter has no barrier to being compared to another abstract essence. This comparison, he claims, is exactly what intellection is. In this lesson, we will unpack his reasoning, see how he defines intellect, and understand why no additional forms are needed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the key terms: مجرّد, ماهية, مقارنة, ارتسام, عقل.
2
Identify the main claim
What is Avicenna trying to prove? Identify the conclusion: every abstract essence is an intellect by itself.
3
Understand the definition of intellect
Avicenna defines intellect (عقل) as 'a comparison of an abstract essence to an abstract essence'. Note that this is not the usual definition; it's technical.
This definition is crucial for the proof to work.
4
Follow the argument: imprinting as awareness
He argues that when an abstract essence is imprinted in our rational faculty, the imprinting itself is the awareness. There is no need for a separate act.
This avoids an infinite regress.
5
Reflect on the infinite regress argument
Why would requiring an additional form lead to infinite regress? Because we would need another form to connect the first form, and so on.
This strengthens his definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو علي بن سينا: البرهان على أن كل مجرَّد عن المادة فهو عقل بذاته هو بدليل أن كل ماهية مجردة عن المادة، فلا مانع لها من حيث ذاتها عن مقارنة ماهية أخرى مجردة، فيمكن أن تكون معقولة؛ أي: مرتسمة في ماهية أخرى مجردة، وارتسامها هو مقارنتها، ولا معنى للعقل إلا أنه مقارنة ماهية مجردة لماهية مجردة، ولذلك إذا ارتسمت في القوة العاقلة منا ماهية مجردة كان نفس الارتسام فيها هو نفس شعورها بها وإدراكها لها، وذلك هو العقل والتعقل، إذ لو كان يحتاج إلى هيئة وصورة غير الصورة المرتسمة لكان الكلام في تلك الصورة كالكلام في هذه الصورة ويتسلسل، وإذا كان نفس تلك المقارنة هو العقل فيلزم منه أن كل ماهية مجردة فلا مانع لها من ذاتها أن تعقل.
Abū ʿAlī Ibn Sīnā said: The proof that every abstracted from matter is an intellect by itself is by the evidence that every essence abstracted from matter has no obstacle from its own self to being compared (muqārana) with another abstract essence, so it can be conceived (maʿqūla); that is, imprinted (murtasima) in another abstract essence. Its imprinting is its comparison. And there is no meaning of intellect (ʿaql) except that it is a comparison of an abstract essence to an abstract essence. Therefore, when an abstract essence is imprinted in our rational faculty (al-quwwa al-ʿāqila), the very imprinting in it is the very awareness (shuʿūr) of it and perception (idrāk) of it. That is intellection (al-ʿaql) and intellectual apprehension (al-taʿaqqul). For if it needed a disposition (hayʾa) and form (ṣūra) other than the imprinted form, then the discussion about that form would be like the discussion about this form, leading to infinite regress. And since that comparison itself is intellect, it follows that every abstract essence has no obstacle from its own self to intellect (that is, to be an intellect or to intellect).
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a proof by Avicenna. He argues that any essence free from matter has no inherent barrier to being compared to another abstract essence. This comparison is exactly what intellection is. When such an essence is imprinted in a rational faculty, that imprinting is itself the awareness. He adds that if it required an additional form, there would be an infinite regress. Therefore, every abstract essence is intrinsically an intellect (capable of intellection and being intellected). This is a foundational argument in Avicenna's metaphysics.

Key Terms
مجرّد عن المادة
ماهية
مقارنة
ارتسام
عقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof only applies to God or to Divine intellects.
Correction: Avicenna intends this proof for any essence abstracted from matter, including human intellects and immaterial forms.

Checkpoint
According to Avicenna in this passage, what is the meaning of intellect (ʿaql)?
A) A substance that thinks.
B) A comparison of an abstract essence to another abstract essence.
C) A faculty of the soul.
D) A form in matter.

Key Takeaway
Every essence abstracted from matter is, by its very nature, an intellect that can both intellect and be intellected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat your own intellect as something passive that merely receives forms, or do you recognize that your intellect is active by its very nature? What would change if you believed that your intellect, in its essence, is an active comparison of meanings?
?
If every abstract essence is an intellect by itself, what does that imply about your own soul's capacity to know God? Are you living as though your intellect is merely material or as though it is truly abstract and thus capable of direct intellection of divine realities?
?
Avicenna's proof shows that abstract essences have no obstacle to intellection from their own side. What obstacles have you placed in your own soul that prevent you from this kind of direct apprehension of truth? Are you willing to remove them?

The Circularity of the Freedom-from-Matter Argument
The initial claim and its immediate refutation for circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that God's freedom from matter implies self-knowledge is logically circular.

Core Idea
Being free from matter does not automatically make something an intellect.

The debate begins with a common claim: God is free from matter, so He is not veiled from Himself, hence He knows Himself. But this reasoning begs the question. In this lesson, we examine the initial argument and the immediate objection that it assumes what it needs to prove.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial claim
Read the philosophers' claim: 'being free from matter, so He is not veiled, thus knowing.' Notice they assume that absence of veil means knowledge.
2
Examine the objection
The objector says this is 'begging the first question'—'not veiled' already means 'knowing'. This is circular.
3
Consider the analogy
Negating other attributes like finitude does not imply knowledge. So why would negating matter imply knowledge?
4
Conclude the failure
The argument provides no real proof; it is not a valid middle term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : طريقنا في ذلك إنما هو برئ من المادة وعلائقها فغير محتجب عن ذاته ؛ لأن الحجاب هو المادة، والمقدس عن المادة هو عالم لنفسه بنفسه إذ لا حجاب. قيل : هذه مصادرة على المطلوب الأول، فإن معنى قولكم : غير محتجب عن ذاته ؛ أي هو عالم بذاته، والكلام فيه كالكلام في الأول، وتقريره إنما هو برئ من المادة، فهو عالم فلم قلتم : إنما هو برئ عن المادة عالم، ونفي المادة كيف يناسب العالمية والعلم هذا كمن نفى التناهي والانقسام والأين والكيف عنه لم يجب من ذلك أن يكون عالماً ، فنفي الجسمية والهيولانية عنه ليس يقتضي أن يكون عالما، ولم نجد لعامتكم برهانا على ثبوت كونه عالما بالمعلومات، سواء التجرد عن المادة وعلائقها ، وليس ذلك حدا أوسط في برهان أن ولا في برهان لم. قال أبو علي بن سينا: البرهان على أن كل مجرَّد عن المادة فهو عقل بذاته هو بدليل أن كل ماهية مجردة عن المادة، فلا مانع لها من حيث ذاتها عن مقارنة ماهية أخرى مجردة، فيمكن أن تكون معقولة ؛ أي: مرتسمة في ماهية أخرى مجردة، وارتسامها هو مقارنتها ، ولا معنى للعقل إلا أنه مقارنة ماهية مجردة لماهية مجردة، ولذلك إذا ارتسمت في القوة العاقلة منا ماهية مجردة كان نفس الارتسام فيها هو نفس شعورها بها وإدراكها لها، وذلك هو العقل والتعقل، إذ لو كان يحتاج إلى هيئة وصورة غير الصورة المرتسمة لكان الكلام في تلك الصورة كالكلام في هذه الصورة ويتسلسل ، وإذا كان نفس تلك المقارنة هو العقل فيلزم منه أن كل ماهية مجردة فلا مانع لها من ذاتها أن تعقل. قلت: ما زدت في البيان إلا أنك جعلت المقارنة حدا أوسط، ولسنا نشك أنك ما عنيت بهذه المقارنة مقارنة الجسم، ولا مقارنة الجوهر العرض، ولا مقارنة الصورة المادة، بل عنيت به كما فسرت التمثيل والارتسام، وعنيت بالتمثيل والارتسام التعقل فقد صادرت على المطلوب الأول أقبح المصادرة، فكأنك قلت : الدليل على كونه عالما أنه لا يمتنع على آنيته وذاته أن يكون مرتسما بصورة - أي : عالما فبان أنك أدرجت لفظ المقارنة حتى أخفيت الحال، ثم فسرت المقارنة حتى أبديت الحال زالت المصادرة عنك كل الزوال. وأما استشهادك بالقوة العاقلة منا فإنما ينفعك مع من لا ينازعك في أن العقل هل يعقل بذاته ذاته ؟ وإن عقل أفيعقل غير ذاته أم بعقل هو ذاته؟ أما من نازعك في إثبات كونه عقلاً وعاقلا -أي: علمًا وعالماً فلا ينفعه هذا الاستشهاد. وأما اقتباس كونه عاقلا من كونه معقولا فهو من أمحل المطلوبات، وهو كمن قال : لا يمتنع عليه أن يعلم فلا يمتنع عليه أن يعلم، والعرض في ذاته ممتنع أن يعقل ويعلم عند القوم، فكيف يصح القياس؟ وهب أنه يعلم فلم ينبغي أن يعلم، ثم أخذ ما يجب مما لا يمتنع من أبعد القياسات وأمحل المحالات، فإن سلمنا لكم كونه تعالى عالما بذاته، وعلمه بذاته نفس علمه بعلمه، فلم قلتم : إن علمه
They said: Our way in this is only being free from matter and its attachments, so He is not veiled from His essence; because the veil is matter, and one sanctified from matter is knowing of Himself by Himself since there is no veil. It was said: This is a begging of the first question, for the meaning of your saying 'not veiled from His essence' is that He is knowing of His essence, and the discussion about it is like the discussion about the first, and its formulation is only that He is free from matter, so He is knowing. So why did you say 'He is only free from matter, knowing'? And how does the negation of matter befit knowingness and knowledge? This is like one who negated finitude, divisibility, place, and modality from Him; it does not necessarily follow from that that He is knowing. So the negation of corporeality and hylomorphism from Him does not imply that He is knowing. And we have not found for your common people a proof for the establishment of His being knowing of things known, whether through abstraction from matter and its attachments, and that is not a middle term in a proof for 'that' nor in a proof for 'why'. Abu Ali ibn Sina said: The proof that every abstract from matter is an intellect in itself is by the evidence that every essence abstract from matter has no impediment from its own aspect to the conjunction with another abstract essence, so it can be intelligible, i.e., imprinted in another abstract essence, and its imprinting is its conjunction. And there is no meaning of intellect except that it is a conjunction of an abstract essence with an abstract essence. Therefore, when an abstract essence imprints in the rational faculty in us, that imprinting itself is its very awareness of it and its perception of it, and that is intellect and intellection, for if it needed a disposition and form other than the imprinted form, then the discussion about that form would be like the discussion about this form and would regress infinitely. And if that conjunction itself is intellect, then it follows that every abstract essence has no impediment from its essence to intellect. I said: You have not increased in the explanation except that you made conjunction a middle term, and we do not doubt that you did not mean by this conjunction the conjunction of body, nor the conjunction of substance and accident, nor the conjunction of form and matter, but you meant by it, as you interpreted, representation and imprinting, and by representation and imprinting you meant intellection. So you have begged the first question in the ugliest begging. It is as if you said: The proof for His being knowing is that it is not impossible for His selfness and essence to be imprinted with a form—i.e., knowing. So it became clear that you inserted the word 'conjunction' to conceal the situation, then you interpreted 'conjunction' to reveal the situation, and the begging left you completely. As for your citation of the rational faculty in us, it only benefits you with one who does not dispute with you whether the intellect intellects its essence by itself? And if it intellects, does it intellect something other than its essence or by an intellect that is its essence? As for one who disputes with you in establishing that He is intellect and intelligent—i.e., knowledge and knowing—this citation does not benefit him. As for deriving 'being intelligent' from 'being intelligible', it is among the most absurd begged questions, and it is like one who said: It is not impossible for Him to know, so it is not impossible for Him to know. And the accident in its essence is impossible to be intellect or known according to the people, so how is the analogy valid? Suppose He knows, yet He ought not to know? Then taking what is necessary from what is not impossible is among the most far-fetched analogies and most absurd impossibilities. For even if we grant you that God—exalted—is knowing of His essence, and His knowledge of His essence is identical to His knowledge of His knowledge, why did you say that His knowledge...
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage presents a layered theological debate. It begins with the claim of some group that God's freedom from matter entails that He is not veiled from Himself and is therefore self-knowing. An immediate objection is raised: this is a circular argument (musadara), because saying 'not veiled from Himself' already assumes He is knowing. Negating matter does not automatically imply knowledge—just as negating finitude or location does not imply knowledge. The objector demands a genuine proof. Next, Ibn Sina offers a proof: every essence abstract from matter can be united (muqarana) with another abstract essence, and this union is precisely what intellect ('aql) is. Therefore, any abstract essence is an intellect. He illustrates with human rational faculty: when an abstract form imprints (irtisam) in the mind, that imprinting is identical to awareness. The author then refutes Ibn Sina. First, he argues that Ibn Sina's 'conjunction' is merely a synonym for 'intellect' – he has defined intellect as conjunction, then concluded that every abstract essence is an intellect because it can enter into conjunction. This is a blatant circularity. Second, the appeal to human intellect is irrelevant: the dispute concerns whether God is an intellect and intelligent at all; Ibn Sina's analogy only works if one already accepts that human intellect is self-aware by its essence, which is precisely what is contested. Third, deriving 'being intelligent' from 'being intelligible' is absurd, because it assumes the very point in question. The author also notes that accidents are thought to be incapable of intellection by the same opponents, so the analogy fails. Finally, he points out that even if we grant that God knows His essence, that does not prove that His knowledge extends to all things, which was the original claim. The passage thus critically examines the logical structure of arguments for divine knowledge from abstraction.

Key Terms
الحجاب (al-hijab)
المصادرة (al-musadara)
العلم (al-'ilm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That being free from matter automatically makes something an intellect or self-knowing.
Correction: The passage shows that this inference is logically unjustified; it is circular and does not follow from mere negation of material properties.

Checkpoint
What is the main logical flaw the author identifies in the initial argument?
A) It assumes that matter is a veil.
B) It equates 'not veiled' with 'knowing' without proof, making it circular.
C) It uses an analogy that does not apply.
D) It denies that God has matter.

Key Takeaway
Freedom from matter does not itself prove that something is an intellect; such an inference is circular unless independently justified.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because something seems logical (like 'God has no matter, so He knows Himself'), it must be true without examining the reasoning? Will you commit this week to inspecting one cherished belief for circularity?
?
The author shows that negating negative attributes (like finitude) does not automatically grant positive attributes (like knowledge). In your understanding of Allah, do you tend to define Him only by what He is not? How can you actively affirm His positive attributes from revelation?
?
This passage warns against accepting sophisticated-sounding arguments without scrutiny. When was the last time you questioned a theological argument you heard? How can you cultivate a habit of seeking sound proofs based on the Quran and Sunnah?

Ibn Sina's Proof from Conjunction
Ibn Sina's positive argument that abstraction entails intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's argument that every abstract essence is an intellect because it can be conjoined with another abstract essence.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina defines intellect as the conjunction of abstract essences.

In response to the objection, Ibn Sina offers a formal syllogism. He argues that any essence free from matter can be united with another abstract essence, and this union is precisely what intellect means. He illustrates with human intellection, where the imprint of an abstract form is identical to awareness.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Sina's proof
Read the proof carefully. Note the key premise: no impediment to conjunction.
2
Understand the definition
Ibn Sina defines intellect as the conjunction of two abstract essences. This is crucial.
3
Study the human analogy
In humans, imprinting an abstract form is identical to awareness; no infinite regress.
4
Connect to conclusion
Since every abstract essence can be conjoined, and conjunction is intellect, every abstract essence is an intellect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage presents a layered theological debate. It begins with the claim of some group that God's freedom from matter entails that He is not veiled from Himself and is therefore self-knowing. An immediate objection is raised: this is a circular argument (musadara), because saying 'not veiled from Himself' already assumes He is knowing. Therefore, any abstract essence is an intellect. He illustrates with human rational faculty: when an abstract form imprints (irtisam) in the mind, that imprinting is identical to awareness. The author then refutes Ibn Sina. First, he argues that Ibn Sina's 'conjunction' is merely a synonym for 'intellect' – he has defined intellect as conjunction, then concluded that every abstract essence is an intellect because it can enter into conjunction. This is a blatant circularity. Second, the appeal to human intellect is irrelevant: the dispute concerns whether God is an intellect and intelligent at all; Ibn Sina's analogy only works if one already accepts that human intellect is self-aware by its essence, which is precisely what is contested. Third, deriving 'being intelligent' from 'being intelligible' is absurd, because it assumes the very point in question. The author also notes that accidents are thought to be incapable of intellection by the same opponents, so the analogy fails. Finally, he points out that even if we grant that God knows His essence, that does not prove that His knowledge extends to all things, which was the original claim. The passage thus critically examines the logical structure of arguments for divine knowledge from abstraction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Sina says that when an abstract essence imprints in your mind, that imprint is precisely your awareness. Think of a moment you truly understood a concept – was that awareness identical to the thought itself, or did you need an extra step? If an extra step exists, what is it? And if not, does that mean your mind is already abstract? How does that confront your daily habit of reducing ideas to images or emotions?
?
The proof claims that every abstract essence can be an intellect because it has no impediment to conjunction. But do you actually live as if your intellect is unencumbered? What material or selfish attachments have you allowed to become impediments to grasping the highest truths about God? Will you identify one impediment today and actively seek to remove it through study and purification?
?
Ibn Sina's argument avoids infinite regress by insisting that the act of intellection is immediate. Consider your own belief in God: when you think of Him, do you rely on a chain of reasoning that never quite ends, or is there a moment of direct intellectual intuition you have experienced? If not, are you willing to strive for that immediate knowing, or are you satisfied with second-hand knowledge? What concrete step can you take this week to seek direct intellectual vision of the divine?

The Refutation of Ibn Sina's Proof
The detailed refutation exposing circularity in Ibn Sina's proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed refutation of Ibn Sina's proof, exposing its circularity, the misuse of analogy, and the flawed derivation.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's proof is circular: he defined intellect as conjunction, then used conjunction to prove intellect.

The author now launches a sharp critique of Ibn Sina's argument. He accuses Ibn Sina of begging the question by using 'conjunction' as a middle term but then interpreting it as intellect itself. He also attacks the analogy with human intellect and the move from 'being intelligible' to 'being intelligent'. This lesson unpacks these criticisms.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening accusation
The author says Ibn Sina made 'conjunction' a middle term, but it is just intellection in disguise.
2
Analyze the circularity
Ibn Sina defined intellect as conjunction, then used conjunction to prove intellect. This is blatant begging.
3
Critique the human analogy
The analogy assumes the human intellect is self-aware by essence, which is precisely disputed.
4
Understand the modal fallacy
Deriving 'intelligent' from 'intelligible' moves from possibility to necessity, which is invalid.

قالوا : طريقنا في ذلك إنما هو برئ من المادة وعلائقها فغير محتجب عن ذاته ؛ لأن الحجاب هو المادة، والمقدس عن المادة هو عالم لنفسه بنفسه إذ لا حجاب. قيل : هذه مصادرة على المطلوب الأول، فإن معنى قولكم : غير محتجب عن ذاته ؛ أي هو عالم بذاته، والكلام فيه كالكلام في الأول، وتقريره إنما هو برئ من المادة، فهو عالم فلم قلتم : إنما هو برئ عن المادة عالم، ونفي المادة كيف يناسب العالمية والعلم هذا كمن نفى التناهي والانقسام والأين والكيف عنه لم يجب من ذلك أن يكون عالماً ، فنفي الجسمية والهيولانية عنه ليس يقتضي أن يكون عالما، ولم نجد لعامتكم برهانا على ثبوت كونه عالما بالمعلومات، سواء التجرد عن المادة وعلائقها ، وليس ذلك حدا أوسط في برهان أن ولا في برهان لم. قال أبو علي بن سينا: البرهان على أن كل مجرَّد عن المادة فهو عقل بذاته هو بدليل أن كل ماهية مجردة عن المادة، فلا مانع لها من حيث ذاتها عن مقارنة ماهية أخرى مجردة، فيمكن أن تكون معقولة ؛ أي: مرتسمة في ماهية أخرى مجردة، وارتسامها هو مقارنتها ، ولا معنى للعقل إلا أنه مقارنة ماهية مجردة لماهية مجردة، ولذلك إذا ارتسمت في القوة العاقلة منا ماهية مجردة كان نفس الارتسام فيها هو نفس شعورها بها وإدراكها لها، وذلك هو العقل والتعقل، إذ لو كان يحتاج إلى هيئة وصورة غير الصورة المرتسمة لكان الكلام في تلك الصورة كالكلام في هذه الصورة ويتسلسل ، وإذا كان نفس تلك المقارنة هو العقل فيلزم منه أن كل ماهية مجردة فلا مانع لها من ذاتها أن تعقل. قلت: ما زدت في البيان إلا أنك جعلت المقارنة حدا أوسط، ولسنا نشك أنك ما عنيت بهذه المقارنة مقارنة الجسم، ولا مقارنة الجوهر العرض، ولا مقارنة الصورة المادة، بل عنيت به كما فسرت التمثيل والارتسام، وعنيت بالتمثيل والارتسام التعقل فقد صادرت على المطلوب الأول أقبح المصادرة، فكأنك قلت : الدليل على كونه عالما أنه لا يمتنع على آنيته وذاته أن يكون مرتسما بصورة - أي : عالما فبان أنك أدرجت لفظ المقارنة حتى أخفيت الحال، ثم فسرت المقارنة حتى أبديت الحال زالت المصادرة عنك كل الزوال. وأما استشهادك بالقوة العاقلة منا فإنما ينفعك مع من لا ينازعك في أن العقل هل يعقل بذاته ذاته ؟ وإن عقل أفيعقل غير ذاته أم بعقل هو ذاته؟ أما من نازعك في إثبات كونه عقلاً وعاقلا -أي: علمًا وعالماً فلا ينفعه هذا الاستشهاد. وأما اقتباس كونه عاقلا من كونه معقولا فهو من أمحل المطلوبات، وهو كمن قال : لا يمتنع عليه أن يعلم فلا يمتنع عليه أن يعلم، والعرض في ذاته ممتنع أن يعقل ويعلم عند القوم، فكيف يصح القياس؟ وهب أنه يعلم فلم ينبغي أن يعلم، ثم أخذ ما يجب مما لا يمتنع من أبعد القياسات وأمحل المحالات، فإن سلمنا لكم كونه تعالى عالما بذاته، وعلمه بذاته نفس علمه بعلمه، فلم قلتم : إن علمه
They said: Our way in this is only being free from matter and its attachments, so He is not veiled from His essence; because the veil is matter, and one sanctified from matter is knowing of Himself by Himself since there is no veil. It was said: This is a begging of the first question, for the meaning of your saying 'not veiled from His essence' is that He is knowing of His essence, and the discussion about it is like the discussion about the first, and its formulation is only that He is free from matter, so He is knowing. So why did you say 'He is only free from matter, knowing'? And how does the negation of matter befit knowingness and knowledge? This is like one who negated finitude, divisibility, place, and modality from Him; it does not necessarily follow from that that He is knowing. So the negation of corporeality and hylomorphism from Him does not imply that He is knowing. And we have not found for your common people a proof for the establishment of His being knowing of things known, whether through abstraction from matter and its attachments, and that is not a middle term in a proof for 'that' nor in a proof for 'why'. Abu Ali ibn Sina said: The proof that every abstract from matter is an intellect in itself is by the evidence that every essence abstract from matter has no impediment from its own aspect to the conjunction with another abstract essence, so it can be intelligible, i.e., imprinted in another abstract essence, and its imprinting is its conjunction. And there is no meaning of intellect except that it is a conjunction of an abstract essence with an abstract essence. Therefore, when an abstract essence imprints in the rational faculty in us, that imprinting itself is its very awareness of it and its perception of it, and that is intellect and intellection, for if it needed a disposition and form other than the imprinted form, then the discussion about that form would be like the discussion about this form and would regress infinitely. And if that conjunction itself is intellect, then it follows that every abstract essence has no impediment from its essence to intellect. I said: You have not increased in the explanation except that you made conjunction a middle term, and we do not doubt that you did not mean by this conjunction the conjunction of body, nor the conjunction of substance and accident, nor the conjunction of form and matter, but you meant by it, as you interpreted, representation and imprinting, and by representation and imprinting you meant intellection. So you have begged the first question in the ugliest begging. It is as if you said: The proof for His being knowing is that it is not impossible for His selfness and essence to be imprinted with a form—i.e., knowing. So it became clear that you inserted the word 'conjunction' to conceal the situation, then you interpreted 'conjunction' to reveal the situation, and the begging left you completely. As for your citation of the rational faculty in us, it only benefits you with one who does not dispute with you whether the intellect intellects its essence by itself? And if it intellects, does it intellect something other than its essence or by an intellect that is its essence? As for one who disputes with you in establishing that He is intellect and intelligent—i.e., knowledge and knowing—this citation does not benefit him. As for deriving 'being intelligent' from 'being intelligible', it is among the most absurd begged questions, and it is like one who said: It is not impossible for Him to know, so it is not impossible for Him to know. And the accident in its essence is impossible to be intellect or known according to the people, so how is the analogy valid? Suppose He knows, yet He ought not to know? Then taking what is necessary from what is not impossible is among the most far-fetched analogies and most absurd impossibilities. For even if we grant you that God—exalted—is knowing of His essence, and His knowledge of His essence is identical to His knowledge of His knowledge, why did you say that His knowledge...
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage presents a layered theological debate. It begins with the claim of some group that God's freedom from matter entails that He is not veiled from Himself and is therefore self-knowing. An immediate objection is raised: this is a circular argument (musadara), because saying 'not veiled from Himself' already assumes He is knowing. Negating matter does not automatically imply knowledge—just as negating finitude or location does not imply knowledge. The objector demands a genuine proof. Next, Ibn Sina offers a proof: every essence abstract from matter can be united (muqarana) with another abstract essence, and this union is precisely what intellect ('aql) is. Therefore, any abstract essence is an intellect. He illustrates with human rational faculty: when an abstract form imprints (irtisam) in the mind, that imprinting is identical to awareness. The author then refutes Ibn Sina. First, he argues that Ibn Sina's 'conjunction' is merely a synonym for 'intellect' – he has defined intellect as conjunction, then concluded that every abstract essence is an intellect because it can enter into conjunction. This is a blatant circularity. Second, the appeal to human intellect is irrelevant: the dispute concerns whether God is an intellect and intelligent at all; Ibn Sina's analogy only works if one already accepts that human intellect is self-aware by its essence, which is precisely what is contested. Third, deriving 'being intelligent' from 'being intelligible' is absurd, because it assumes the very point in question. The author also notes that accidents are thought to be incapable of intellection by the same opponents, so the analogy fails. Finally, he points out that even if we grant that God knows His essence, that does not prove that His knowledge extends to all things, which was the original claim. The passage thus critically examines the logical structure of arguments for divine knowledge from abstraction.

Key Terms
المصادرة (al-musadara)
المقارنة (al-muqarana)
التمثيل (al-tamthil)
القياس (al-qiyas)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ibn Sina's proof is logically sound and not circular.
Correction: The author shows that Ibn Sina's proof is circular because he defined intellect as conjunction and then used conjunction to prove intellect, and his analogies are invalid.

Checkpoint
According to the author, how does Ibn Sina's use of 'conjunction' make the argument circular?
A) He uses conjunction to mean physical union.
B) He defines conjunction as intellection itself, so the conclusion is already in the premise.
C) He denies that conjunction exists.
D) He uses conjunction as a proof for something else.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's proof is invalid because it is circular, relies on an unjustified analogy, and commits a modality fallacy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used a vague term to win a debate or convince yourself, rather than to genuinely understand the truth? What does the author's exposure of Ibn Sina's 'conjunction' trick teach you about the danger of imprecise language in matters of faith? Will you commit to using clear definitions when discussing Allah's attributes?
?
The author accuses Ibn Sina of assuming what he needs to prove. In your own life, where might you be assuming something about Allah (e.g., that He must act in a certain way) without proper evidence from revelation or sound reason? How can you guard your heart from such subtle circular reasoning?
?
If someone challenged your understanding of a core Islamic belief and showed it rested on a fallacy, would you be willing to abandon your position? The author does not spare even the great Ibn Sina. Are you more loyal to a scholar or to truth? Make a firm resolve to seek truth, even if it means admitting you were wrong.

The Dilemma of God's Knowledge: Essence vs. Multiplicity
Analysis of whether God's knowledge of His essence suffices for all knowledge or necessitates multiplicity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma posed by the claim that God's knowledge of His essence is identical to His knowledge of all other things, and why it leads to either exclusivity or multiplicity.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of His essence cannot automatically extend to all things without contradiction.

This passage from Nihayat al-Aqdam presents a sophisticated argument against the view that God's knowledge of His essence suffices for all knowledge. The objector grants that God knows Himself by His essence, but then poses a dilemma: if that same knowledge covers all things, God knows only Himself; if a separate knowledge is needed, multiplicity enters the divine essence. This forces a re-examination of the nature of divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the dense logical style typical of kalam.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The passage presents two alternatives: either God's knowledge of His essence is also knowledge of all things, or a separate knowledge is required. Follow the consequences of each.
The first horn leads to God knowing only Himself; the second leads to multiplicity.
3
Consider the active vs. passive knowledge distinction
The passage ends with a point about active knowledge: if God's knowledge is active, then everything known must be an effect of His act. Reflect on what that implies.
This adds another layer to the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن سلمنا لكم كونه تعالى عالما بذاته، وعلمه بذاته نفس علمه بعلمه، فلم قلتم: إن علمه بذاته الذي هو نفس العلم فعلمه بمعلوماته هو علمه بذاته ما هو مبدأ وهو مبدأ كل موجود، أفيتعلق علمه بذاته ثم يتعلق نفس ذلك العلم بمعلوماته على النسق الذي حصل، أم يتعلق علمه بذاته، ويتعلق علم آخر بمعلوماته ويلزم على الأول أن يقال: لا يعلم إلا ذاته، إذ لا صورة عنده غير ذاته، ولا ارتسام لعقله إلا تعقله، فإن العقل عندكم مقارنة ماهية لماهية والمقارنة هو ارتسام ماهية بماهية فعلى هذا التفسير لم يقترن بوجوده غير وجوده، ولا ارتسم في عقليته غير عقليته، وسائر اللوازم كما مفارقة لذاته يجب أن يكون معقوليتها مفارقة لمعقولية ذاته، ويلزم على القسم الثاني التكثر الصريح، فإن عقله لذاته وعقله للعقل الأول إن كانا شيئًا واحدًا من وجه واحد فيلزم أن تكون ذاته هو العقل الأول والعقل الأول ذاته، وإن لم يكن ذلك على وجه واحد فقد تعدد وتكثر وجوه الذات. ثم نقول: إذا كان عقله وعلمه علمًا فعليا لا انفعاليا، وإنما يلزم منه ما يلزم لعلمه بذاته فيجب أن يكون كل معلوم مفعولا له وهو معلوم لعلمه، فانظروا أي شيء يلزم من ذلك.
If we grant you that He, exalted, is knowing by His essence, and His knowledge of His essence is identical to His knowledge of His knowledge, then why do you say that His knowledge of His essence, which is knowledge itself, is such that His knowledge of known things is His knowledge of His essence as the principle, and it is the principle of every existent? Does His knowledge attach to His essence, then that same knowledge attaches to His known things in the sequence that occurred, or does His knowledge attach to His essence, and another knowledge attaches to His known things? And it follows from the first that it must be said: He knows only His essence, for there is no form with Him other than His essence, and no impression for His intellect except its intellection. For the intellect, according to you, is a comparison of an essence to an essence, and the comparison is an impression of an essence in an essence. According to this interpretation, nothing other than His existence has been conjoined with His existence, and nothing other than His intellectuality has been impressed in His intellectuality. And the other concomitants, as they are separate from His essence, their intelligibility must be separate from the intelligibility of His essence. And upon the second division, explicit multiplicity follows. For His intellection of His essence and His intellection of the first intellect, if they are one thing from one aspect, then it follows that His essence is the first intellect and the first intellect is His essence; and if that is not from one aspect, then the aspects of the essence have become numerous and multiplied. Then we say: If His intellection and knowledge are active knowledge, not passive, and what follows from it is only what follows from His knowledge of His essence, then every known thing must be an effect for Him, and it is known by His knowledge. So see what follows from that.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a logical analysis of the consequences of granting that God is knowing by His essence, and that this knowledge is identical to His knowledge of His knowledge. The objector presents a dilemma: if God's knowledge of His essence is also His knowledge of all other things, then He knows only Himself (since there is no form or impression other than His essence in His intellect). But if there is a separate knowledge for other things, then multiplicity arises in the divine essence. The objector then further argues that if God's knowledge is active (fi'lī) rather than passive (infi'ālī), then everything known by Him must be an effect of His act, implying that all known things are produced by Him. This leads to a challenge: what follows from that? The passage is a typical kalam argument exploring the implications of a given position to show its absurdity.

علم فعلي
علم انفعالي

صورة
ارتسام
تكثر

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's knowledge of His essence automatically encompasses all knowledge without further complication.
Correction: This argument shows that such a view leads to logical absurdities: either God knows only Himself or multiplicity arises in the divine essence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what follows if God's knowledge of His essence is the same knowledge that attaches to other things?
A) He knows everything in detail
B) He knows only His essence
C) Multiplicity arises in the essence
D) His knowledge becomes passive

Key Takeaway
The dilemma shows that equating God's self-knowledge with universal knowledge leads to logical absurdities, either restricting God's knowledge to Himself or implying multiplicity in the divine essence.

?

?
This passage shows that even a seemingly simple attribute like 'knowledge' leads to deep logical puzzles. How often do you think deeply about the attributes of God, and are you willing to sit with the discomfort of not fully comprehending them? The Dilemma of Divine Knowledge and Simplicity
Dilemma on divine knowledge of other things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the tension between divine simplicity and knowledge of multiple things, and the consequences of the two alternatives presented.

Core Idea
God's knowledge cannot be like human knowledge without undermining His simplicity.

This passage presents a foundational problem in philosophical theology: if God is absolutely simple, how can He know multiple things? The author lays out two alternatives—either His knowledge of essence extends to other things, or He has a separate knowledge—and shows that both lead to undesirable consequences. As you read, consider the tension between divine unity and the complexity of knowing a world of distinct objects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two alternatives
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Identify the two alternatives presented: (1) the same knowledge of essence extends to other things, (2) there is a separate knowledge for other things.
The passage begins with a statement that God's essence is knowledge itself.
2
Understand the definition of intellect
Notice the definition: intellect is 'conjunction of an essence with an essence' (مقارنة ماهية لماهية). This is crucial for the first alternative's consequence.
This definition implies no form other than God's essence can be present.
3
Examine the second alternative and the problem of multiplicity
Consider the consequence: either the intellection of self and of the First Intellect are identical (identifying God with the First Intellect) or distinct (multiplying aspects of the essence).
Both options seem to violate divine simplicity.
4
Reflect on the final point about active knowledge
The passage adds that God's knowledge is active, so every known thing is an effect. Consider the implications for the dilemma.
This ties knowledge to causality.
