⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بذاته الذي هو نفس العلم فعلمه بمعلوماته هو علمه بذاته (۱) ما هو مبدأ و (٣) هو مبدأ كل موجود، أفيتعلق علمه بذاته ثم يتعلق نفس ذلك العلم بمعلوماته على النسق الذي حصل، أم يتعلق علمه بذاته، ويتعلق علم آخر بمعلوماته ويلزم على الأول أن يقال : لا يعلم إلا ذاته ؛ إذ لا صورة عنده غير ذاته، ولا ارتسام لعقله إلا تعقله، فإن العقل عندكم مقارنة ماهية لماهية والمقارنة هو ارتسام ماهية بماهية فعلى هذا التفسير لم يقترن بوجوده غير وجوده، ولا ارتسم في عقليته غير عقليته، وسائر اللوازم كما مفارقة لذاته يجب أن يكون معقوليتها مفارقة لمعقولية ذاته، ويلزم على القسم الثاني التكثر الصريح، فإن عقله لذاته وعقله للعقل الأول إن كانا شيئًا واحدًا من وجه واحد فيلزم أن تكون ذاته هو العقل الأول والعقل الأول ذاته، وإن لم يكن ذلك على وجه واحد فقد تعدد وتكثر وجوه الذات. ثم نقول : إذا كان عقله وعلمه علمًا فعليا لا انفعاليا، وإنما يلزم منه ما يلزم لعلمه بذاته فيجب أن يكون كل معلوم مفعولا له وهو معلوم لعلمه، فانظروا أي شيء يلزم من ذلك.
By His essence which is the knowledge itself, so His knowledge of His known things is His knowledge of His essence (1). He is the principle and (3) He is the principle of every existent. Does His knowledge of His essence then relate? Then the same knowledge relates to His known things in the order that occurred? Or does His knowledge of His essence relate, and another knowledge relates to His known things? The first entails that it is said: He knows only His essence, since there is no image with Him other than His essence, and no imprinting for His intellect except His intellection. For intellect according to you is the conjunction of an essence with an essence, and conjunction is the imprinting of an essence by an essence. So on this interpretation, nothing other than His existence is conjoined with His existence, and nothing other than His intellectuality is imprinted in His intellectuality. And the other necessary concomitants, being separate from His essence, must have their intelligibility separate from the intelligibility of His essence. The second division entails explicit multiplicity. For if His intellection of His essence and His intellection of the First Intellect are one thing from one aspect, then it follows that His essence is the First Intellect and the First Intellect is His essence. And if that is not from one aspect, then the aspects of the essence have become multiple and multiplied. Then we say: If His intellection and His knowledge is an active knowledge, not a passive one, and only what follows from His knowledge of His essence follows from it, then every known thing must be an effect of Him, and it is known by His knowledge. So consider what follows from that.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage presents a dilemma regarding God's knowledge of things other than Himself. The author first states that God's essence is identical with knowledge, so His knowledge of known things is His knowledge of His essence. Then two alternatives are considered: (1) The same knowledge of essence extends to other things in a certain order, or (2) there is a separate knowledge for other things. The first alternative leads to the conclusion that God knows only His essence, because there is no form other than His essence, and intellect is defined as the conjunction of an essence with an essence, implying that nothing other than His existence is conjoined with Him. The second alternative leads to explicit multiplicity: either the intellection of self and of the First Intellect are identical (which would identify the two) or they are distinct, which multiplies the aspects of the essence. The author then adds a further point: if God's knowledge is active (not passive), then every known thing must be an effect of Him, and invites the reader to consider the implications. The passage thus highlights the tension between divine simplicity and the knowledge of multiple things.

A) God knows only His essence.

Key Takeaway
The passage shows that traditional philosophical accounts of divine knowledge face a serious dilemma: either God knows only Himself, or His essence becomes multiple.

?

What does it mean to say His knowledge is active—that you exist because He knows you actively? How does that change your understanding of your dependence on Him?

God's Knowledge as Necessary from Self-Knowledge
Ibn Sina explains that God's knowledge is necessary from self-knowledge, without forms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's concept of necessary (luzumi) divine knowledge that does not necessitate multiplicity.

Core Idea
God knows all things through His self-knowledge as their source.

Ibn Sina presents a sophisticated account of divine knowledge that avoids multiplicity while affirming omniscience. He argues that God's knowledge of everything follows necessarily from His knowledge of Himself as the principle of all existence. Let's see how he establishes this.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening claim
Read the first sentence of the passage where Ibn Sina states that God is knowledgeable of existents with a necessary knowledge from His self-knowledge, not detailed.
2
Understand 'necessary knowledge'
Examine how Ibn Sina defines necessary knowledge: it is knowledge of concomitants that is not intentional but derived from knowledge of the thing itself, thus not adding multiplicity.
This is a key concept in his system.
3
Reflect on the avoidance of multiplicity
Notice that because God's knowledge of existents is contained in His self-knowledge, no additional forms are needed in His essence, so no multiplicity arises.
This answers a major objection against divine knowledge of particulars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابن سينا الرب تعالى عالم بالموجودات، ولكن علمه بها علم لزومي عن علمه بذاته غير مفصل للصور، فإنه يعلم ذاته، وإنما يعلم ذاته كما هو عليه وهو أنه مبدأ للموجودات كلها بأسرها، فيدخل علمه بالموجودات تحت علمه بذاته من غير أن تترتب للموجودات صورة في ذاته حتى يلزم منه كثرة، فإن العلم بلوازم الشيء إذا لم يكن متجها نحو تلك اللوازم قصدًا، بل حاصلا من العلم بلزومها فلا يكون زائدا على نفس العلم بالملزوم، ولا فيه كثرة مترتبة فترتبه حسب ترتيب تلك اللوازم ، ونحن نجد من أنفسنا مثل هذا العلم بالأشياء الكثيرة من غير أن يتمايز لها صورة في ذاتها، وهو أنا إذا سألنا عن مسألة نكون قد علمناها وأتقناها، لكنا في تلك معرضون عنها، فمع السؤال عنها نجد من أنفسنا التفاتا إلى جوابها ويقينا لنا بحصول ذلك العلم المشتمل على جميع الجواب من غير أن يكون في ذلك العلم تفصيل لصور الأشياء التي تنتقش في نفوسنا حين أخذنا في الجواب، ثم يأتي التفصيل مع الأخذ في الجواب مرتبا ، وليس ذلك استعدادًا بحثا ويقينا بالاستعداد، بل يقينًا بحصول العلم لا بالاستعداد، وإنما يتيقن المعلوم لا المجهول، وأما علم العقل الأول بالباري تعالى فلا يكون لازما لعلمه بذاته، فإن مبدأه قبل ذاته لا لازما عنه، فلا يكون العلم بما هو قبل ذاته لازما من نفس علمه بذاته، فيكون علمًا آخر على حدة، والمعلوم ليس نفس العالم ولا لازما منه، فيكون زائدا لا محالة على نفس العالم، فتحصل فيه كثرة بسبب هذا العلم.
Ibn Sina said: The Lord, exalted, is knowledgeable of existents, but His knowledge of them is a necessary (luzumi) knowledge from His knowledge of His essence, not detailed in images. For He knows His essence, and He knows His essence as it is, namely that it is the principle of all existents entirely. So His knowledge of existents enters under His knowledge of His essence, without there being arranged for existents an image in His essence such that multiplicity would follow from it. For knowledge of concomitants of a thing, when it is not directed towards those concomitants intentionally, but is obtained from knowledge of their being necessary, is not additional to the knowledge of the thing itself, nor is there in it an ordered multiplicity; rather its ordering is according to the ordering of those concomitants. And we find in ourselves such knowledge of many things without there being for them distinct images in its essence. This is when we are asked about a question that we have known and mastered, but we are then heedless of it. So upon being asked about it, we find in ourselves an attention to its answer and certainty of obtaining that knowledge which encompasses the entire answer, without there being in that knowledge a detailed listing of the images of things that are imprinted in our souls when we begin the answer. Then the detail comes with the beginning of the answer in an ordered way. And that is not a preparedness for seeking and certainty of preparedness, but rather certainty of obtaining knowledge, not of preparedness. For one is certain of the known, not the unknown. As for the knowledge of the First Intellect of the Creator, exalted, it is not necessary from its knowledge of its essence, because its principle is prior to its essence, not necessary from it. So knowledge of what is prior to its essence is not necessary from its own knowledge of its essence. Thus it is another knowledge separately. And the known is neither the knower nor necessary from it, so it is additional without exception to the knower itself, and multiplicity results in it due to this knowledge.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's positive account of divine knowledge. He asserts that God knows existents, but His knowledge of them is 'necessary' (luzumi), meaning it follows necessarily from His knowledge of His own essence without requiring distinct forms or images. God knows Himself as the principle (mabda') of all existents, so knowledge of everything else is implicitly contained in that self-knowledge. Knowledge of concomitants (lawaazim) of a thing, when not directed intentionally at those concomitants but obtained through the knowledge that they are necessary, is not additional to the knowledge of the thing itself and does not introduce multiplicity. Ibn Sina illustrates this with a human analogy: when we have fully mastered a topic but are not thinking about it, upon being asked a question we immediately have certainty of the answer without detailed images; the detailed articulation comes only when we begin to answer. This shows that undetailed knowledge is possible without multiplicity. Finally, he distinguishes the case of the First Intellect: its knowledge of God is not necessary from its knowledge of its own essence, because God is prior to it and not a necessary concomitant. Therefore, the First Intellect has an additional knowledge that does cause multiplicity in it.

Key Terms
علم لزومي
لوازم
مبدأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's knowledge of many things necessitates many distinct forms in His essence.
Correction: Ibn Sina argues that necessary knowledge from self-knowledge avoids this, as knowledge of concomitants is implicit and not additional.

Checkpoint
How does Ibn Sina describe God's knowledge of existents?
A) Detailed images (suwar) are present in God's essence.
B) It is a necessary (luzumi) knowledge from His knowledge of His essence, without detailed forms.
C) It is a separate knowledge for each existent.
D) It is passive (infi'ali) knowledge received from things.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of all things is a necessary consequence of His self-knowledge, requiring no additional forms and thus avoiding multiplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge of you is a necessary consequence of His self-knowledge, not a separate thought, how does that confront your assumption that you are an independent reality? Do you truly see yourself as a mere concomitant of the Necessary Being?
?
Ibn Sina argues that knowledge of concomitants does not add multiplicity to God's essence. What does that imply about your own attachment to the multiplicity of your desires and sins? Are you clinging to an illusion of independence that contradicts this reality?
?
Since God's knowledge of all things is necessary and not acquired, what does that say about your attempts to hide anything from Him? Are you living as if your actions are outside His necessary and all-encompassing knowledge?

The Human Analogy of Undetailed Knowledge
Ibn Sina provides a human analogy to illustrate undetailed knowledge without multiplicity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's human analogy for undetailed knowledge and how it illustrates the possibility of knowing many things without distinct mental forms.

Core Idea
Undetailed knowledge is possible, as when we are certain of an answer without having the details in mind.

To make his account of divine knowledge more plausible, Ibn Sina offers a striking human example: the experience of being asked a question we have fully mastered. At first, we have a global certainty without detailed images; the details only come when we begin to articulate the answer. This analogy helps us grasp how God's knowledge can be undetailed yet comprehensive.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy passage
Read the passage where Ibn Sina describes the human experience of being asked a question we know well.
2
Identify the two stages
Notice the distinction between the initial certainty (undetailed) and the subsequent detailed articulation.
The initial knowledge is certain but not detailed; the detailed listing comes later.
3
Apply to divine knowledge
Reflect on how this analogy supports Ibn Sina's claim that God's knowledge of existents can be undetailed and without multiplicity.
Just as our undetailed knowledge does not contain distinct images, God's knowledge does not require forms.

Source Text

Knowledge of the First Intellect Causes Multiplicity
Ibn Sina distinguishes the First Intellect's knowledge as additional and productive of multiplicity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the First Intellect's knowledge of God is additional and causes multiplicity, contrasting with God's own knowledge.

Core Idea
The First Intellect's knowledge of God is not necessary from self-knowledge, so it adds multiplicity.

Having explained that God's knowledge of existents is necessary from self-knowledge and does not introduce multiplicity, Ibn Sina now addresses a potential problem: the First Intellect, which is the first created intellect. Its knowledge of God is not necessary from its self-knowledge, because God is prior to it and not a concomitant. Therefore, this knowledge is additional and causes multiplicity in the First Intellect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the First Intellect
Read the final part of the source text where Ibn Sina discusses the knowledge of the First Intellect.
2
Understand why it is not necessary from self-knowledge
Notice that the principle (God) is prior to the First Intellect, not a concomitant, so knowledge of God does not follow necessarily from knowledge of itself.
This is the key difference from God's own knowledge.
3
Recognize the consequence: multiplicity
Since the knowledge is additional to the knower, it introduces multiplicity in the First Intellect.
This shows that only God's knowledge is free from multiplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابن سينا الرب تعالى عالم بالموجودات، ولكن علمه بها علم لزومي عن علمه بذاته غير مفصل للصور، فإنه يعلم ذاته، وإنما يعلم ذاته كما هو عليه وهو أنه مبدأ للموجودات كلها بأسرها، فيدخل علمه بالموجودات تحت علمه بذاته من غير أن تترتب للموجودات صورة في ذاته حتى يلزم منه كثرة، فإن العلم بلوازم الشيء إذا لم يكن متجها نحو تلك اللوازم قصدًا، بل حاصلا من العلم بلزومها فلا يكون زائدا على نفس العلم بالملزوم، ولا فيه كثرة مترتبة فترتبه حسب ترتيب تلك اللوازم ، ونحن نجد من أنفسنا مثل هذا العلم بالأشياء الكثيرة من غير أن يتمايز لها صورة في ذاتها، وهو أنا إذا سألنا عن مسألة نكون قد علمناها وأتقناها، لكنا في تلك معرضون عنها، فمع السؤال عنها نجد من أنفسنا التفاتا إلى جوابها ويقينا لنا بحصول ذلك العلم المشتمل على جميع الجواب من غير أن يكون في ذلك العلم تفصيل لصور الأشياء التي تنتقش في نفوسنا حين أخذنا في الجواب، ثم يأتي التفصيل مع الأخذ في الجواب مرتبا ، وليس ذلك استعدادًا بحثا ويقينا بالاستعداد، بل يقينًا بحصول العلم لا بالاستعداد، وإنما يتيقن المعلوم لا المجهول، وأما علم العقل الأول بالباري تعالى فلا يكون لازما لعلمه بذاته، فإن مبدأه قبل ذاته لا لازما عنه، فلا يكون العلم بما هو قبل ذاته لازما من نفس علمه بذاته، فيكون علمًا آخر على حدة، والمعلوم ليس نفس العالم ولا لازما منه، فيكون زائدا لا محالة على نفس العالم، فتحصل فيه كثرة بسبب هذا العلم.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If even the First Intellect's knowledge of God is additional and introduces multiplicity, how do you react to the fact that your own knowledge of God is also fragmented and layered? Are you content with that multiplicity, or do you yearn for a knowledge of God that is simple and direct like His own? What would it take to move from additional knowledge to a more immediate awareness?
?
The passage says the known (God) is neither the knower nor a necessary concomitant of it. How does that challenge any assumption you might have that your concept of God is somehow identical to God Himself, or that your understanding flows necessarily from your essence? Are you willing to admit that your knowledge of God is always secondary and deficient?
?
Consider that your knowledge of God comes from a cause prior to you (God Himself), yet it is not necessary from your self. Does your prayer and worship reflect an acknowledgment that your knowledge is a gift, not a possession? Do you humbly receive or arrogantly claim? How can you cultivate a posture of receiving rather than possessing?

Classification of Intellects and Their Modes of Knowledge
Classification of intellects by mode of knowledge (form vs. impression)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how different intellects (from the First Intellect to God) are distinguished by their mode of knowledge: form (rasm), impression (irtisām), conception (taṣawwur), and depiction (taṣwīr).

Core Idea
Modes of knowledge vary: form, impression, conception, depiction — each intellect has its own.

In this passage, the author presents a detailed classification of intellects based on how they know. He introduces two key distinctions: between 'form' and 'impression', and between 'conception' and 'depiction'. Understanding this classification helps clarify the nature of different intellects, from the First Intellect to God's own self-knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the rhythm and the pairs of terms.
The passage is dense but short.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the two main pairs: rasm/irtisām (form/impression) and taṣawwur/taṣwīr (conception/depiction). Understand that they are distinct modes.
These terms are central to the classification.
3
Map each intellect to its mode
List each intellect mentioned (First Intellect, intellect of intellect and soul, human intellects, separate intellects, God) and note which mode(s) apply.
Use the guided breakdown below to help.
4
Reflect on God's unique self-knowledge
Observe that God's intellect of Himself is neither conception nor depiction. What does this imply about His transcendence?
This is a key theological point.
5
Consider why this classification matters
Think about how this hierarchy of intellects and modes of knowledge fits into broader cosmological and theological frameworks.
This connects to discussions about emanation and divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للعقل الأول رسم وليس بارتسام وعقله لذاته ليس برسم ولا ارتسام، وعقل العقل والنفس له ارتسام وليس برسم، فعقولنا مثلا تصورات وعقول المقارنات تصويرات وتصورات وعقل الباري تعالى لذاته ليس بتصور ولا تصوير، وعقله للعقل الأول تصوير لا تصور.
The First Intellect has a form but not by impression, and its intellect of itself is neither by form nor by impression. The intellect of the intellect and the soul has impression and not form. For example, our intellects are conceptions (taṣawwurāt) and the intellects of the separate [intellects] are both depictings (taṣwīrāt) and conceptions. The intellect of the Exalted Lord of Himself is neither conception nor depiction, and His intellect of the First Intellect is depiction not conception.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage classifies the modes of intellection across different levels of intellect. The author distinguishes between 'form' (rasm) and 'impression' (irtisām). The First Intellect (al-ʿaql al-awwal) has a form but not by way of impression. Its intellection of itself is neither form nor impression. The intellect of the intellect and the soul (i.e., the human intellect in relation to the active intellect?) has impression but not form. Our human intellects are 'conceptions' (taṣawwurāt), while the intellects of the separate intellects (al-muqāranāt) are both depictings (taṣwīrāt) and conceptions. God's intellect of Himself is neither conception nor depiction; His intellect of the First Intellect is depiction but not conception. This establishes a hierarchy of cognitive modes, with God's self-knowledge being unique.

Key Terms
العقل الأول
رسم
ارتسام
تصور
تصوير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'form' and 'impression' are synonymous, or that 'conception' and 'depiction' mean the same thing.
Correction: The author carefully distinguishes them as different modes of knowledge, as seen in the varying assignments to intellects.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what mode of knowledge does God have of Himself?
A) Conception
B) Depiction
C) Neither conception nor depiction
D) Both

Key Takeaway
Modes of knowledge vary across intellects: form and impression, conception and depiction, with God's self-knowledge being unique.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You conceive of God daily in your prayers and thoughts. After learning that His self-knowledge is beyond conception and depiction, are you content with a conceptual God, or do you feel a longing for a direct, ineffable awareness? What would it take for you to seek that deeper knowledge?
?
The First Intellect knows itself without form or impression. Have you ever tried to observe your own consciousness without any mental image or concept? Try it now for one minute. What did you discover about your own mode of knowing yourself, and how does it compare to the hierarchy in this lesson?

Ibn Sina on God's Knowledge: Necessary from Self-Knowledge
Ibn Sina states that God's knowledge of existents is a necessary concomitant of His self-knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's concept of necessary knowledge (ʿilm luzūmī) and how God's knowledge of all existents follows necessarily from His self-knowledge without multiplicity.

Core Idea
God knows all things by knowing Himself, not by adding separate forms.

This lesson explores a key doctrine of Ibn Sina on divine knowledge. Ibn Sina argues that God's knowledge of existents is not a separate act but a necessary concomitant of His self-knowledge. He uses an analogy from human knowledge to show how comprehensive knowledge can be present without detailed forms until needed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without analyzing. Let Ibn Sina's own argument come through before interpreting.
Pay special attention to the term 'علم لزومي'.
2
Identify the concept of 'necessary knowledge'
Notice how Ibn Sina describes God's knowledge as arising from self-knowledge. Understand that 'luzūmī' means it is a necessary consequence, not an acquired or distinct act.
3
Analyze the human analogy
Consider the example of knowing an answer without having details in mind. How does this illustrate that knowledge can be comprehensive without being detailed? Relate this to God's knowledge.
The analogy is meant to show that multiplicity is not required for comprehensive knowledge.
4
Reflect on the theological point
Why does Ibn Sina emphasize that no multiplicity results? What does this imply about God's simplicity and the nature of His knowledge?
This doctrine guards against attributing change or plurality in God's essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابن سينا الرب تعالى عالم بالموجودات، ولكن علمه بها علم لزومي عن علمه بذاته غير مفصل للصور، فإنه يعلم ذاته، وإنما يعلم ذاته كما هو عليه وهو أنه مبدأ للموجودات كلها بأسرها، فيدخل علمه بالموجودات تحت علمه بذاته من غير أن تترتب للموجودات صورة في ذاته حتى يلزم منه كثرة، فإن العلم بلوازم الشيء إذا لم يكن متجها نحو تلك اللوازم قصدًا، بل حاصلا من العلم بلزومها فلا يكون زائدا على نفس العلم بالملزوم، ولا فيه كثرة مترتبة فترتبه حسب ترتيب تلك اللوازم، ونحن نجد من أنفسنا مثل هذا العلم بالأشياء الكثيرة من غير أن يتمايز لها صورة في ذاتها، وهو أنا إذا سألنا عن مسألة نكون قد علمناها وأتقناها، لكنا في تلك معرضون عنها، فمع السؤال عنها نجد من أنفسنا التفاتا إلى جوابها ويقينا لنا بحصول ذلك العلم المشتمل على جميع الجواب من غير أن يكون في ذلك العلم تفصيل لصور الأشياء التي تنتقش في نفوسنا حين أخذنا في الجواب، ثم يأتي التفصيل مع الأخذ في الجواب مرتبا، وليس ذلك استعدادا بحثا ويقينا بالاستعداد، بل يقينا بحصول العلم لا بالاستعداد، وإنما يتيقن المعلوم لا المجهول.
Ibn Sina said: The Lord, exalted, is knowledgeable of existents, but His knowledge of them is a necessary knowledge (ʿilm luzūmī) from His knowledge of His essence, not detailed in terms of forms. For He knows His essence, and He only knows His essence as it is, and it is that He is the principle of all existents entirely. So His knowledge of existents enters under His knowledge of His essence without forms of existents being arranged in His essence such that multiplicity would result. For knowledge of the concomitants of a thing, when it is not intentionally directed towards those concomitants but rather arises from knowledge of their being necessary, is not additional to the very knowledge of the necessitating thing, nor is there in it arranged multiplicity; its arrangement is according to the arrangement of those concomitants. We find in ourselves an example of such knowledge of many things without their forms being distinct in its essence. That is, when we are asked a question that we already know and have mastered, but we are at the time unmindful of it, then upon the question we find in ourselves a turning to its answer and a certainty for us of the occurrence of that knowledge which comprises the entire answer, without there being in that knowledge a detail of the forms of things that become imprinted in our souls when we begin to answer. Then detail comes with beginning to answer in an ordered manner. That is not a preparedness seeking and certainty of preparedness, but rather a certainty of the occurrence of knowledge, not of preparedness. Only the known is certain, not the unknown.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's view on divine knowledge. The author quotes Ibn Sina to explain that God's knowledge of existents is 'necessary knowledge' (ʿilm luzūmī) derived from His self-knowledge. God knows His own essence, and since His essence is the principle of all existents, knowledge of them follows automatically without producing multiplicity in God's essence. The author then provides an analogy from human experience: when we know something thoroughly but are not actively thinking about it, upon being asked we immediately turn to the answer with certainty, without having detailed forms present in our mind beforehand. The detail arises only when we begin to answer. This illustrates how knowledge can be comprehensive without being detailed, supporting Ibn Sina's claim that God's knowledge is not detailed with separate forms.

Key Terms
علم لزومي
ذاته
مبدأ
لوازم
كثرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's knowledge being 'necessary' means it is passive or external.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that it arises from His self-knowledge and is not passive; it is an active necessary concomitant.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina as presented, God's knowledge of existents is:
A) Detailed and distinct for each existent
B) A necessary consequence of His self-knowledge
C) Acquired through sense perception
D) Multiple and increasing

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of all things is a necessary consequence of His self-knowledge, without adding multiplicity to His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe God knows every thought and action, yet do you live as if His knowledge is a necessary concomitant of His essence—meaning He knows without needing to 'find out'—or do you act as if He is unaware of your hidden sins? This week, will you repent of one sin you have been hiding because you know God already knows it?
?
Ibn Sina describes human knowledge that is certain without being actively detailed. When was the last time you were absolutely certain of a truth from God—like His mercy or justice—but it was not at the forefront of your mind? How will you make that knowledge active in your heart today, not just intellectual, so it changes your choices?
?
If God's knowledge of you comes from His own essence, He knows you more intimately than you know yourself. Does that comfort or frighten you? Are you willing to let His knowledge of you—not your self-image—define who you truly are? Write down one area where you have been avoiding His gaze and commit to bringing it before Him in prayer.

The Objection: Knowledge Must Be Detailed
Objection to necessary knowledge as non-detailed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objector argues that God's knowledge cannot be both necessary and non-detailed, and why knowledge is always detailed.

Core Idea
Knowledge, by definition, relates to its object as it is — thus it must be detailed.

The objector challenges the distinction between essential and necessary knowledge. He asks whether 'necessary knowledges' are a single summary or many, and insists that knowledge is inherently detailed. This forces us to clarify what we mean by God's knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Note how the objector poses two alternatives for 'necessary knowledges'.
2
Identify the core argument
The objector states that knowledge is never anything but detailed. Find that sentence and understand its implication.
This challenges the idea of non-detailed knowledge.
3
Reflect on the challenge
Ask yourself: if knowledge must be detailed, how can God's knowledge be one and simple? This sets up the response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلت: فرقت فرقًا بين علمه بذاته وبين علمه بالأشياء حتى سميت الأول ذاتيا . والثاني علما لزوميا ، أفتعني بالعلوم اللزومية معلومات له بعلم واحد على طريق فذلك أو تعني بذلك علومًا أخر لزومية لعلمه بذاته، فما المتصور صحيح بالعلوم؟ وما محلها ؟ وكيف تتعلق هي بالمعلومات؟ وما معنى أنها غير مفصلة للصور؟ والعلم لا يكون قط إلا مفصلا ، فإنه معنى يتعلق بالمعلوم على ما هو به ، فهو مفصل بالنسبة إلى معلومه ، وقولك : إنه يعلم ذاته كما هو عليه، وهو مبدأ للموجودات، فيا عجبًا من إطلاق الذات والعلم بالذات والمبدأ للموجودات، فإن كانت هذه الثلاثة الاعتبارات عبارة عن معبر واحد حتى يقال : ذاته علمه وعلمه هو مبدئيته وكونه مبدأ أمر إضافي، فكونه ذاتاً وعلما يجب أن يكون أمراً إضافيا أوإن كان ثم اعتبار واعتبار حتى يكون من حيث إنه مبدأ إضافيا ، ومن حيث إنه علم سلبيا، ومن حيث إنه ذات لا سلبيا ولا إضافيًا فقد تعددت اعتبارات ثلاثة ، فذاته ثالث ثلاثة. ونقول : ما المانع من تعلق العلم الأزلي بالمعلومات على نسق واحد حتى يكون منه ما هو بالقصد الأول وهو العلم بالملزوم ، ولا منه ما هو بالقصد الثاني وهو العلم باللازم ؟ إن كان العلم والذات لا يتأثر ولا يتكثر باللوازم والسلوب حتى يقال : معنى كونه عالما أنه ( لَا تَخْفَىٰ عَلَيْهِ شَيْءٌ فِي الْأَرْضِ وَلَا فِي السَّمَاءِ (آل عمران: ٥)
I said: You made a distinction between His knowledge of His essence and His knowledge of things, calling the first 'essential' (dhātī) and the second 'necessary knowledge' (ʿilm luzūmī). Do you mean by 'necessary knowledges' that He has information by one knowledge in the manner of a summary, or do you mean by that other knowledges that are necessary concomitants of His knowledge of His essence? What is the correct conception of those knowledges? What is their locus? How do they relate to the known things? What is the meaning that they are not detailed in forms? Knowledge is never anything but detailed, for it is a meaning that relates to the known as it is, so it is detailed in relation to its known. And your saying that He knows His essence as it is, and it is the principle of existents - oh marvel at the application of 'essence', 'knowledge of essence', and 'principle of existents'! If these three considerations are expressions of one meaning, such that it is said: His essence is His knowledge and His knowledge is His being a principle - and being a principle is a relative matter - then His being essence and knowledge must be relative. Or if there is one consideration and another, such that from the aspect of being a principle it is relative, from the aspect of being knowledge it is negative, and from the aspect of being essence it is neither negative nor relative - then there are three considerations; so His essence is a third of three. And we say: What prevents eternal knowledge from relating to known things in one pattern such that some of it is by first intention, which is knowledge of the necessitating thing, and some of it is by second intention, which is knowledge of the necessary concomitant? If knowledge and essence are not affected and do not multiply by concomitants and negations, such that it is said: the meaning of His being knowing is that 'nothing is hidden from Him in the earth or in the heaven' (Quran 3:5)?
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and the beginning of a response. The objector (probably the author adopting a dialectical stance) questions the distinction made between God's self-knowledge (essential) and His knowledge of things (necessary). The objector asks for clarification: does 'necessary knowledges' mean multiple pieces of information within one knowledge, or separate knowledges? He argues that knowledge is always detailed, contradicting the claim that it is not detailed. He also criticizes the application of 'essence', 'knowledge', and 'principle' to God, noting that if they are one meaning, then essence and knowledge are relative; if they are distinct, then God's essence becomes multiple. The response ('we say') begins by asking what prevents eternal knowledge from being one pattern with different intentions, and references the Quranic verse that nothing is hidden from God. The response is incomplete but defends the coherence of God's knowledge being both comprehensive and not causing multiplicity.

Key Terms
ذاتي
علم لزومي
فذلك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the objector, why must knowledge be detailed?
A) because God's knowledge is infinite
B) because knowledge relates to its object as it is
C) because the Quran says so
D) because it is necessary

Key Takeaway
The objector insists that knowledge must be detailed, challenging the notion of non-detailed divine knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector insists that knowledge must be detailed—it relates to the known exactly as it is. When you reflect on your knowledge of Allah's attributes, do you settle for a vague 'summary' understanding, or have you pursued the detailed reality that this lesson demands? What specific attribute will you study in depth starting this week?
?
You believe Allah knows every detail of your life. But do you live as if He knows only a summary? How does the truth that knowledge is inherently detailed expose the gap between your belief and your actions? What one area of your daily life will you hold to account because Allah knows it in full detail?
?
The objection here is not merely academic—it challenges the very nature of what it means to know. In your own pursuit of religious knowledge, are you content with the surface, or do you dig deep until every concept is 'detailed' in your heart? What excuse have you been using to avoid that depth, and will you abandon it now?

The Problem of Multiple Considerations and the Response
Objection about essence-knowledge-principle and response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection regarding the three considerations (essence, knowledge, principle) and the beginning of the response using the concepts of first and second intention.

Core Idea
God's essence, knowledge, and principle are not separate entities but aspects of one reality.

The objector then turns to a new problem: if we distinguish essence, knowledge, and principle, we seem to multiply God's essence. The response begins by asking what prevents God's eternal knowledge from relating to things in one pattern with different intentions, and cites a Quranic verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection about three considerations
Read the Arabic passage from 'وقولك' to 'ثالث ثلاثة'. Notice how the objector presents a dilemma: either all three are one meaning (making essence relative) or they are distinct (making essence multiple).
2
Analyze the dilemma
The objector says if the three considerations are expressions of one meaning, then essence becomes relative; if they are different, then essence is a third of three. Understand why this is problematic.
3
Examine the response
Read the response beginning with 'ونقول'. The responder asks: what prevents eternal knowledge from being one pattern with first and second intention? Consider what 'first intention' and 'second intention' mean.
First intention = knowledge of the main thing; second intention = knowledge of its necessary concomitant.
4
Connect to the Quranic verse
The response references the verse 'nothing is hidden from Him'. This supports the idea that God's knowledge encompasses all without multiplicity.

Source Text

Objection on Distinguishing God's Knowledge
Author interrogates the distinction between essential and necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's critique of distinguishing between God's essential and necessary knowledge, and the implications for divine simplicity.

Core Idea
Distinguishing aspects of God's knowledge leads to contradictions about divine simplicity.

This passage is a sharp interrogation by the author against an opponent who tries to differentiate between God's knowledge of His essence and His knowledge of things. The author exposes the problem: if these are different types, what are they? If they are the same, then relationality infects the essence. The passage is a defense of divine simplicity and the unity of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the interrogative tone: the author is challenging the opponent.
2
Identify the two options presented
The author asks if the 'necessary knowledges' are a single knowledge in summary or multiple knowledges. Pause to consider what each option would entail.
The term 'فذلك' (summary) is key here.
3
Analyze the claim about knowledge
The author states that knowledge is always detailed because it relates to the object as it is. Reflect on how this challenges the idea of a summary knowledge.
This is a crucial point about the nature of knowledge.
4
Understand the dilemma about the three considerations
The author presents a dilemma: if essence, knowledge, and principle are the same, they become relational; if distinct, the essence becomes one of three. Consider why both options are problematic for the opponent.
This exposes a contradiction in the opponent's position.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلت: فرقت فرقًا بين علمه بذاته وبين علمه بالأشياء حتى سميت الأول ذاتيا والثاني علما لزوميا، أفتعني بالعلوم اللزومية معلومات له بعلم واحد على طريق فذلك أو تعني بذلك علومًا أخر لزومية لعلمه بذاته، فما المتصور صحيح اللزوم بتلك العلوم؟ وما محلها ؟ وكيف تتعلق هي بالمعلومات؟ وما معنى أنها غير مفصلة للصور؟ والعلم لا يكون قط إلا مفصلا ، فإنه معنى يتعلق بالمعلوم على ما هو به ، فهو مفصل بالنسبة إلى معلومه ، وقولك : إنه يعلم ذاته كما هو عليه، وهو مبدأ للموجودات، فيا عجبًا من إطلاق الذات والعلم بالذات والمبدأ للموجودات، فإن كانت هذه الثلاثة الاعتبارات عبارة عن معبر واحد حتى يقال : ذاته علمه وعلمه هو مبدئيته وكونه مبدأ أمر إضافي، فكونه ذاتاً وعلما يجب أن يكون أمراً إضافيا أوإن كان ثم اعتبار واعتبار حتى يكون من حيث إنه مبدأ إضافيا ، ومن حيث إنه علم سلبيا، ومن حيث إنه ذات لا سلبيا ولا إضافيًا فقد تعددت اعتبارات ثلاثة ، فذاته ثالث ثلاثة.
I said: You made a distinction between His knowledge of His essence and His knowledge of things, such that you called the first essential and the second necessary knowledge. Do you mean by necessary knowledge that He knows things with one knowledge in a summary way, or do you mean thereby other knowledges that are necessary for His knowledge of His essence? For what is the correct conception of the necessity of those knowledges? And what is their locus? And how do they relate to the known things? And what is the meaning that they are not detailed of forms? For knowledge is never except detailed: it is a meaning that relates to the known as it is, so it is detailed relative to its known. And your statement: He knows His essence as it is, and He is the principle of existents. So how wonderful is the attribution of essence, knowledge of essence, and principality of existents. For if these three considerations are a single expression such that it is said: His essence is His knowledge, and His knowledge is His principality, and His being a principle is an relational matter, then His being essence and knowledge must be relational. Or if there is consideration after consideration such that from the aspect that He is principle it is relational, from the aspect that He is knowledge it is negative, and from the aspect that He is essence it is neither negative nor relational, then there have become three considerations, so His essence is the third of three.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is an objection and interrogation by the author directed at an opponent who distinguishes between God's knowledge of His own essence (called essential knowledge) and His knowledge of other things (called necessary knowledge). The author asks: Do you mean that the necessary knowledges are information known by a single knowledge in a summary fashion, or do you mean additional knowledges that necessarily follow from His knowledge of His essence? He then challenges the opponent on the nature of knowledge: knowledge is always detailed, since it is a relation to the object as it is. The author then points out a contradiction: the opponent says God knows His essence as it is, and that He is the principle of existents. If the three aspects (essence, knowledge, principle) are the same, then even the essence and knowledge become relational, which compromises their absoluteness. If they are distinct considerations (relational for principle, negative for knowledge, absolute for essence), then essence becomes one of three distinct things, which implies multiplicity in God. This guards against anthropomorphic and composite conceptions of the divine.

Key Terms
علم ذاتي
علم لزومي
فذلك
اعتبار
مبدأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Distinguishing aspects of God's knowledge (essential vs necessary) is a valid way to talk about God's attributes.
Correction: The author shows that such a distinction either collapses or leads to multiplicity, challenging the opponent's position.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue about knowledge being detailed?
A) Knowledge is sometimes detailed and sometimes not.
B) Knowledge is always detailed because it relates to the object as it is.
C) Knowledge is always summary.
D) Knowledge is only detailed in creatures.

Key Takeaway
The author demonstrates that any attempt to separate God's knowledge of essence from knowledge of things leads to logical contradictions, upholding the unity and simplicity of divine attributes.

What will you do the next time you catch yourself compartmentalizing God's attributes?
?

Commit to examining one attribute of God today and ask yourself: does my conception of it truly uphold His absolute simplicity?
?

Eternal Knowledge Relates to All Things in One Manner
Author defends that eternal knowledge embraces all objects uniformly

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that God's eternal knowledge encompasses all things in a single uniform manner, without any distinction of primary or secondary objects and without change in His essence.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is one, relating to all things uniformly without change.

This passage addresses a key objection against the unity of divine knowledge. The author argues that there is no reason to assume that eternal knowledge must be divided into primary and secondary objects. Instead, all objects of knowledge are known in one and the same act, without any alteration in God's essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to understand the overall argument.
Notice the rhetorical question at the start.
2
Identify the rhetorical question
The author asks: 'What prevents eternal knowledge from relating to objects in a single manner?' This sets up the refutation of a potential objection.
3
Understand 'first intention' and 'second intention'
The author mentions that some knowledge could be by first intention (knowledge of the entailing cause) and some by second intention (knowledge of the entailment). He argues this distinction does not apply to God.
These are logical concepts borrowed from philosophy.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion: God's knowledge is not multiplied or changed by the multiplicity of known objects, just as His essence is not multiplied by entailments.
The Quranic verse confirms that nothing is hidden from God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقول : ما المانع من تعلق العلم الأزلي بالمعلومات على نسق واحد حتى يكون منه ما هو بالقصد الأول وهو العلم بالملزوم، ومنه ما هو بالقصد الثاني وهو العلم باللازم ؟ إن كان العلم والذات لا يتأثر ولا يتكثر باللوازم والسلوب حتى يقال : معنى كونه عالما أنه لا تخفى عليه شيء في الأرض ولا في السماء [آل عمران: 5] من غير أن ينسب إليه بعض المعلومات بالذات، والبعض بالعرض من غير أن تتغير ذات العالم بالمعلوم، كما لم تتكثر ذاته بتكثر اللوازم.
And we say: What prevents the eternal knowledge from relating to objects in a single manner, such that some of it is by first intention—that is, knowledge of the entailer—and some of it is by second intention—that is, knowledge of the entailer? Since knowledge and essence are not affected nor multiplied by entailments and negations such that it is said: The meaning of His being knowing is that nothing in the earth nor in the heaven is hidden from Him [Al Imran: 5], without attributing to Him some objects as essential and some as accidental, and without the essence of the knower changing by the known, just as His essence is not multiplied by the multiplicity of entailments.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage presents an argument in response to the previous objection. The author asks: What prevents eternal knowledge from relating to all known things uniformly, so that some of it is directly about the entailing cause and some about the entailment (by second intention)? The point is that God's knowledge is one, covering all things, without distinguishing some as essential and some as accidental. The author cites the verse that nothing is hidden from God. He emphasizes that the knower's essence does not change with the known, just as the essence is not multiplied by the multiplicity of things that necessarily follow from it. The key is that divine knowledge is not subject to multiplicity or change.

Divine Attributes: Distinguished by Perfection, Not Process
Author explains that distinction of attributes is based on human potential for ignorance; attributes are by perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine attributes are attributed to God by their perfect state (كمال) rather than by any process or beginning (مبدأ), and that distinctions among attributes exist only in our minds due to human limitations.

Core Idea
God's attributes are defined by perfection, not process.

This passage tackles a subtle theological point: why do we distinguish between God's knowledge, power, and mercy? The author argues that such distinctions are only in our minds, because we are prone to ignorance and forgetfulness. In God, attributes are described by their perfect form, not by the steps we humans take. Let's see how the author builds this argument with concrete examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation together. Notice the key terms مبدأ (beginning) and كمال (perfection).
2
Identify the contrast between مبدأ and كمال
The author repeatedly contrasts the 'beginning' of an attribute (how it arises in humans) with its 'perfection' (the ideal state). See how this applies to knowledge, power, and mercy.
3
Understand the examples
For each attribute, note what the 'beginning' is for humans and what the 'perfection' is. For knowledge: beginning is inference, perfection is clarity. For power: beginning is exertion, perfection is ability. For mercy: beginning is emotional confinement, perfection is beneficence.
This shows that in God, only the perfect aspect applies.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that if attributes are understood this way, then there is no real distinction in God between universal and particular knowledge—any division we impose is a mental construct.
This guards against thinking of God as having multiple, separate faculties.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this principle prevents us from anthropomorphizing God's attributes. The multiplicity of names does not imply multiplicity in essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما التمييز في عقولنا إنما هو بحسب إمكان الجهل والغفلة والنسيان وإلا فلو قدرنا علمًا لم يطر عليه ضده البتة لم يخف عليه شيء البتة، ولعلمنا مباد وكمالات فمبادئها النظر والاستدلال والتفكير والتدبير وكماله أنه لا يخفى علينا المنظور فيه، وإنما يطلق العلم على الباري تعالى بحسب الكمال لا بحسب المبدأ ، كما أن مبدأ أفعالنا المعالجة والمزاولة والاكتساب والمجاهدة، وكمالها بأن لا يتعذر علينا شيء، وتطلق القدرة على الباري تعالى بحسب الكمال لا بحسب المبدأ، وكما أن العفو والعطف منا بحسب المبدأ هو انحصار القلب على حال المعطوف عليه، وبحسب الكمال هو الإنعام والملاحظة والإكرام والملاطفة، فإذا كان هذا معنى الصفات، فلا فرق في تلك النسبة وهو [أن لا] يخفى عليه شيء من الكلي والجزئي، والذاتي والعرضي، وإذا فرقتم بين قسم وقسم فقد حكمتم بتعدد الاعتبار وتكثر الجهات والآثار.
As for the distinction in our minds, it is only according to the possibility of ignorance, heedlessness, and forgetfulness. Otherwise, if we suppose a knowledge that its opposite never befell, nothing would be hidden from it at all. We know beginnings and perfections: their beginnings are consideration, inference, thinking, and deliberation, and their perfection is that the object of consideration is not hidden from us. Knowledge is attributed to God, exalted, according to perfection, not according to beginning. Similarly, the beginning of our actions is treatment, exertion, acquisition, and struggle, and their perfection is that nothing is impossible for us. Power is attributed to God, exalted, according to perfection, not according to beginning. And just as forgiveness and affection from us, according to beginning, is the confinement of the heart to the state of the one shown affection, and according to perfection is beneficence, attention, honor, and gentleness. If this is the meaning of attributes, then there is no difference in that relation—that nothing is hidden from Him, whether universal or particular, essential or accidental. And if you make a distinction between one division and another, you have judged the multiplicity of consideration and the multitude of aspects and effects.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage explains why we perceive distinctions among divine attributes. The author argues that such distinctions arise only because our minds are conditioned by the possibility of ignorance and forgetfulness. In contrast, God's knowledge is perfect and never subject to opposites. The author then introduces a key principle: attributes like knowledge and power are attributed to God in terms of perfection (كمال), not in terms of the process or beginning (مبدأ) that we experience. For example, human knowledge begins with inference and deliberation, but its perfection is clarity. Similarly, power's beginning is exertion, its perfection is ability. Likewise, mercy in us begins as a movement of the heart, but its perfection is beneficence. Therefore, when we say God knows, we mean He possesses the perfect form of knowledge without any deficiency. Consequently, there is no real distinction in God's knowledge between universal and particular, essential and accidental. If one divides these, one is imposing multiple considerations, which leads to multiplicity in God.

مبدأ
صفات

Misconception: One might think God's attributes are like human attributes, involving a process or beginning.
Correction: The author clarifies that divine attributes are attributed by perfection (كمال) alone, not by any beginning (مبدأ). God's knowledge, power, and mercy are perfect and not preceded by any process.

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes are named by their perfect state, not by any process; thus distinctions among them are only mental constructs due to human limitation.

Divine Attributes Are by Perfection, Not by Beginning
Analogical explanation that divine attributes are attributed by perfection, not by beginning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine attributes like knowledge and power are attributed to Allah according to their perfect state, not according to any process or beginning.

Core Idea
God's attributes are defined by their perfect end, not by human-like beginnings.

In this passage, the author uses analogies from human knowledge, power, and affection to clarify a crucial point about divine attributes. Human attributes have a beginning (like effort) and a perfection (like full ability). When we speak of God's attributes, we refer only to the perfect state, free from any deficiency. This passage helps us avoid comparing God's attributes to our own limited processes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Let the analogical structure come through.
Notice the three examples: knowledge, power, and pardon/affection.
2
Identify the beginning and perfection for each example
For knowledge, the beginning is reasoning and deliberation, perfection is complete awareness. For power, beginning is effort, perfection is ability. For affection, beginning is emotional bond, perfection is beneficence.
3
Understand the principle for divine attributes
The author states that 'knowledge' is applied to God only in terms of perfection, not beginning. The same for power and affection.
This is the key doctrinal point.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that if attributes are understood by perfection, then there is no distinction in God's knowledge—He knows all universals and particulars equally. Distinguishing would imply multiplicity in God.
Consider why this rejects anthropomorphism.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasp why divine attributes are not like human attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما التمييز في عقولنا إنما هو بحسب إمكان الجهل والغفلة والنسيان وإلا فلو قدرنا علمًا لم يطر عليه ضده البتة لم يخف عليه شيء البتة، ولعلمنا مباد وكمالات فمبادئها النظر والاستدلال والتفكير والتدبير وكماله أنه لا يخفى علينا المنظور فيه، وإنما يطلق العلم على الباري تعالى بحسب الكمال لا بحسب المبدأ، كما أن مبدأ أفعالنا المعالجة والمزاولة والاكتساب والمجاهدة، وكمالها بأن لا يتعذر علينا شيء، وتطلق القدرة على الباري تعالى بحسب الكمال لا بحسب المبدأ، وكما أن العفو والعطف منا بحسب المبدأ هو انحصار القلب على حال المعطوف عليه، وبحسب الكمال هو الإنعام والملاحظة والإكرام والملاطفة، فإذا كان هذا معنى الصفات، فلا فرق في تلك النسبة وهو [أن لا] يخفى عليه شيء من الكلي والجزئي، والذاتي والعرضي، وإذا فرقتم بين قسم وقسم فقد حكمتم بتعدد الاعتبار وتكثر الجهات والآثار.
As for distinction in our intellects, it is only according to the possibility of ignorance, heedlessness, and forgetfulness. Otherwise, if we were to conceive a knowledge that never has its opposite occur to it, nothing at all would be hidden from it. And we have beginnings and perfections: their beginnings are consideration, inference, thinking, and deliberation; and their perfection is that the thing considered is not hidden from us. And the term 'knowledge' is applied to the Most High God only in terms of perfection, not in terms of beginning. Just as the beginning of our actions is effort, exertion, acquisition, and striving, and their perfection is that nothing is impossible for us, and the term 'power' is applied to the Most High God in terms of perfection, not in terms of beginning. And just as pardon and affection from us, in terms of beginning, is the confinement of the heart to the state of the one shown affection, and in terms of perfection is beneficence, consideration, honor, and kindness. So if this is the meaning of attributes, then there is no difference in that relation—that nothing is hidden from Him of universal or particular, essential or accidental. And if you distinguish between one division and another, you have judged that there is multiplicity of consideration and plurality of aspects and effects.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is an analogical explanation of divine attributes. The author begins by stating that distinctions in human intellects arise only due to the possibility of ignorance, heedlessness, and forgetfulness. If we imagine a knowledge that never has its opposite (ignorance) occur to it, nothing would be hidden from it. The author then notes that human knowledge has beginnings (consideration, inference, thinking, deliberation) and perfections (the object is fully known). The term 'knowledge' is applied to God only according to perfection, not beginning—similarly for power and affection. For power, our beginning is effort and acquisition, perfection is ability; for affection, beginning is emotional bond, perfection is beneficence and kindness. The author concludes that attributes are attributed to God by perfection, so there is no difference in His knowledge of universal or particular, essential or accidental. Distinguishing between types of knowledge would imply multiplicity in God, which is denied.

Refuting Universal Knowledge Only
Refutation of the claim that God knows only universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that God knows only universals leads to logical contradictions and is therefore false.

Core Idea
Universals multiply with particulars; universal-only knowledge is impossible.

This passage presents a powerful refutation of the view that God knows things only in a universal, unchanging way. The author demonstrates that such a view leads to contradictions, proving that God must know each particular directly. Let's follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how the author begins by stating the opponent's view.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent says God knows things in a universal manner that does not admit change. Note this claim.
3
Follow the author's refutation
The author argues that each particular has its own specific universal. Therefore universals are multiple. If God knew only universals, His knowledge would either be multiple (contradicting divine simplicity) or collapse into knowing only one thing (absurd).
Trace the logical steps: premises → deduction → absurdity.
4
Conclude the argument
Since the opponent's view leads to absurdity, it must be false. The correct view is that God knows all particulars distinctly.
The passage cuts off, but the conclusion is clear.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument supports the orthodox Islamic position that Allah's knowledge encompasses every particular thing directly and without change.
This connects to divine omniscience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما من قال منهم : إنه يعلم الأمور على وجه كلي لا يتطرق إليه التغير. فنقول : كل موجود شخصي في هذا العالم يستدعي كليا خاصا بذلك الشخص، فإن كلية الشخص الإنساني وهو كونه إنسانًا ليس بكون كلية شخص حيوان آخر بل الكليات تتكثر بحسب تكثر الشخصيات، فإذا كان لا يعلم الشخصي إلا من نحو كليته حتى لا يتغير العلم بالكلية ويتغير العلم بالجزية، فيلزم أن يتكثر العلم بكليته كما يتكثر بشخصيته، وإن اجتمعت الكليات كلها في كل واحد فيلزم أن لا يكون المعلوم إلا ذلك الكلي الواحد، ثم ذلك الكلي الواحد لازم
And as for those among them who say: He knows things in a universal manner that does not admit change. We say: Every particular existent in this world requires a universal specific to that particular. For the universality of the human individual, which is his being human, is not the universality of another animal individual; rather, universals multiply according to the multiplicity of particulars. So if He knows the particular only through its universality, so that knowledge does not change with the universal and changes with the particular, then it follows that knowledge multiplies by its universality just as it multiplies by its particularity. And if all universals are combined in a single one, then it follows that the known is only that single universal, and then that single universal is necessary.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the view that God knows only universals without knowledge of particulars. The author argues that each particular existent requires a universal specific to that particular—the universality of a human is different from that of an animal. Therefore, universals are multiple. If God knows particulars only through their universals, His knowledge would still be multiple because each universal is distinct. If all universals were combined into one, then only one thing would be known, which is absurd. The argument is cut off but the conclusion is that God must know particulars directly.

Key Terms
الكلي
الشخصي
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God knows only universals, not particulars.
Correction: This passage shows that such a view leads to logical absurdities; rather, God knows each particular distinctly and directly.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what would happen if God knew only universals?
A) His knowledge would be simple and unchanging
B) His knowledge would still multiply because universals are multiple
C) He would know all particulars at once
D) The universals would collapse into one

Key Takeaway
The claim that God knows only universals is false because it leads to logical contradictions; God knows all particulars directly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Write down one area of your life where you need to be more conscious of Allah's detailed knowledge and take a specific step to change.

The Sifatiyya on Divine Knowledge and Time
Sifatiyya state that temporality is not intrinsic to knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sifatiyya's argument that knowledge as such is not temporal, and that the confusion about God's knowledge arises from projecting temporal categories onto it.

Core Idea
Knowledge in itself is disclosure, not time-bound.

The Sifatiyya (those who affirm divine attributes) encountered a common misunderstanding: that God's knowledge must change because events change. In this passage, they diagnose the root of that error and offer a corrective by distinguishing knowledge from temporality. This lesson unpacks their argument and helps you grasp a key principle in divine attribute theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice how the Sifatiyya begin by stating the problem that arises from imagining knowledge as temporal. Then read the translation to follow the reasoning.
Pay attention to the contrast between 'knowledge as knowledge' and 'knowledge as attribute of a temporal being'.
2
Identify the two aspects of knowledge
The passage distinguishes between knowledge as such (تبين وانكشاف) and knowledge as an attribute of a temporal being (إحاطة وإدراكًا). Understand the difference and why it matters.
This distinction is key to solving the problem of God's knowledge and time.
3
Reflect on the objection and resolution
Consider how this refutes the claim that God's knowledge changes. What is the Sifatiyya's underlying assumption about divine attributes? How does this relate to other theological schools?
Compare this with the Ash'ari or Mu'tazili views for deeper insight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الصفاتية : إن الإشكال في هذه المسألة على جميع المذاهب من جهة أنهم تصوروا تعلق العلم بالمعلوم على وجه يتطرق إليه الزمان الماضي والمستقبل والحال ، حتى يقال : علم ،ويعلم وهو عالم وسيعلم فظنوا أن العلم زماني يتغير بتغير الحوادث ، ومن تحقق أن العلم من حيث هو علم لا يستدعي زمانًا، بل هو في نفسه تبين وانكشاف، وذلك إذا كان صفة للحادث وإحاطة وإدراكًا إذا كان
The Sifatiyya said: The difficulty in this issue according to all schools is that they conceived the relation of knowledge to the known in a manner that admits past, future, and present time, such that it is said: 'He knew, He knows, He is knowing, He will know.' So they thought that knowledge is temporal, changing with the change of events. But whoever realizes that knowledge, as knowledge, does not require time, but rather is in itself disclosure and unveiling, and that is when it is an attribute of the temporal being, and encompassment and perception when it is
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a statement from the Sifatiyya (the affirmationists of divine attributes) diagnosing the root cause of the problem of divine knowledge in relation to time. They argue that all schools have mistakenly imagined that God's knowledge relates to known things in a way that involves past, present, and future time. This leads to expressions like 'He knew, He knows, He will know', which imply that God's knowledge changes with changing events. The Sifatiyya then offer the resolution: one must realize that knowledge in its essence does not require time. Knowledge, considered purely as knowledge, is simply disclosure and unveiling (تبين وانكشاف). Temporality only attaches to it when it is an attribute of a temporal being (الحادث). When knowledge is an attribute of the temporal, it involves encompassment and perception. The implication is that for God, who is eternal, knowledge is not temporal. The passage cuts off but the main point is clear: the confusion comes from projecting temporal categories onto God's knowledge.

Key Terms

زمان
صفة
حادث
تبين
انكشاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's knowledge must change because events change
Correction: The Sifatiyya clarify that temporality is not intrinsic to knowledge itself; knowledge is disclosure, and temporality only applies when it is an attribute of a temporal being.

Checkpoint
According to the Sifatiyya, what is the root of the difficulty regarding God's knowledge?
A) Assuming God knows particulars directly.
B) Thinking that God's knowledge is universal.
C) Imagining that God's knowledge relates to the known in a temporal manner.
D) Denying that God has knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge in itself is timeless disclosure; temporality belongs only to created knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does the Sifatiyya's distinction between timeless disclosure and temporal attribution challenge that assumption, and what does it mean for your sense of being constantly known?

When you sin, do you feel that God's knowledge of you becomes somehow different—perhaps disappointed or surprised? How should the truth that His knowledge is timeless and unchanging reshape your response to His awareness of your failures?
?
If knowledge in itself is simply disclosure, timeless and immediate, what does that demand of you regarding belief in His foreknowledge of your choices? Does it comfort or unsettle you? How can you live today in light of His eternal, unchanging knowledge of every detail of your life?

The Redundancy of Adding Universality to Divine Knowledge
The author critiques the addition of 'universality' and distinguishes two epistemic approaches.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that claiming God's knowledge is only of universals adds nothing to the position that He only knows His essence.

Core Idea
Adding 'universality' to divine knowledge is redundant—it tells us nothing new.

The author begins by critiquing a common refinement of the view that God only knows Himself. Some added that God knows universals, but the author shows this addition is meaningless. He then distinguishes two ways of knowing: from cause to effect and from necessary concomitant to subject.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the author's critical tone.
2
Identify the critique of adding universality
The author says that saying God knows universals is like saying He knows particulars—both are necessary. Why is this a return to the pure doctrine?
Focus on the phrase 'فما زاد هذا القائل على مذهبه إلا إطلاق لفظ الكلية'.
3
Understand the two epistemological approaches
The author contrasts two epistemic methods: from cause to effect, and from specific attribute to general. Notice the vast difference he mentions.
Think about which approach is more comprehensive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
له في وجوده فيكون العلم به لازما للعلم بذاته، فهو رجوع إلى محض مذهب من قال : إنه لا يعلم إلا ذاته فما زاد هذا القائل على مذهبه إلا إطلاق لفظ الكلية وهي معلومة لزوما ، كما كانت الجزئيات معلومة لزوما ، فلم يستفد من هذه الزيادة شيئًا، ومن عرف مبادئ الموجودات عرف ما يتأدى منها وما يحصل بها نظرًا من العلة إلى المعلول ومن عرف أخص أوصاف الموجودات عرف الأعم نظرا من الملزوم إلى اللازم وبين البابين بون عظيم بعيد. وما تمثل به ابن سينا من حديث خسوف القمر فهو دليل عليه فإن من علم مثل ذلك العلم ؛ أعني إذا كان كذا يكون كذا فيكون علمه مشروطًا، وهي قضية شرطية فلا يكون علمه علمًا محققا حتى يقول : إن كان الأمر كذا فهو كذا، فصار الأمر جزئيا بعد ما كان كليًّا، ويتعالى علم الباري تعالى عن القضايا الشرطية، بل عمله أعلى من أن يكون كليا أو جزئياً أو متغيراً بتغير الزمان، أو متكثرا بتكثر المعلومات، انظروا كيف عاد تنزيه القوم تشبيها، وكيف صار تحقيق القوم تمويها.
His knowledge of His existence is necessary for knowledge of His essence, so this is a return to the pure doctrine of one who says: He only knows His essence. This person has added nothing to his doctrine except the utterance of the word 'universality' which is necessarily known, just as the particulars are necessarily known, so he has gained nothing from this addition. And whoever knows the principles of existents knows what results from them and what is obtained by them, by considering from the cause to the effect. And whoever knows the most specific attributes of existents knows the more general, by considering from the subject to the necessary concomitant. Between the two approaches there is a great and vast difference. The example given by Ibn Sina concerning the lunar eclipse is evidence against him. For whoever knows such knowledge, i.e., 'if it is thus, it will be thus,' his knowledge is conditional, and it is a conditional proposition, so his knowledge is not definitive knowledge until he says: 'if the matter is thus, it is thus.' Thus the matter becomes particular after being universal. The knowledge of the Exalted Creator is far above conditional propositions. Rather, His knowledge is higher than being universal or particular, or changing with the change of time, or multiplying with the multiplicity of known things. See how their attempt at transcendence turned into anthropomorphism, and how their verification turned into obfuscation.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific view about divine knowledge. The author begins by critiquing the position that God only knows His own essence, arguing that adding the term 'universality' adds nothing because universals are already necessarily known. He then contrasts two approaches: one from cause to effect (knowing principles to derive consequences) and one from necessary concomitant (knowing specific attributes to derive general ones), stating they are vastly different. He then attacks Ibn Sina's analogy of the lunar eclipse, arguing that conditional knowledge ('if it is thus, it is thus') is not definitive and particularizes the universal. The author concludes that God's knowledge transcends conditionals, universals, particulars, time, and multiplicity, and that the so-called purification of God's knowledge actually leads to anthropomorphism and obfuscation.

Key Terms
علم الباري
كلية
جزئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That claiming God knows universals is a meaningful refinement of the view that He only knows His essence.
Correction: The author shows that both universals and particulars are necessarily known, so adding 'universality' adds no new information; it is merely a verbal addition.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that adding the term 'universality' to the claim that God only knows His essence adds nothing?
A) Because universals are not real
B) Because particulars are unknowable
C) Because universals are necessarily known just as particulars are
D) Because God's knowledge is beyond universals and particulars

Key Takeaway
Saying God knows universals adds nothing; He knows them necessarily, just as He knows particulars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What is one particular sin you have been ignoring because you think He only cares about generalities? Resolve today to bring that sin before Him in sincere repentance.
?
The author distinguishes two epistemic approaches: from cause to effect and from specific to general. Which approach characterizes your own understanding of God? Are you trying to infer His attributes from specific experiences, or do you start from the necessary truths about His essence? How does this impact your worship? Commit to studying the necessary divine attributes this week.
?
You just read that adding 'universality' to divine knowledge is redundant. Yet, how often do you limit God's knowledge to grand categories, forgetting that He knows every detail of your heart? What is one specific thought or deed you will bring before Him today, accepting that He already knows it completely? Write it down and ask His forgiveness.

Divine Knowledge Transcends Conditionals and Multiplicity
The author refutes Ibn Sina's conditional example and affirms divine transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author rejects Ibn Sina's example of the lunar eclipse and affirms that divine knowledge is beyond conditional propositions, universality, and particularity.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is not conditional; it transcends universal, particular, time, and multiplicity.

Having critiqued the addition of universality, the author now turns to Ibn Sina's famous analogy of the lunar eclipse. He shows that such conditional knowledge is deficient and particularized, and that God's knowledge is far above such limitations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Ibn Sina's analogy

2
Understand why conditional knowledge is problematic

3
Grasp the author's conclusion about divine transcendence
The author declares that God's knowledge is above conditionals, universals, particulars, time, and multiplicity, and ends with a sharp critique of those who try to purify God's knowledge.
Reflect on the final rhetorical question.
