The Ash'ari Resolution: Divine Knowledge is Timeless
The Ash'ari group identifies the source of confusion as temporalizing divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari school argues that divine knowledge is not temporal and how this resolves the apparent confusion across theological schools.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge is timeless manifestation; temporality is a human projection.

The Ash'ari group (الصفاتية) identifies a common mistake: thinking of God's knowledge as temporal, changing with events. This passage offers the corrective: knowledge as such is pure disclosure, and for God it is a single, timeless act of encompassing all things. Understanding this unity dissolves the difficulty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the full argument. Notice that the text begins by stating the problem is a shared confusion.
2
Identify the two key concepts
The passage contrasts temporal knowledge (changing with events) with knowledge as manifestation and disclosure. Also note the distinction between knowledge in originated beings (encompassment/perception) and in the Eternal (timeless unity).
3
Reflect on the resolution
Consider why realizing that God's knowledge is one and timeless makes the difficulty easy. This is the core of the Ash'ari solution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الصفاتية : إن الإشكال في هذه المسألة على جميع المذاهب من جهة أنهم تصوروا تعلق العلم بالمعلوم على وجه يتطرق إليه الزمان الماضي والمستقبل والحال ، حتى يقال : علم ،ويعلم وهو عالم وسيعلم فظنوا أن العلم زماني يتغير بتغير الحوادث ، ومن تحقق أن العلم من حيث هو علم لا يستدعي زمانًا، بل هو في نفسه تبين وانكشاف، وذلك إذا كان صفة للحادث وإحاطة وإدراكًا إذا كان صفة للقديم ، فهو مع وحدته محيط بكل الأشياء، ومع إحاطته واحد، ومن تحقق كونه واحدا سهل عليه الإشكال.
The attributes-group said: The difficulty in this matter is common to all schools, because they conceived the relation of knowledge to the known in a way that past, future, and present time pertain to it, so that it is said: he knew, he knows, and he will know. So they thought that knowledge is temporal, changing with events. But whoever realizes that knowledge, qua knowledge, does not require time—rather it is in itself manifestation and disclosure, and that, if it is an attribute of an originated being, it is encompassment and perception if it is an attribute of the Eternal—then He, despite His unity, encompasses all things, and despite His encompassing, He is one. And whoever realizes that He is one, the difficulty becomes easy for him.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a statement from the 'attributes-group' (الصفاتية), likely referring to the Ash'ari school. They argue that the confusion about divine knowledge is common to all theological schools because they mistakenly apply temporal categories (past, present, future) to God's knowledge, assuming it changes with events. The solution is to understand that knowledge as such is timeless; it is a pure manifestation and disclosure. For originated beings, knowledge is an encompassing and perception, but for the Eternal, it is a single, timeless act. Because God is one and His knowledge is one, He encompasses all things without multiplicity or change. Realizing this unity resolves the apparent difficulty.

علم
زمان
تبين وانكشاف
إحاطة
إدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine knowledge changes with events because we say 'He knew, He knows, He will know'.
Correction: Knowledge as such is timeless; God's knowledge is a single, eternal act encompassing all things without change.

Checkpoint

A) That God's knowledge is multiple
B) That people conceive it as temporal (past, present, future)
C) That God's knowledge is only of universals
D) That divine knowledge requires a specifier

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari solution to the problem of divine knowledge is to affirm its timeless unity, resolving the apparent conflict of temporal language.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Can you identify one habit that reduces your pursuit of knowledge to a transaction rather than a transformation? The Dilemma of Divine Knowledge's Multiplicity
Shahrastani presents a dilemma to prove God's knowledge is one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine knowledge cannot be multiple and must be one, through the dilemma of infinite or finite multiplicity.

Core Idea
God's knowledge cannot be multiple; it must be one and encompass all things.

Shahrastani employs a classic kalam dilemma to prove the oneness of divine knowledge. He argues that if knowledge were multiple, it would have to be either infinite (matching the infinite number of knowable things) or finite (requiring a specifier). Both options are impossible for God, establishing that His knowledge is one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the structure: a dilemma with two horns.
2
Understand the first horn: infinite multiplicity
The first possibility is that knowledge is multiple according to the number of all known things, which are infinite in estimation. This would require infinite actual knowledges, which is impossible because actual infinites cannot exist.
The author uses 'لا تتناهى' to indicate infinity.
3
Understand the second horn: finite multiplicity
The second possibility is that knowledge is limited to a specific number, but then it would require a specifier (مخصص). Since God is eternal and uncaused, He cannot have a specifier.
The term 'قديم' (eternal) implies no need for a cause.
4
Conclude the oneness of divine knowledge
Since both horns are impossible, the only remaining possibility is that His knowledge is one. The passage then states this conclusion.
This sets the stage for the next lesson on the scope of this knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالبرهان على أن علمه شيء واحد أنه لو كان كثيرًا لم يخلُ إما أن يتعدد بتعدد المعلومات كلها والمعلومات من حيث إن لها صلاحية المعلومية من الواجب والجائز والمستحيل لا تتناهى على التقدير فيلزم أن تكون العلوم المتعلقة بها لا تتناهى على التحقيق، وقد قام الدليل على أن أعدادًا في الوجود المحقق بالفعل لا تتناهى مستحيل، وإنما حصره الوجود فهو متناءٍ بالضرورة، وإما أن يتعدد بعدد مخصوص فيستدعي مخصصاً خاصا ، والقديم لا مخصص، فإذا علمه تعالى واحد فهو متعلق بجميع المعلومات والمعلومات لا تتناهى فعلمه متعلق بما لا يتناهى ولا يفرض اختصاصه بمعلوم معين كالعلم الحادث فإن الاختصاص والانحصار نقص وقصور من حيث إنه لا يختص إلا بمخصص ، والدليل على ذلك أن ما من علم يفرض إلا ويصح تعلق علم واحد منا به، ثم لا يثبت لنا العلم بالمعلوم إلا ضروريًا أو كسبيا، وعلى أي الوجهين فرض ثبوته فالله تعالى موجده ومبدعه، فإذا وجد كان عالما به، وإذا وجب كونه عالما بالعلم فهو عالم بالمعلوم ؛ إذ يستحيل أن يعلم العلم ولا يعلم المعلوم، فلزم أن يكون العلم القديم متعلقا بكل معلوم، وإنما اقتصر العلم الحادث على بعض المعلومات الجواز طريان الضد عليه، وإلا فالعلم من حيث هو علم لم يمتنع عليه التعلق بكل معلوم وكذلك كل صفة قديمة فإن متعلقاتها لا تتناهى، فقد أطلقت الأشعرية بأن معلومات الله تعالى في كل معلوم لا تتناهى، وأشاروا بذلك إلى التقديرات الجائزة في حق كل معلوم ؛ إذ ما من وقت من
The proof that His knowledge is one thing is that if it were multiple, it would inevitably be either multiple according to the multiplicity of all known things—and the known things, by virtue of their capacity to be known, from the necessary, the possible, and the impossible, are infinite in estimation, so it would follow that the knowledges pertaining to them are infinite in actuality, and proof has established that actual infinite numbers in existence are impossible—indeed, existence confines them, so they are necessarily finite; or it would be multiple according to a specific number, requiring a specific specifier, but the Eternal has no specifier. So since His knowledge is one, it relates to all known things. And the known things are infinite, so His knowledge relates to what is infinite, and it is not supposed that it is restricted to a particular known thing like originated knowledge, because restriction and confinement are deficiency and limitation insofar as it is only restricted by a specifier. The proof of that is that any knowledge that is supposed, one of our knowledges can relate to it. Then knowledge of the known is only established for us either necessarily or acquired. And whichever way it is supposed to be established, God is its originator and creator. So when it exists, He knows it. And when it is necessary that He knows the knowledge, He knows the known, because it is impossible for Him to know the knowledge and not know the known. So it follows that eternal knowledge relates to every known. Only originated knowledge is limited to some known things due to the possibility of the occurrence of its opposite. Otherwise, knowledge, qua knowledge, is not prevented from relating to every known. And likewise every eternal attribute has infinite relata. The Ash'ariyya have stated that God's known things in every known are infinite, and by that they refer to the possible estimations in respect of every known, for there is no time...
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the oneness of divine knowledge. The author presents a dilemma: if God's knowledge were multiple, it must be either infinite (matching the infinite number of knowable things) or finite. An infinite number of actual knowledges is impossible because actual infinites are impossible. If finite, it would require a specifier, but God is eternal and has no specifier. Thus, His knowledge must be one. This one knowledge relates to all known things, which are infinite, so it is infinite in scope. The author argues that restriction is a deficiency requiring a specifier, which does not apply to God. He then provides an additional proof: any supposed knowledge can be known by a human; God creates that knowledge, so He knows it, and knowing the knowledge entails knowing the known. Therefore, eternal knowledge relates to every known. Originated knowledge is limited only because it can be opposed, but knowledge in itself is not limited. The Ash'ari school accordingly states that God's knowledge in every known thing encompasses infinite possible estimations (تقديرات جائزة). The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is essentially complete.

تعدد
معلومات
لا تتناهى
مخصص
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's knowledge is multiple because He knows many different things.
Correction: The proof shows that multiplicity would lead to impossibilities, so knowledge must be one despite the multiplicity of known things.

Checkpoint
What is the first horn of the dilemma in the proof that divine knowledge is one?
A) If knowledge is multiple, it must be either infinite or finite.
B) If knowledge is one, it cannot relate to many things.
C) If knowledge is multiple, it must change with time.
D) If knowledge is infinite, it must be originated.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is one, as both infinite and finite multiplicity are impossible for Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Shahrastani proves that God's knowledge is one and encompasses all things. Your own knowledge is fragmented and limited. In what area of your life are you most tempted to rely on your partial understanding rather than submitting to God's complete knowledge? What specific step will you take this week to surrender that area to Him?
?
The proof shows that any multiplicity in God's knowledge would require a specifier, but God has no external cause. Reflect on a time when you assumed you understood the 'reason' behind a trial or blessing in your life. Are you willing to acknowledge that God's wisdom may have no specifiable cause that you can grasp? What assumption about God's actions do you need to let go of today?
?
If God's knowledge is one and all-encompassing, then He knows your every thought, secret, and sin in a single timeless act. Does this thought bring you comfort or fear? In light of this, what is one secret you have been hiding that you need to bring to Him in repentance or gratitude right now? Will you do it before this day ends?

Why Eternal Knowledge Encompasses All Things
Shahrastani proves that eternal knowledge relates to all known things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's eternal knowledge necessarily relates to all known things, including infinite possibilities.

Core Idea
All knowledge, by its nature, relates to every known; restriction is a deficiency only found in originated knowledge.

Having established that divine knowledge is one, Shahrastani now argues that this one knowledge encompasses all known things without limitation. He uses a sophisticated argument based on the nature of knowledge itself, showing that any supposed known thing can be known by God, and that restriction is only due to the possibility of opposition in originated knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. This part builds on the previous conclusion.
The passage begins with 'والمعلومات لا تتناهى' connecting to the previous lesson.
2
Follow the proof from human knowledge
The author argues that any supposed known thing can be known by a human, and that God creates that knowledge. Therefore, God knows the known thing.
This uses the principle that God is the creator of all knowledge.
3
Understand the distinction between eternal and originated knowledge
Originated knowledge is limited because it can be opposed, but knowledge qua knowledge is not prevented from relating to everything. Eternal attributes have infinite relata.
The Ash'ari school affirms infinite estimations in every known.

Proving That Divine Knowledge is One and Infinite
The author proves that God's knowledge is one and encompasses all infinite objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that God's knowledge must be one and cannot be multiple, and that it encompasses all infinite known things.

Core Idea
God's knowledge must be one because multiple knowledge would require a specifier, impossible for the Eternal.

This passage presents a sophisticated rational argument for the oneness and infinite scope of God's knowledge. The author uses a dichotomy: if God's knowledge were multiple, it would have to be either infinite or finite in number—both impossible for the Eternal. The argument then shows that this one knowledge relates to all infinite objects, unlike temporal knowledge which is limited. Understanding this proof is crucial for grasping the divine attribute of knowledge in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Try to grasp the overall flow before diving into details.
Notice the logical structure: the author starts with a conditional and explores two possibilities.
2
Identify the dichotomy
Locate the two possibilities offered: either knowledge would be infinite in number (impossible due to actual infinity) or finite with a specific number (requiring a specifier, which the Eternal lacks).
The author uses these two options to prove that multiple knowledge is impossible.
3
Understand the conclusion on oneness
The argument concludes that God's knowledge must be one. Then it shows that this one knowledge relates to all infinite known things, unlike temporal knowledge which is limited by accidents.
4
Examine the supporting argument
The author provides additional evidence: any supposed knowledge can be known by a single human knowledge, and since God creates all knowledge, He must know all things.
This reinforces that eternal knowledge cannot be restricted.
5
Reflect on the Ash'ari point
The passage ends with the Ash'ari claim that God's information about each known thing is infinite, referring to all possible events and states.
Consider how this relates to the infinite scope of divine knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالبرهان على أن علمه شيء واحد أنه لو كان كثيراً لم يخلُ إما أن يتعدد بتعدد المعلومات كلها والمعلومات من حيث إن لها صلاحية المعلومية من الواجب والجائز والمستحيل لا تتناهى على التقدير فيلزم أن تكون العلوم المتعلقة بها لا تتناهى على التحقيق، وقد قام الدليل على أن أعداداً في الوجود المحقق بالفعل لا تتناهى مستحيل، وإنما حصره الوجود فهو متناءٍ بالضرورة، وإما أن يتعدد بعدد مخصوص فيستدعي مخصصاً خاصا، والقديم لا مخصص، فإذا علمه تعالى واحد فهو متعلق بجميع المعلومات والمعلومات لا تتناهى فعلمه متعلق بما لا يتناهى ولا يفرض اختصاصه بمعلوم معين كالعلم الحادث فإن الاختصاص والانحصار نقص وقصور من حيث إنه لا يختص إلا بمخصص، والدليل على ذلك أن ما من علم يفرض إلا ويصح تعلق علم واحد منا به، ثم لا يثبت لنا العلم بالمعلوم إلا ضرورياً أو كسبياً، وعلى أي الوجهين فرض ثبوته فالله تعالى موجده ومبدعه، فإذا وجد كان عالماً به، وإذا وجب كونه عالماً بالعلم فهو عالم بالمعلوم؛ إذ يستحيل أن يعلم العلم ولا يعلم المعلوم، فلزم أن يكون العلم القديم متعلقاً بكل معلوم، وإنما اقتصر العلم الحادث على بعض المعلومات لجواز طريان الضد عليه، وإلا فالعلم من حيث هو علم لم يمتنع عليه التعلق بكل معلوم وكذلك كل صفة قديمة فإن متعلقاتها لا تتناهى، فقد أطلقت الأشعرية بأن معلومات الله تعالى في كل معلوم لا تتناهى، وأشاروا بذلك إلى التقديرات الجائزة في حق كل معلوم؛ إذ ما من وقت من الأوقات وحين من الأحيان إلا ويجوز وقوع الحادثة فيه على البدل، وكذلك ما من عرض إلا ويجوز اختصاصه بكل جوهر على البدل.
The proof that His knowledge is one is that if it were many, it would either be multiplied by the multiplication of all known things — and the known things, insofar as they have the potentiality of being known from the necessary, the possible, and the impossible, are infinite in estimation — so it would follow that the sciences related to them would be infinite in actuality, and it has been established by evidence that [infinite] numbers in actual existence are impossible, and that which existence encompasses is necessarily finite; or it would be multiplied by a specific number, which would require a particular specifier, but the Eternal has no specifier. So if His knowledge is one, it relates to all known things, and known things are infinite, so His knowledge relates to what is infinite, and it is not supposed to be restricted to a specific known thing like temporal knowledge, for restriction and limitation are deficiency and imperfection, since it is only restricted by a specifier. The evidence for that is that there is no knowledge that can be supposed except that a single knowledge of ours can relate to it; then we do not have knowledge of the known thing except necessarily or acquired, and whichever way its existence is supposed, God Almighty is its originator and innovator. So if it exists, He is knowing of it, and if His being knowing through knowledge is necessary, He is knowing of the known thing, for it is impossible that He knows the knowledge and does not know the known thing. So it follows that the eternal knowledge relates to every known thing. Temporal knowledge is restricted to some known things only because of the possibility of occurrence of its opposite; otherwise, knowledge in itself is not prevented from relating to every known thing. Similarly, every eternal attribute has infinite relations. The Ash'aris have expressed that the information of God Almighty concerning every known thing are infinite, and by that they referred to the possible estimations regarding each known thing; for there is no time or moment except that it is possible for an event to occur in it on substitution, and likewise there is no accident except that it is possible for it to be specific to every substance on substitution.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God's knowledge (العلم) is one (واحد) and that its scope is infinite (لا يتناهى). The author presents a rational argument using a dichotomy: if God's knowledge were multiple, it would have to be either infinite in number or finite with a specific number. Both options are impossible for the Eternal (القديم). Infinite multiplicity is impossible because actual infinity cannot exist. Finite multiplicity would require a specifier (مخصص), but the Eternal has no specifier. Therefore, God's knowledge must be one. This one knowledge then relates to all infinite known things (المعلومات) without being restricted to any particular one, unlike temporal (حادث) knowledge which is limited due to accidental impediments. The author supports this by noting that if a single human knowledge can relate to any supposed thing, then since God creates all knowledge, He must know all things. The passage concludes with an Ash'ari dictum that God's information (معلومات) regarding each known thing are infinite, referring to all possible states and events that could occur in every time and substance.

Key Terms
علم
قديم
معلومات
تعلق
مخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that one knowledge cannot relate to infinite objects because it would be overwhelmed.
Correction: The author argues that knowledge in itself is not limited; limitation comes from accidental factors like possibility of opposites. Eternal knowledge is free from such limitations.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that God's knowledge cannot be multiple with a specific number?
A) Because a specific number would require a specifier, which is impossible for the Eternal.
B) Because a specific number would imply imperfection.
C) Because a specific number would contradict God's unity.
D) Because a specific number would be finite and thus insufficient.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is one, eternal, and encompasses all infinite objects without limitation, unlike temporal knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge is one and encompasses every detail of your life, including every thought and intention, are you living with the awareness that nothing is hidden from Him? What would change today if you truly believed His knowledge covers even the secret whispers of your heart?
?
The author says restriction and limitation are deficiency and imperfection. In what areas of your life are you trying to restrict God's involvement or limit His knowledge, perhaps by ignoring His all-encompassing awareness? Will you intentionally surrender that area to His infinite knowledge today?
?
Consider that God knows every possible event that could occur in your future, and He holds all knowledge at once. How does this truth affect your anxiety about the unknown? Are you willing to trust His knowledge more than your own planning, and ask Him to guide you in light of His infinite awareness?

Objection: Infinite Relations and the Unity of Knowledge
Objector challenges the possibility of infinite relations in a single knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's argument that infinite relations attached to a single knowledge are just as problematic as infinite separate sciences, and the dilemma whether these relations are real or merely estimative.

Core Idea
Infinite relations face the same problem as infinite sciences.

The objector begins by pointing out a seeming inconsistency in the author's position. The author had used the impossibility of infinite sciences being encompassed by existence to argue for the unity of divine knowledge. Yet, the objector argues, the same problem applies to the infinite relations that the one knowledge must have. He then raises a further dilemma: are these relations real or only estimative? Both options appear problematic when applied to the Eternal. In this lesson, we explore the first part of this objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the objector's challenge come through clearly.
Notice how the objector summarizes the author's position first.
2
Identify the core analogy
The objector draws a parallel between infinite separate sciences and infinite relations in one knowledge. See how he uses the same reasoning.
3
Examine the two options
The objector presents a dilemma: the relations are either real (تحقيق) or estimative (تقدير). Think about why each option leads to a problem for the author.
Consider how 'estimation' applies to the Eternal.
4
Reflect on the challenge
Ask yourself: Why does the objector think this undermines the author's argument? What answer might the author give?
This connects to the broader theological debate.

قال المعترض: تلقيتم وحدة العلم الأزلي من استحالة علوم غير متناهية بحصرها الوجود ومن أن الاختصاص بعدد مخصوص ثم ارتكبتم مثله في العلم الواحد حيث قلتم: إنه واحد متعلق بمعلومات لا تتناهى، فقد أثبتم له تعلقات حقيقية أو تقديرية لا تتناهى وما حصره الوجود كيف يشتمل على تعلقات غير متناهية، فإن قلتم: إنها على التقدير المفروض لا تتناهى فالتقدير كيف يتصور في حق القديم سبحانه، وإن قلتم: إنها على التحقيق الموجود، فالتحقيق للمتعلق كالتحقيق للمتعلق، فلم لا يجوز علوم غير متناهية، ثم التناهي بعدد مخصوص كالتناهي بالواحد فإن أوجب اختصاص العدد مخصصاً كذلك يستدعي اختصاص الوحدة مخصصاً، وهو كما أن كثرة نوع الإنسان في الوجود استدعت مكثراً، ووحدة نوع الشمس في الوجود استدعت موحداً، ثم معلومات الله تعالى مجملة أم مفصلة؟ فإن قلتم: إنها مجملة على معنى أنه يعلم ما لا يتناهى من حيث إنها لا تتناهى من غير أن ينفصل معلوم عن معلوم، فهو علم واحد تعلق بمعلوم واحد وما ينفصل به بقي مجهولاً، وإن قلتم: إنها مفصلة على معنى أنها تتميز في علمه بخصائص صفاتها، فالجمع بين التفصيل ونفي النهاية مستحيل، فما الجواب عن هذه المشكلات؟
The objector said: You derived the unity of eternal knowledge from the impossibility of infinite sciences being encompassed by existence and from the restriction to a specific number, yet you have committed the same [problem] regarding the one knowledge when you said: It is one relating to infinite known things. So you have affirmed for it real or estimative relations that are infinite. How can that which existence encompasses contain infinite relations? If you say: They are infinite on the supposed estimation, then how can estimation be conceived regarding the Eternal, exalted? And if you say: They are infinite in actual existence, then the actuality for the relation is like the actuality for the related, so why is it not permissible to have infinite sciences? Moreover, finitude by a specific number is like finitude by one, for if the restriction of number requires a specifier, likewise the restriction of unity requires a specifier. This is like how the multiplicity of the type 'human' in existence requires a multiplier, and the unity of the type 'sun' in existence requires a unifier. Then are God's known things summary or detailed? If you say: They are summary in the sense that He knows the infinite insofar as it is infinite, without distinguishing one known from another, then it is one knowledge relating to one thing, and what is distinguished from it remains unknown. If you say: They are detailed in the sense that they are distinguished in His knowledge by the specific properties of their attributes, then combining detail and the negation of finitude is impossible. So what is the answer to these problems?
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is an objection (اعتراض) raised by a critic against the author's earlier argument that God's knowledge is one yet relates to infinite objects. The objector points out a seeming inconsistency: if the author rejected infinite sciences (علوم غير متناهية) because existence cannot encompass them, then how can the one knowledge have infinite relations (تعلقات)? The objector presents a series of challenges:

1. If the relations are real (حقيقية) and infinite, the same problem of being encompassed by existence applies.
2. If the relations are only estimative (تقديرية), then how can mere estimation apply to the Eternal?
3. If they are actual, then why not allow infinite sciences instead of a single infinite knowledge?
4. The need for a specifier applies not only to multiplicity but also to unity: if a specific number requires a specifier, then the specific number 'one' also requires a specifier, just as the types 'human' (many individuals) and 'sun' (one individual) each require a cause.
5. The objector then asks whether God's knowledge of infinite things is summary (مجمل) or detailed (مفصل). If summary, it is essentially knowledge of one thing (the infinite as a whole), so individual things remain unknown. If detailed, then an infinite number of distinct pieces of knowledge would be required, which is impossible. The objector ends by demanding an answer.

Key Terms
تعلقات (relations)
تقدير (estimation)
تحقيق (actualization)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objector is rejecting divine knowledge altogether.
Correction: The objector is only pointing out a logical inconsistency within the author's own framework; he is not denying that God knows.

Checkpoint
According to the objector, what problem arises if the relations of the one knowledge are real?
A) They would be finite.
B) They would be estimative.
C) They would be comparable to having infinite separate sciences.
D) They would be unknown.

Key Takeaway
The objector's first challenge equates infinite relations in one knowledge to infinite separate sciences, creating a dilemma about the nature of those relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a logical objection to your understanding of God's attributes, do you feel threatened or do you trust that a coherent answer exists? How does your immediate reaction reveal the strength of your faith?
?
The objector uses reason to question the coherence of divine knowledge. How comfortable are you with using your own intellect to probe the nature of God? Do you fear it leads to doubt, or do you embrace it as a way to deepen your trust in His perfection?
?
This passage forces you to consider how God's knowledge can be both one and encompass all things. How does this tension affect your reliance on His knowledge in your daily life? Are you willing to accept mystery, or do you insist on logical resolution before you can fully trust?

Objection: Unity Also Requires a Specifier
Objector argues that unity, like multiplicity, needs a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's argument that just as multiplicity requires a cause, unity also requires a specifier.

Core Idea
The number 'one' is just as contingent as any other number.

The objector now shifts to a different but related point. The author had used the necessity of a specifier for a specific number of sciences to argue for the unity of knowledge. The objector turns this argument around: if a specific number (like the number of sciences) requires a cause, then the specific number 'one' also requires a cause. He illustrates with the examples of the human species (many individuals) and the sun (one individual), both needing a cause. This lesson examines this analogical argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the concise wording.
2
Identify the principle
The objector states: finitude by a specific number is like finitude by one. Both require a specifier.
This is the key logical point.
3
Analyze the examples
He gives two examples: human species (many individuals) requires a 'multiplier', and sun species (one individual) requires a 'unifier'. Understand the analogy.
The sun is an example of a single celestial body.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: How does this challenge the author's argument that unity avoids the need for a specifier?
The author had argued that a specific number needs a specifier, but the objector says unity is also a specific number.

Objection: The Summary-Detailed Dilemma for Infinite Knowledge
Objector presents the summary vs. detailed dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's dilemma: whether God's knowledge of infinite things is summary or detailed, and why both options seem impossible.

Core Idea
Knowing infinite things in detail would require infinite distinct knowledge.

The objector's final challenge focuses on the nature of God's knowledge of infinite objects. He asks: Is this knowledge summary (مجمل) or detailed (مفصل)? If summary, then God only knows the infinite as a whole, not each individual thing; if detailed, then He must have infinitely many distinct pieces of knowledge, which contradicts the unity of knowledge. This lesson examines this dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the question format and the two horns.
2
Understand the summary option
If God's knowledge is summary, He knows the infinite only in general; individual objects remain unknown.
This option leads to ignorance of particulars.
3
Understand the detailed option
If detailed, God knows each object distinctly, but that implies infinite distinct knowns, which is impossible.
This contradicts the unity and finitude of divine knowledge.
4
Reflect on the challenge
Both options are problematic for the author. Consider what answer might resolve the dilemma.
This is a classic problem in theology.

?

?

?
If you had to choose between God knowing you only as a 'summary'—a general category—and knowing you in excruciating detail, which would you prefer? Why? And how does your preference reveal what you truly expect from your Creator?

Objection and Answer on the Unity of Divine Knowledge
Shahrastani presents an objection and the Ash'arite answer on divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'arite response to the objection that a single divine knowledge cannot relate to infinite objects.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is one potentiality, not infinite distinct relations.

Shahrastani presents a powerful objection against the Ash'arite view of divine knowledge: if God's knowledge is one, how can it encompass infinite objects? The answer clarifies that by 'relation' the Ash'arites mean a single capacity to know whatever presents itself, not a multiplicity of actual ties. This lesson unpacks the objection and the response, showing how the unity of God's knowledge is preserved.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Begin by reading the objector's argument carefully. Note the three main points: the problem of infinite relations, the specification by unity requiring a cause, and the dilemma of summarized vs. detailed knowledge.
2
Read the Ash'arite response
Now read the reply from the proponents of attributes. Focus on the definition of 'relation' as a potentiality (salahiyya) and the analogy of the Bestower of Forms.
3
Reflect on the analogy
Consider the analogy of the Bestower of Forms and matter: how does it illustrate the point? How does the unity of the Bestower coexist with infinite forms by substitution?
This analogy is inspired by the philosophers but used to support the Ash'arite position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المعترض: تلقيتم وحدة العلم الأزلي من استحالة علوم غير متناهية يحصرها الوجود ومن أن الاختصاص بعدد مخصوص ثم ارتكبتم مثله في العلم الواحد حيث قلتم: إنه واحد متعلق بمعلومات لا تتناهى، فقد أثبتم له تعلقات حقيقية أو تقديرية لا تتناهى وما حصره الوجود كيف يشتمل على تعلقات غير متناهية، فإن قلتم: إنها على التقدير المفروض لا تتناهى فالتقدير كيف يتصور في حق القديم سبحانه، وإن قلتم: إنها على التحقيق الموجود، فالتحقيق للمتعلق كالتحقيق للمتعلق، فلم لا يجوز علوم غير متناهية، ثم التناهي بعدد مخصوص كالتناهي بالواحد فإن أوجب اختصاص العدد مخصصا كذلك يستدعي اختصاص الوحدة مخصصا، وهو كما أن كثرة نوع الإنسان في الوجود استدعى مكثرا، ووحدة نوع الشمس في الوجود استدعت موحدا، ثم معلومات الله تعالى مجملة أم مفصلة؟ فإن قلتم: إنها مجملة على معنى أنه يعلم ما لا يتناهى من حيث إنها لا تتناهى من غير أن ينفصل معلوم عن معلوم، فهو علم واحد تعلق بمعلوم واحد وما ينفصل به بقي مجهولا، وإن قلتم: إنها مفصلة على معنى أنها تتميز في علمه بخصائص صفاتها، فالجمع بين التفصيل ونفي النهاية مستحيل، فما الجواب عن هذه المشكلات؟
قالت الصفاتية: لسنا نعني بالتعلقات علائق حسية ولا حبائل خيالية، ولا ينفي التناهي عن المعلومات أعدادا من المعلومات غير متناهية تحاصرة في الوجود، بل نعني بالتعلق والمتعلق أن الصفة الأزلية صالحة لدرك ما يعرض عليها على وجه لا يستحيل، فيعبر عن تلك الصلاحية نحو الدرك بالتعلق، ويعبر عن جهة العرض عليه حتى يدركه بالمتعلق، ثم وجوه الجائزات على التقدير غير متناهية، فالمتعلقات غير متناهية، فالتعلقات غير متناهية، فالعلم القديم صفة متهيئة لدرك ما يعرض عليها على وجه الجواز دون الاستحالة، والقدرة الأزلية صفة متهيئة لإيجاد ما يعرض عليها على وجه الجواز دون الاستحالة، فالمعنى بالعرض جهة الإمكان، وتعيين الآحاد على البدل، والمعنى بالمعروض عليه جهة الصلاحية؛ إما نحو الإدراك، وإما نحو الإيجاد.
وهذا كما أن الصور المتعاقبة على الهيولي عند القوم لا تتناهى على البدل، وإنها فائضة على الهيولي من واهب الصور، وذات واهب الصور واحدة إلا أنها على صفة لها صلاحية الفيض حتى ما يتهيأ الهيولى لقبول الصورة فتعرض الإمكان على القدرة كتهيئ الهيولي لقبول الصور، وصلاحية الصفة له نحو الإدراك والإيجاد كصلاحية الواهب لفيض الصورة ثم الواهب واحد، ومع وحدته على كمال يفيض منه صور لا تتناهى على البدل فذات الواهب واحدة ولكنه في حكم ما لا يتناهى والصورة لا تتناهى ولكنها في حكم الواحد، ثم ما
The objector said: You derived the unicity of eternal knowledge from the impossibility of infinite sciences encompassed by existence, and that specification by a particular number [requires a specifier], then you committed the same in the single knowledge when you said: it is one related to infinite objects of knowledge. So you affirmed for it real or notional relations without end, and how can what existence encompasses include infinite relations? If you say that they are notional and infinite, how can the notional be conceived with respect to the Eternal, exalted? And if you say that they are actual, then actuality for the relation is like actuality for the related, so why are infinite sciences not possible? Moreover, finitude by a particular number is like finitude by unity: if it necessitates a specifier for number, similarly it necessitates a specifier for unity, just as the multiplicity of the human species in existence necessitated a multiplier, and the unity of the species of the sun in existence necessitated a unifier. Then are the objects of God's knowledge summarized or detailed? If you say they are summarized, meaning He knows the infinite insofar as it is infinite without discrimination of one object from another, then it is one knowledge related to one object, and what is discriminated remains unknown. If you say they are detailed, meaning they are distinguished in His knowledge by the particularities of their attributes, then combining detail with infinity is impossible. So what is the answer to these problems?
The proponents of attributes said: We do not mean by 'relations' sensory ties or imaginary ropes, nor does finitude negate from objects of knowledge numbers of infinite objects limited in existence. Rather, we mean by 'relation' and 'related' that the eternal attribute is capable of perceiving what presents itself to it in a way that is not impossible. So that capability (salahiyya) towards perception is expressed as 'relation', and the aspect of presentation to it until it perceives it is expressed as 'the related'. Then the aspects of possibles (wujuh al-ja'izat) are infinite in estimation, so the 'relateds' are infinite, thus the relations are infinite. So the eternal knowledge is an attribute ready to perceive what presents itself to it in a way of possibility (al-jawaz) not impossibility, and the eternal power is an attribute ready to bring into existence what presents itself to it in a way of possibility not impossibility. So by 'presentation' is meant the aspect of contingency (al-imkan), and the specification of particulars by substitution (ala al-badal), and by 'that which is presented to' is meant the aspect of capability; either towards perception or towards bringing into existence.
And this is like how the successive forms upon matter (al-hayula) according to [the philosophers] are infinite by way of substitution, and they emanate upon matter from the Bestower of Forms (wahib al-suwar), and the essence of the Bestower of Forms is one yet it has a quality of capability for emanation, so that when matter is prepared to receive a form, contingency presents itself to power like the preparation of matter to receive forms. And the capability of the attribute towards perception and bringing into existence is like the capability of the Bestower for the emanation of the form. Then the Bestower is one, and with His unity He is in a state of perfection from which emanate infinite forms by way of substitution, so the essence of the Bestower is one but it is in the ruling of the infinite, and the form is infinite but it is in the ruling of the one, then what...
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the attribute of divine knowledge. The objector argues against the Ash'arite position that God's knowledge is one yet relates to infinite objects. He presents several dilemmas: first, if infinite relations are real, they cannot be encompassed by a single existent; if notional, they are not real and cannot be attributed to God. Second, he argues that finitude by number or by unity both require a specifier (mukhassis), so unity of knowledge would also need a cause. Third, he asks whether God's knowledge is summarized (mujmal) or detailed (mufassal), showing either leads to absurdity. The answer (by the proponents of attributes) clarifies the intended meaning of 'relation' (ta'alluq). They deny that they mean actual, discrete ties; rather they mean a potentiality (salahiyya) of the attribute to perceive whatever occurs. This potentiality is one and does not entail multiplicity. They explain that the aspects (wujuh) of possibles are infinite in estimation, but the attribute's readiness is one. They analogize with the philosophers' concept of forms successively emanating from the one Bestower of Forms (wahib al-suwar) upon matter (hayula): the Bestower is one yet infinite forms emanate by substitution (ala al-badal). Similarly, divine knowledge is one attribute with a single readiness to know whatever possible object presents itself. The argument thus preserves the unity of God's knowledge while accounting for infinite objects.

Key Terms
تعلق (ta'alluq)
صلاحية (salahiyya)
الجائزات (al-ja'izat)
الهيولي (al-hayula)
واهب الصور (wahib al-suwar)
البدل (al-badal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Ash'arites affirm actual infinite distinct relations in God's knowledge.
Correction: The Ash'arites instead affirm a single potentiality (salahiyya) to perceive whatever object presents itself, not actual multiplicity.

Checkpoint
According to the response of the proponents of attributes, what is the intended meaning of 'relation' (تعلق) of the eternal knowledge?
A) Actual infinite distinct ties to each object
B) Imaginary conceptual links
C) A potentiality (صلاحية) to perceive whatever presents itself
D) The act of knowing each object individually

Key Takeaway
The Ash'arites resolve the tension between one knowledge and infinite objects by interpreting 'relation' as a single potentiality, not as actual multiplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that God's knowledge is one and all-encompassing. But when you encounter something you do not understand about God's decree, do you actually trust that His single knowledge already comprehends it perfectly, or does your heart feel that He must have a separate 'record' of your deed? What would it mean for your reliance on Him if you truly internalized that His knowledge is a simple, infinite capacity?
?
Consider the objector's challenge: 'If God's knowledge is one, then either He knows everything in a blurry summary or He can't know each detail without multiplicity.' Which side of that dilemma does your own understanding of God tend toward? Have you ever caught yourself thinking that God must be 'busy' managing the universe because you imagine His knowledge as many separate thoughts? How does this passage correct that mental image?
?
The Ash'arites use the analogy of the Bestower of Forms: one source emanates infinite forms without change. Is your own soul more like matter—receptive but passive—or like the eternal attribute, ready but unchanging? When you learn new things about God, do you become more or less convinced that He is beyond any accumulation of facts? What would it take for you to let go of the need to 'add' to God's perfection by your own understanding?

The Sifatiyya Definition of Ta'alluq and Muta'allaq
The Sifatiyya define ta'alluq and muta'allaq for eternal attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sifatiyya technical definition of ta'alluq and muta'allaq as they apply to eternal divine attributes, emphasizing that they refer to the attribute's inherent capability not sensory connection.

Core Idea
Eternal attributes relate to objects through capability, not attachment.

The Sifatiyya (those who affirm divine attributes) were often accused of positing multiple eternals. In response, they clarified their terminology. Here they define two key terms—ta'alluq (relation) and muta'allaq (relatant)—stressing that they refer to the attribute's inherent capacity to perceive or create, not to any material or changing bond. This passage lays the conceptual groundwork for discussing how God's knowledge and power encompass infinite objects without multiplicity in His essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Pay attention to what the Sifatiyya deny before they affirm.
The opening negation is important to avoid misunderstanding.
2
Understand the positive definition
Locate where the Sifatiyya define ta'alluq as the attribute's capability (salāḥiyya) and muta'allaq as the aspect of occurrence (jihat al-'araḍ).
Notice that the definition centers on capability, not change.
3
Trace the infinity argument
The passage states that because possible objects are infinite, the relatants and relations are infinite. Understand why this does not imply a change in the attribute.
Infinity here relates to the objects, not the attribute's essence.
4
Apply to knowledge and power
See how eternal knowledge and power are described as 'prepared' (muhayyi'a) to perceive or create possible occurrences.
Both attributes are similar in their relation to possibility.
5
Clarify the final terms
Read the last sentence carefully: 'occurrence' (al-'araḍ) means possibility and sequential specification, while 'subject of occurrence' (al-ma'rūḍ 'alayh) means capability.
This distinguishes between what is possible and the attribute's capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الصفاتية: لسنا نعني بالتعلقات علائق حسية ولا حبائل خيالية، ولا ينفي التناهي عن المعلومات أعدادًا من المعلومات غير متناهية محصورة في الوجود، بل نعني بالتعلق والمتعلق أن الصفة الأزلية صالحة لدرك ما يعرض عليها على وجه لا يستحيل، فيعبر عن تلك الصلاحية نحو الدرك بالتعلق، ويعبر عن جهة العرض عليه حتى يدركه بالمتعلق، ثم وجوه الجائزات على التقدير غير متناهية، فالمتعلقات غير متناهية، فالتعلقات غير متناهية، فالعلم القديم صفة متهيئة لدرك ما يعرض عليها على وجه الجواز دون الاستحالة، والقدرة الأزلية صفة متهيئة لإيجاد ما يعرض عليها على وجه الجواز دون الاستحالة، فالمعنى بالعرض جهة الإمكان، وتعيين الآحاد على البدل، والمعنى بالمعروض عليه جهة الصلاحية؛ إما نحو الإدراك، وإما نحو الإيجاد.
The Sifatiyya said: We do not mean by the relations (ta‘alluqat) sensory attachments nor imaginary snares, nor does finitude negate from the known things (al-ma‘lumat) numbers of infinite known things enclosed in existence. Rather we mean by relation (ta‘alluq) and relatant (muta‘allaq) that the eternal attribute (al-ṣifa al-azaliyya) is capable (ṣaliḥa) of perceiving what occurs to it in a manner not impossible. So that capability (ṣalāḥiyya) towards perception is expressed by relation, and the aspect of the occurrence (jihat al-‘araḍ) upon it so that it perceives it is expressed by the relatant. Then the aspects of the possible things (wujuh al-ja’izat) upon estimation are infinite, so the relatants are infinite, so the relations are infinite. Thus eternal knowledge (al-‘ilm al-qadim) is an attribute prepared (muhayyi’a) to perceive what occurs to it in a manner of possibility without impossibility, and eternal power (al-qudra al-azaliyya) is an attribute prepared to bring into existence what occurs to it in a manner of possibility without impossibility. So the meaning by occurrence is the aspect of possibility, and the specification of individuals consecutively, and the meaning by that upon which it occurs is the aspect of capability; either towards perception or towards bringing into existence.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a definition by the Sifatiyya (those who affirm divine attributes) clarifying their usage of the terms 'relation' (ta‘alluq) and 'relatant' (muta‘allaq) when discussing eternal attributes like knowledge and power. They begin by negating any material or figurative interpretation: they do not mean sensory attachments or imaginary snares. The infinity of objects does not contradict that there are infinite objects of knowledge. Instead, they explain that an eternal attribute is inherently capable (ṣaliḥa) of perceiving or affecting whatever occurs to it without impossibility. This capability towards perception is called 'relation,' and the aspect of the occurrence (i.e., the object that comes to be known or created) is called 'relatant.' Since the possible objects (jawā’izāt) are infinite, the relatants and consequently the relations are infinite. They then specify that eternal knowledge is a quality prepared for perceiving possible occurrences in a permissible manner, and eternal power is a quality prepared for bringing them into existence. Finally, they define 'occurrence' as the aspect of possibility and the specification of individuals as happening consecutively, while the 'subject of occurrence' is the aspect of capability, directed either towards perception or creation.

Key Terms
تعلق (ta‘alluq)
متعلق (muta‘allaq)
صلاحية (ṣalāḥiyya)
جواز (jawāz)
إمكان (imkān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that infinite relations imply multiplicity or change in the divine essence.
Correction: The Sifatiyya clarify that the relation is a description of the attribute's capability, not a change in the attribute itself. The infinity refers to the possible objects, not to any actualization in God.

Checkpoint
According to the Sifatiyya in this passage, what do they mean by the terms 'relation' (ta‘alluq) and 'relatant' (muta‘allaq) when applied to eternal attributes?
A) They mean sensory attachments to material objects.
B) They mean that the eternal attribute is capable of perceiving or creating what occurs to it, and the aspect of occurrence is the relatant.
C) They mean that the attribute changes with each new object.
D) They mean that God has infinite separate attributes for each object.

Key Takeaway
The Sifatiyya define ta'alluq as the inherent capability of an eternal attribute to perceive or create possible objects, and muta'allaq as the aspect of the possible object, emphasizing that infinite objects do not change the attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Allah's eternal knowledge is 'prepared to perceive every possible occurrence,' including every sin you might commit. Does the fact that His knowledge does not change diminish the weight of your sins in your heart, or does it magnify the reality that He has always known them? How does this truth confront the secret sins you have hidden from others?
?
The passage says eternal power is 'prepared to bring into existence what occurs to it.' If you truly believed that Allah’s power is always ready to create or change anything at any moment, would you still live with the same attachments to worldly outcomes? What is one specific situation in your life today where you need to trust in His readiness (ṣalāḥiyya) to act?

Analogy of the Bestower of Forms and the Unification of Divine Attributes
The author draws an analogy from the bestower of forms to illustrate the unity of the attribute despite infinite relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophical analogy of the Bestower of Forms and hyle illustrates the unity of the divine attribute while allowing for infinite relations.

Core Idea
One eternal attribute can have infinite relations without multiplicity in the essence.

Sanusi employs a sophisticated analogy from philosophical cosmology to address a potential objection: if the divine attributes are eternal and one, how can they have infinitely many relations (e.g., knowing all things)? He draws on the concept of the Bestower of Forms (Wāhib al-Ṣuwar) who, despite being one, emanates infinite successive forms upon the hyle. This analogy helps clarify that a single eternal attribute can relate to infinite objects without fragmentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Familiarize yourself with the flow.
The passage is dense; don't worry about details yet.
2
Understand the philosophical background
Identify the key terms: hyle (hayulā), Bestower of Forms, capability (ṣalāḥiyya), estimation (taqdīr). Understand that the philosophers believed forms emanate sequentially onto a receptive matter.
3
Trace the analogy
Notice how Sanusi parallels the relation between the Bestower and forms to the relation between the divine attribute and its objects of perception/creation.
The central point: the Bestower is one yet infinite forms emanate; analogously, the attribute is one yet relates to infinite things.
4
Distinguish between metaphorical and literal capability
The passage states that 'capability' (ṣalāḥiyya) is used metaphorically for the Eternal and literally for the originated. Reflect on why this distinction matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا كما أن الصور المتعاقبة على الهيولي عند القوم لا تتناهى على البدل، وإنها فائضة على الهيولي من واهب الصور، وذات واهب الصور واحدة إلا أنها على صفة لها صلاحية الفيض حتى ما يتهيأ الهيولى لقبول الصورة فتعرض الإمكان على القدرة كتهيأ الهيولى لقبول الصور، وصلاحية الصفة له نحو الإدراك والإيجاد كصلاحية الواهب لفيض الصورة ثم الواهب واحد، ومع وحدته على كمال يفيض منه صور لا تتناهى على البدل فذات الواهب واحدة ولكنه في حكم ما لا يتناهى والصورة لا تتناهى ولكنها في حكم الواحد، ثم ما الوجود فهو متناه، وما قدره المقدر غير متناه، فإن التقدير تعبير عن الصلاحتين، أو الاستعدادين، واستعمال لفظ الصلاحية في جانب القديم توسع، وفي جانب الحادث حقيقة، فإن القديم بالإعطاء أجدر والحادث بالقبول أولى.
And this is just as the successive forms (ṣuwar) upon the hyle (al-hayulā) according to the philosophers (al-qawm) are infinite consecutively, and they emanate upon the hyle from the Bestower of Forms (wāhib al-ṣuwar), and the essence of the Bestower of Forms is one, except that it is in a state of having the capability of emanation so that when the hyle becomes prepared to receive the form, then the possibility is presented to the power, like the preparedness of the hyle to receive forms. And the capability of the attribute for it towards perception and bringing into existence is like the capability of the Bestower for the emanation of the form. Then the Bestower is one, and despite His oneness, He is in a state of perfection from which emanate forms infinite consecutively. So the essence of the Bestower is one, but He is in the status of that which is infinite, and the form is infinite, but it is in the status of the one. Then as for existence (al-wujūd), it is finite, and what the estimator (al-muqaddir) estimates is infinite. For estimation (al-taqdīr) is an expression of the two capabilities or two potencies. And the use of the word 'capability' regarding the Eternal is metaphorical, and regarding the originated it is literal. For the Eternal is more deserving of giving and the originated is more worthy of reception.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents an analogy from philosophical cosmology to illustrate how a single eternal attribute can have infinite relations without multiplicity in the divine essence. The author references the theory that forms succeed each other upon the hyle (hayulā) infinitely, emanating from the Bestower of Forms (Wāhib al-Ṣuwar), whose essence is one yet possesses the capability (ṣalāḥiyya) to emanate forms whenever the hyle is prepared. Similarly, the eternal attributes have a capability towards perception and creation. The Bestower is one, but due to His perfection, infinite forms emanate consecutively, so He is, as it were, in the status of the infinite. The forms themselves are infinite in number but each is in the status of the one (i.e., individual). The author then discusses existence and estimation: existence is finite (only what exists is finite), but what the estimator estimates (possibilities) is infinite. The term 'estimation' expresses the two capabilities: that of the attribute and the receptivity of the subject. Using 'capability' for the Eternal is metaphorical (tawassu‘), indicating His perfection, while for the originated it is literal (ḥaqīqa), as the originated has a real capacity to receive. The Eternal is more deserving of giving, and the originated is more worthy of receiving.

Key Terms
هيولي (hayulā)
واهب الصور (wāhib al-ṣuwar)
صلاحية (ṣalāḥiyya)
استعداد (isti‘dād)
تقدير (taqdīr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The analogy implies that God is passive or dependent on matter.
Correction: The author states that the capability of the Eternal is metaphorical, indicating His perfection and giving nature, not any need.

Checkpoint
In the analogy, what is the role of the hyle (hayulā) in relation to the bestower of forms?
A) The hyle is the source of the forms.
B) The hyle is receptive to forms when prepared.
C) The hyle is identical to the bestower.
D) The hyle creates the forms independently.

Key Takeaway
The infinite relations of a single divine attribute do not imply multiplicity in the essence, just as the single Bestower of Forms emanates infinite forms without division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's knowledge of every detail of your life, do you unconsciously imagine it as a vast collection of separate bits of information? How does this lesson's truth—that one simple attribute encompasses all without division—expose that as a shirk-like limitation in your thinking? What specific step will you take today to correct that imagination in your worship?
?
You often ask God for things and feel He might be 'able' to answer, like a powerful human who might or might not act. This lesson says God's capability is not a passive potential like yours. How does this challenge your prayer life? What does it demand of you when you supplicate? Write down one concrete change you will make in your dua tonight because of this.

The Roles of the Four Divine Attributes
Enumerates the functions of the four attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how each of the four essential attributes—knowledge, power, will, and speech—functions in relation to the infinite possible objects.

Core Idea
Each attribute has a specific capability for a distinct divine act.

This passage from al-Shahrastani details how the infinite aspects of possibility all depend on the Eternal. The author then explains how knowledge, power, will, and speech each have a unique role: knowledge perceives, power creates, will specifies, and speech commands. This sets the stage for a deeper theological problem.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, focusing on the four functions described. Then read the English translation to ensure understanding.
The Arabic uses 'ṣalāḥiyya' (capability) for each attribute.
2
Identify the four attributes and their roles
List each attribute (knowledge, power, will, speech) and its associated function as given in the passage. Notice the pattern: each has a 'capability towards' a specific action.
3
Reflect on the relationship
Consider that all four attributes act upon the same infinite possible objects. How do they work together without conflict? This will be explored in the next lesson.
This lesson only covers the roles, not the unity problem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم جهات الإمكان لا تتناهى وهي تشترك كلها في جهة الإمكان المحتاج إلى من يخرجه من وجه الإمكان إلى عين الوجود، فوجوه الممكنات كلها إلى القديم سبحانه، وهو تعالى من حيث العلم يحيط بها ويدركها بوجه واحد وهو صلاحية العلم نحو الإدراك، ويوجدها ويخترعها بوجه وهو صلاحية القدرة نحو الإيجاد، وتخصصها بمثل دون مثل بوجه وهو صلاحية الإرادة نحو التخصيص، ويتصرف فيها بتكليف وتعريف بوجه وهو صلاحية الكلام نحو الأمر والنهي، ثم هل تشترك هذه الحقائق والخصائص في صفة واحدة أم في ذات واحدة؟ فتلك الطامة الكبرى على المتكلمين فرَّ القاضي أبو بكر الباقلاني رضي الله عنه منها إلى السمع، وقد استعاذ بمعاذ والتجأ إلى ملاذ والله الموفق.
Then the aspects of possibility (jihāt al-imkān) are infinite, and they all share in the aspect of possibility that is in need of one who brings it out from the aspect of possibility into the very existence. So the aspects of possible things all turn to the Eternal, exalted is He. And He, exalted, from the aspect of knowledge encompasses them and perceives them with a single aspect, which is the capability of knowledge towards perception. And He brings them into existence and creates them from an aspect, which is the capability of power towards bringing into existence. And He specifies them with one likeness rather than another from an aspect, which is the capability of will towards specification. And He disposes of them through obligation and definition from an aspect, which is the capability of speech towards command and prohibition. Then, do these realities (ḥaqā’iq) and properties (khaṣā’iṣ) share in one attribute or in one essence? That is the great calamity (al-ṭāmma al-kubrā) upon the theologians (al-mutakallimūn). The judge Abū Bakr al-Bāqillānī, may God be pleased with him, fled from it to revelation (al-sam‘), and he sought refuge in a place of refuge and took refuge in a sanctuary. And God is the Grantor of Success.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage explains how the four essential divine attributes—knowledge, power, will, and speech—each have a specific function regarding the infinite possible objects. The aspects of possibility are infinite, and they all share the characteristic of needing an agent to bring them from possibility to actual existence. That agent is the Eternal. From the aspect of knowledge, God encompasses all possibles in one act of perception. From power, He creates them. From will, He specifies which are actualized. From speech, He issues commands and prohibitions concerning them. Then the author poses a critical question: do these distinct functions belong to one attribute or to the one essence? He calls this the 'great calamity' for the theologians—the problem of reconciling attribute multiplicity with divine unity. He notes that Abū Bakr al-Bāqillānī, a leading Ash‘ari theologian, avoided this problem by having recourse to revelation (al-sam‘), i.e., accepting scriptural statements without rational resolution. The passage ends with a prayer for divine assistance.

Key Terms
جهات الإمكان (jihāt al-imkān)
صلاحية (ṣalāḥiyya)
تخصيص (takhṣīṣ)
تكليف (taklīf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the role of the attribute of will?
A) To perceive all possible objects.
B) To bring possible objects into existence.
C) To specify which possible objects are actualized.
D) To issue commands and prohibitions.

Key Takeaway
The four essential attributes each have a distinct capability: knowledge perceives, power creates, will specifies, and speech commands.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Allah's will specifies which possibilities become your reality. Are you truly content with His choices for you, even the painful ones? What specific area of your life do you need to surrender to His specification today?
?
God's speech issues commands and prohibitions that apply directly to you. Which command of Allah have you been treating as optional or negotiable? What concrete step will you take this week to obey it fully?
?
If Allah's power alone brings everything into existence, where in your life are you still relying on your own strength instead of His? Write down one habit or attitude that reflects self-reliance, and commit to replacing it with trust in His power.

The Great Calamity and Baqillani's Recourse to Revelation
Poses the unity problem and reports Baqillani's flight to revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological problem of attribute unity (the 'great calamity') and why al-Bāqillānī turned to revelation for a resolution.

Core Idea
The relationship between attributes and essence is a mystery that reason cannot solve alone.

After detailing the functions of the four attributes, the author poses a critical question: do these distinct realities and properties inhere in one attribute or in the one essence? He calls this the 'great calamity' for theologians. Then he notes that the prominent Ash'ari scholar Abū Bakr al-Bāqillānī fled from this rational difficulty to revelation, accepting scripture as the final authority on the matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the complete passage
Read the Arabic and translation for this lesson, focusing on the question and Baqillani's response.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the theological problem
The problem is whether the distinct functions of the attributes belong to one attribute (i.e., a single attribute with multiple roles) or to the one essence (i.e., the essence itself has these capabilities). This is the 'great calamity'.
This is a classic Ash'ari debate about the relation of attributes to essence.
3
Examine Baqillani's solution
Baqillani fled from rational speculation to revelation (al-sam‘). That means he accepted what scripture says about attributes without further rational analysis. This is a legitimate Ash'ari position.
The phrase 'fled from it to revelation' indicates a shift from rational theology to reliance on transmitted texts.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why this problem is considered a 'great calamity'—it challenges human reason's ability to fully comprehend the divine nature. Baqillani's recourse is an admission of that limitation.

The problem of attribute unity is so profound that even a great theologian like al-Bāqillānī admitted reason's limits and turned to revelation.

?

What is one area of your faith where you need to surrender your reasoning to the text?
?

?

Are you quick to accept revelation's answers, or do you rely on your own reasoning? Make a concrete plan to 'flee to revelation' the next time you face such a challenge.

Existence, Determination, and the Two Aptitudes
Distinguishing finite existence from infinite determination and explaining the two aptitudes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between finite existence and infinite divine determination, and the metaphorical nature of 'aptitude' when applied to the Eternal.

Core Idea
God's determination is infinite; our existence is finite.

This opening passage from the chapter introduces a fundamental distinction: while existence itself is finite, what God has determined and decreed is without limit. The author also clarifies the key term 'determination' (taqdir) as expressing two aptitudes, and warns against taking the term 'aptitude' for God in a literal sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the contrast
Note the contrast between finite existence and infinite determination. What does 'determination' refer to?
3
Understand the two aptitudes
The term 'aptitude' is used metaphorically for the Eternal but literally for the created. Why? Because the Eternal bestows and the originated receives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجود فهو متناه، وما قدره المقدر غير متناه، فإن التقدير تعبير عن الصلاحتين، أو الاستعدادين، واستعمال لفظ الصلاحية في جانب القديم توسع، وفي جانب الحادث حقيقة، فإن القديم بالإعطاء أجدر والحادث بالقبول أولى، ثم جهات الإمكان لا تتناهى وهي تشترك كلها في جهة الإمكان المحتاج إلى من يخرجه من وجه الإمكان إلى عين الوجود، فوجوه الممكنات كلها إلى القديم سبحانه، وهو تعالى من حيث العلم يحيط بها ويدركها بوجه واحد وهو صلاحية العلم نحو الإدراك، ويوجدها ويخترعها بوجه وهو صلاحية القدرة نحو الإيجاد، وتخصصها بمثل دون مثل بوجه وهو صلاحية الإرادة نحو التخصيص، ويتصرف فيها بتكليف وتعريف بوجه وهو صلاحية الكلام نحو الأمر والنهي، ثم هل تشترك هذه الحقائق والخصائص في صفة واحدة أم في ذات واحدة؟ فتلك الطامة الكبرى على المتكلمين فرَّ القاضي أبو بكر الباقلاني رضي الله عنه منها إلى السمع، وقد استعاذ بمعاذ والتجأ إلى ملاذ والله الموفق
Existence is finite, and what the Determiner has determined is infinite. For determination is an expression of two aptitudes or two dispositions. The use of the word 'aptitude' with respect to the Eternal is metaphorical, and with respect to the originated is literal. For the Eternal is worthier of bestowing and the originated is more worthy of receiving. Then the aspects of possibility are infinite, and they all share in the aspect of possibility needing one who brings it out from the aspect of possibility to the very existence. So all aspects of possible things are directed to the Eternal (glorified be He). And He, exalted, from the perspective of knowledge encompasses them and perceives them in one aspect, which is the aptitude of knowledge for perception. And He brings them into existence and invents them in one aspect, which is the aptitude of power for bringing into existence. And He specifies them with one like without another in one aspect, which is the aptitude of will for specification. And He disposes in them by obligation and definition in one aspect, which is the aptitude of speech for command and prohibition. Then: do these realities and characteristics share in one attribute or in one essence? That is the great calamity for the theologians. Judge Abu Bakr al-Baqillani (may God be pleased with him) fled from it to revelation, and sought refuge in a refuge and took shelter in a shelter. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a theological reflection on the nature of existence, possibility, and the divine attributes. It begins by stating that existence is finite, but what God has determined is infinite—this likely refers to the objects of His power or knowledge. The author then explains that 'determination' (taqdir) is an expression of two aptitudes or dispositions: the term 'aptitude' when applied to the Eternal is metaphorical, but when applied to the originated it is literal. The Eternal is more fitting to give, and the originated more fitting to receive. The aspects of possibility (jihāt al-imkān) are infinite, yet they all share one common aspect: the possibility that stands in need of an agent to bring it from potentiality to actual existence. Thus, all possible things are directed towards the Eternal. The passage then enumerates the divine attributes: knowledge (al-ʿilm) encompasses all possibilities in one mode (aptitude for perception), power (al-qudra) creates them in one mode, will (al-irāda) specifies them, and speech (al-kalām) commands and prohibits. A critical question arises: Do these realities and properties share in a single attribute or in a single essence? This is described as a tremendous difficulty ('ṭāmmat al-kubrā') for theologians, and al-Baqillani is said to have taken refuge in revelation (al-samʿ) from this perplexity, seeking God's help. The passage thus presents both a conceptual framework and a recognition of the limits of reason.

Key Terms
الوجود
التقدير
الصلاحية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the term 'aptitude' is used in the same literal sense for God as for creatures.
Correction: The author explicitly states that using 'aptitude' for the Eternal is metaphorical (tawassuʿ), while for the originated it is literal (ḥaqīqa).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is the word 'aptitude' used for the Eternal?
A) Literally
B) Metaphorically
C) Ambiguously
D) Not at all

Key Takeaway
Existence is finite, but God's determination is infinite, and 'aptitude' for God is a metaphorical expression.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that your existence is finite, but God's determination for you is infinite. In what specific area of your life have you been limiting yourself based on your own finite perception, rather than trusting the infinite determination of God? What will you change today?
?
The author says the term 'aptitude' is literal for you as a created being—meaning you are the receiver. When was the last time you genuinely positioned yourself as a receiver from God, not as the one in control? How can you practice this posture in your next prayer?
?
If God is 'worthier of bestowing' and you are 'more worthy of receiving', what does that say about your constant striving and worry? Can you name one thing you are currently anxious about that you can consciously hand over to God as the Giver, trusting His infinite determination?

Infinite Aspects of Possibility and Divine Knowledge and Power
Explaining the infinite aspects of possibility and God's knowledge and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the aspects of possibility are infinite and all require the Eternal to bring them into actual existence, and that God's knowledge and power encompass them in a unified manner.

Core Idea
Possible things have infinite aspects, but God brings them forth by a single act of knowledge and power.

Having established the finitude of existence, the author now turns to possibility. He states that the 'aspects of possibility' (jihāt al-imkān) are infinite, yet they all share a common need for an agent to actualize them. He then explains how God, through His knowledge and power, relates to these possibilities in a unified way.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on infinite aspects
The passage says the aspects of possibility are infinite and all share the same need for an external agent.
2
Note the role of divine knowledge
God's knowledge encompasses all possibilities in one unified aspect: the aptitude of knowledge for perception.
3
Note the role of divine power
God's power brings them into existence in one aspect: the aptitude of power for creation.
This is separate from specification by will.
