Rejected Positions on Divine Will
Al-Juwayni examines and rejects several views on divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five rejected views on divine will and the reasoning that leads Al-Juwayni to conclude that the will is not in a locus.

Core Idea
God's will cannot be denied, essential, eternal, originated, or in another essence.

Al-Juwayni examines several positions on the nature of divine will that he considers untenable. He systematically eliminates each one, showing why each leads to absurdity. This analysis sets the stage for affirming that the divine will is a meaning subsisting not in a locus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the argument.
Notice the repeated phrase 'لا وجه' which introduces each rejected view.
2
Identify the five rejected views
List each position that Al-Juwayni examines. For each one, note the specific reason he gives for rejecting it.
There are five distinct views: denial of will, will by essence, eternal will, originated will, and will in another essence.
3
Understand the elimination method
Observe how Al-Juwayni uses rational necessity to narrow down possibilities. Each rejected view is shown to lead to an absurdity, leaving only one logical option.
The conclusion is forced by rational necessity, not by revelation alone.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why the remaining possibility—that the will is not in a locus—is the only coherent option according to Al-Juwayni.
This conclusion avoids the problems of eternal wills, change in God, and attaching evil to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل : إنه لا وجه لإنكار الإرادة كما قال الكعبي ؛ لأنه يوجب أن تكون الأفعال غير اختيارية شبيهة بالأفعال الطبيعية عند أهل الطبائع، ولا وجه لإثبات كونه مريداً لذاته ؛ لأن الصفات الذاتية وجب تعميمها فهي عامة التعلق، وحينئذ وجب تعلق كونه مريدا بالفواحش والقبائح وذلك باطل كما سيأتي، ولا وجه لإثبات إرادة قديمة ؛ لأنه يؤدي إلى إثبات إلهين قديمين كما سبق أن الاشتراك في القدم يوجب الاشتراك في الإلهية، ولا وجه لإثبات كونها حادثة قائمة بذات الباري، ولا لإثبات كونها قائمة بذات أخرى لما سبق من المعنى، فتعين أنها لا في محل، فالضرورة العقلية ألجأت إلى تعيين هذا القسم من الأقسام.
It was said: There is no way to deny will as al-Ka‘bī said, because it would necessitate that actions are non-volitional, resembling natural actions according to the naturalists. And there is no way to affirm that He is willer (murīd) by His essence, because essential attributes must be universal in their relation, so they are general in attachment, and then His being willer would necessarily attach to obscenities and abominations, and that is false as will come. And there is no way to affirm an eternal will (irādah qadīmah), because it leads to affirming two eternal gods, as previously that sharing in eternity necessitates sharing in divinity. And there is no way to affirm that it is originated (ḥādithah) subsisting in the essence of the Creator, nor to affirm that it subsists in another essence due to the meaning mentioned earlier. So it is determined that it is not in a locus, so rational necessity compelled the determination of this division among the divisions.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a systematic survey and refutation of various positions on the divine will. Al-Juwayni considers and rejects five views. First, al-Ka‘bī's denial of will is rejected because it would make actions involuntary, like natural processes. Second, the view that God is willer by His essence (without a distinct attribute) is rejected because essential attributes have universal attachment, which would require that God wills evil and abominations, which is false. Third, the view that the will is eternal (qadīmah) is rejected because it would imply two eternal beings competing divinity. Fourth, the view that the will is originated (ḥādithah) and subsists in God's essence is rejected without repeating the earlier argument (apparently about change in the divine). Fifth, the view that it subsists in another essence is also rejected. The only remaining possibility is that the will is a meaning subsisting not in a locus (lā fī maḥall). Thus, rational necessity forces this conclusion.

Key Terms
إرادة
كعبي
ذاتي
قديم
حادث
محل
ضرورة عقلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The rejection of essential will means that God's will is not essential to Him.
Correction: Al-Juwayni rejects the view that will is identical with essence, not that will is a necessary attribute. The will is a real attribute, but not identical to essence, so it avoids universal attachment.

Checkpoint
Why is the view that God is willer by His essence rejected?
A) Because it would make actions non-volitional.
B) Because it would require God to will evil actions.
C) Because it leads to two eternal gods.
D) Because it implies change in God.

Key Takeaway
Rational necessity forces that the divine will is a meaning not subsisting in a locus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that denying God's will makes actions involuntary. Do you ever act as if your own will is irrelevant—as if you are merely a product of your environment or desires? How will you consciously assert your will in obedience to God today?
?
Al-Juwayni rejected the idea that God's will attaches to all things universally because it would include evil. Does the existence of evil in the world ever cause you to doubt God's will or justice? In light of this lesson, how can you reconcile the reality of evil with a will that is not universal in that sense?
?
The passage concludes with 'rational necessity' driving the correct belief. Where in your own faith are you relying on comfortable assumptions rather than letting rational necessity guide you to the truth about God? What specific assumption are you willing to question today?

Defending the Divine Will Not in a Locus: Analogical Arguments
Al-Juwayni defends will not in a locus by analogy to other accepted views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Juwayni uses analogies from other theological and philosophical schools to defend the Ash‘ari position that the divine will subsists not in a locus.

Core Idea
Accepting attributes not in a locus is already common among major schools.

In this passage, al-Juwayni strengthens the Ash‘ari doctrine of the divine will by showing that other respected schools already accept attributes that are not in a locus. He cites the philosophers' separate intellects, Jahm and Hisham's originated knowledges, and the Ash‘ariyya's own view on divine speech. By drawing these parallels, he argues that affirming the will as a meaning not in a locus is neither novel nor unreasonable.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage closely
Start by reading the Arabic text once without analyzing. Notice the rhythm and the names mentioned (philosophers, Jahm, Hishām, Ash‘ariyya).
The passage is a continuous argument; let the author's flow guide you.
2
Identify the three analogies
The passage lists three groups and their accepted attributes not in a locus: philosophers (separate intellects), Jahm and Hishām (originated sciences), and the Ash‘ariyya (speech). Underline each.
These are the building blocks of the analogy.
3
Understand the elaboration on speech
After listing the analogies, al-Juwayni explains how Ash‘ariyya affirm speech not in a locus: the eternal speech in God's essence did not transfer to Moses, and what Moses heard was a created sound not in a locus.
This clarifies that the analogy is not just hypothetical but rooted in established doctrine.
4
Reflect on the logical force of the analogy
Ask yourself: if these other attributes not in a locus are accepted, why should the divine will be rejected? The analogy shows consistency across the schools.
Al-Juwayni is using a 'consensus by analogy' argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحن لم نبعد النجعة في ذلك مع إثبات الفلاسفة عقولا مفارقة للأجسام قائمة بذواتها وهي مبادئ الموجودات، ومع إثبات جهم وهشام علوما حادثة لا في محل، ومع إثبات الأشعرية تكليما لا في محل ؛ لأن ذلك الكلام الذي في ذات الباري تعالى لم ينتقل إلى سمع موسى عليه السلام والذي سمعه موسى لم يكن في محل، فهم وإن لم يصرحوا بتكليم لا في محل كما صرحنا بتعظيم لا في محل غير أنهم أثبتوا كلاما مسموعا، والصفة الأزلية لم تنتقل ، وسمع موسى قد امتلى بالتكليم حتى يقال : إنه سمع كجر السلسلة.
And we have not gone far in this, with the philosophers affirming separate intellects (ʿuqūl mufāriqah) subsisting by themselves and being the principles of existents, and with Jahm and Hishām affirming originated sciences (ʿulūm ḥādithah) not in a locus, and with the Ash‘ariyya affirming speech (taklīm) not in a locus; because that speech which is in the essence of God Most High did not transfer to the hearing of Moses (peace be upon him), and what Moses heard was not in a locus. So they, even if they did not explicitly state that speech (taklīm) is not in a locus as we explicitly stated magnification (taʿẓīm) not in a locus, nevertheless they affirmed audible speech. And the eternal attribute did not transfer, and Moses' hearing was filled with the speech so that it is said: he heard like the sound of a chain.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage defends the Ash‘arī position that the divine will subsists not in a locus by appealing to analogous views accepted by other schools. Al-Juwayni points out that the philosophers affirm separate intellects (ʿuqūl mufāriqah) that are not in bodies, Jahm ibn Ṣafwān and Hishām ibn al-Ḥakam affirm originated knowledges (ʿulūm ḥādithah) not in a locus, and the Ash‘ariyya themselves affirm speech (kalām) not in a locus when they claim that the eternal speech in God's essence did not transfer to Moses' ears and that what Moses heard was a created sound not in a locus. Although the Ash‘ariyya do not explicitly say that speech is not in a locus, they effectively accept it by affirming that the revealed speech (the Qur'an) is a meaning in God's essence and the audible recitation is a created accident not in a locus. Al-Juwayni argues that if these views are accepted, then affirming the divine will as a meaning not in a locus is equally plausible.

Key Terms
عقول مفارقة
علوما حادثة
تكليم
كلام
سمع
محل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The analogy claims that the divine will is identical to the separate intellects or originated knowledges.
Correction: The analogy only shows that other schools accept attributes not in a locus; it does not equate the will with those attributes in nature.

Checkpoint
Which group does al-Juwayni cite as affirming 'originated sciences not in a locus'?
A) The philosophers
B) Jahm and Hishām
C) The Ash‘ariyya
D) The Mu‘tazila

Key Takeaway
Al-Juwayni defends the divine will not in a locus by appealing to accepted examples of attributes not in a locus from other schools.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you defend a theological belief, do you rely on analogies and the authority of other scholars, or do you have direct textual evidence? Are you willing to examine the strength of each analogy you use? Challenge yourself to find one belief you hold based only on analogy and test it against primary sources this week.
?
Al-Juwayni uses consensus-like reasoning (other schools accept the concept) to support his position. In your own life, how often do you accept a view because 'everyone else' does? Can you recall a time when popular opinion led you astray? Commit to verifying one major belief with direct evidence from revelation or reason.
?
If you were to defend a truth that few others accept, would you be as bold as Al-Juwayni? What does your answer reveal about your reliance on social validation rather than conviction from evidence? Pray for the courage to stand by truth even when it is unpopular.

The Will Is Not Willed
Al-Juwayni counters infinite regress by denying will is willed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Juwayni denies that the will itself is willed, and how this refutes the infinite regress objection against divine will.

Core Idea
The will is not willed—it does not require a prior will to exist.

Al-Juwayni now answers a key objection: if God's will is a specific action, wouldn't it need another will to choose it? He argues that the very nature of will prevents infinite regress. Let's see his concise counter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation. Notice the tone: Al-Juwayni is directly addressing an opponent's claim.
2
Identify the objection being countered
The passage begins with 'As for your saying...' — this is the Mu'tazila objection that affirming a will leads to infinite regress. Find it in the translation.
3
Understand 'will is not willed'
Al-Juwayni's main move: 'because the will is not willed (lā turādu)'. Reflect on what this means: a will is a self-initiating action that does not require a prior will to specify it.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Consider the human analogy
He asks: if our own will were willed by another will, would that not lead to infinite regress? We know that our will does not need another will — so neither does God's.
This strengthens the point by showing the absurdity.
5
Connect to larger argument
This defense preserves the doctrine of divine attributes. Without this move, the Mu'tazila could reject God's will as a genuine attribute. See how this passage fits into the broader discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولكم : إن حدثت إرادة فبإرادة أخرى تحدث ويتسلسل فغير مقبول ؛ لأن الإرادة لا تراد أليست إرادتنا لو كانت مرادة بإرادة أخرى أدت إلى التسلسل فلم تستدع الإرادة جنسها ؟
As for your saying: If a will occurs, then it occurs by another will and thus leads to infinite regress, it is not acceptable, because the will is not willed (lā turādu). Is it not that our will, if it were willed by another will, would lead to infinite regress? So the will does not require its own genre.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a direct counter-objection to the Mu‘tazila's argument that affirming a divine will leads to infinite regress. Al-Juwayni argues that the will (irādah) is not itself something that is willed (lā turādu). He draws an analogy to human will: if our will were the object of another will, that would indeed lead to an infinite chain. But since we know that is not the case for human will, it shows that will does not require another will to exist. Therefore, the objection fails. The argument hinges on the idea that will is a self-initiating action that does not need a prior will to specify it.

Key Terms
إرادة
تراد
تسلسل
جنس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Al-Juwayni is claiming God's will is uncaused or that it exists without any reason.
Correction: Al-Juwayni is not saying the will is uncaused; he is saying that the will as an action is not itself willed by another will. The will is a first action that does not require a prior specifying will.

Checkpoint
What reason does al-Juwayni give for rejecting the infinite regress objection?
A) The will is eternal and has no beginning.
B) The will is not something that is willed.
C) The will is identical with God's essence.
D) The will is a created accident.

Key Takeaway
The will does not need another will to exist; therefore, the objection of infinite regress is unfounded.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Juwayni says the will is not willed. Think of a decision you made today—did you ever wonder why you willed that specific thing? Could it be that your will itself needs to be willed by another will? How does this argument free you from that infinite loop? Write down one area in your life where you hesitate because you feel your will is not 'caused' enough. How will you act now?
?
The passage argues that will does not require its own genre. In your relationship with Allah, do you sometimes feel that your intention (niyya) is not 'pure enough' because it wasn't preceded by a more perfect intention? How does al-Juwayni's point that will is self-initiating apply to your excuses for not acting on faith? Commit to one action today without waiting for a 'prior will' to justify it.

Refuting Necessity: The Mu'tazila's Arbitrary Choice
Al-Juwayni refutes the Mu'tazila's necessity claim by listing alternative positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Juwayni refutes the Mu'tazila's claim that divine will must not be in a locus by showing that their position is not rationally necessary and that alternative views are equally plausible.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's claim of necessity is refuted by showing their choice is arbitrary.

This passage presents a rhetorical critique from al-Juwayni. He argues that the Mu'tazila have no rational necessity for their specific view on divine will; they could have chosen several other positions held by other sects. This shows that their claim to necessity is false, as true rational necessity would eliminate all but one possibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Learn that the Mu'tazila claimed rational necessity for their denial that God's will is in a locus. Al-Juwayni challenges this.
The Mu'tazila held that will is an attribute but not in a location (locus).
2
Read the argument
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice how al-Juwayni lists alternative positions to undermine their claim.
3
Identify the alternatives
Find the three alternative positions mentioned: al-Ka'bi's reduction of will to knowledge/power, al-Najjar's negative attribute view, and the Karamiyya's affirmation of multiple wills.
4
Reflect on the refutation
Understand that the existence of alternatives undermines the claim of necessity. The Mu'tazila's choice appears arbitrary, not compelled by reason.
True rational necessity would leave no room for other viable positions.
5
Avoid misunderstanding
Note that al-Juwayni is not endorsing these alternatives, but using them to show inconsistency in the Mu'tazila's claim.
The alternatives are presented as rhetorical devices, not as Ash'ari positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الأشعرية : الضرورة التي ادعيتم ليست الضرورة العقلية التي يضطر العقل إلى التزامها من إبطال قسم وتعيين قسم، فإنكم قدرتم من محال حتى حتى ارتكبتم محالا، والأقسام كلها بزعمكم محالات ، فلِمَ التزمتم هذا المحال دون مذهب الكعبي في رده الإرادة إلى العالمية والقادرية، كما رددتم كونه سميعا بصيرًا إلى كونه عليمًا خبيرا على قول ؟ وهلا التزمتم مذهب النجار في رد الإرادة إلى صفة النفي كمن أنه قال منكم : إنه سميع بصير؛ بمعنى لا آفة به، وهلا قلتم : هو مريد لنفسه كما قال النجار على رأي ولا تلتزموا عموم التعلق إلا فيما يصح أن يكون قادرًا على ما يصح أن يكون مقدوراً له ، وهلا التزمتم مذهب الكرامية في إثبات إرادات في ذاته ؛ لأن المحذور من ذلك الالتزام هو التغيير بالحوادث، والتغيير حاصل في الأحكام حسب حصوله في المعاني، إذا لم يكن مريدا فصار مريدا عندكم ،
The Ash‘ariyya said: The necessity you claim is not the rational necessity that compels the mind to adhere to rejecting one division and determining another, for you measured from the impossible until you committed the impossible, and all the divisions are, in your claim, impossibilities. So why did you adhere to this impossibility instead of the doctrine of al-Ka‘bī in reducing will to knowledge and power, as you reduced His being Hearing and Seeing to His being Knowing and Aware according to one view? And why did you not adhere to the doctrine of al-Najjār in reducing will to a negative attribute, like someone among you said: He is Hearing and Seeing in the sense of 'no affliction with Him'? And why did you not say: He is willer by His essence, as al-Najjār said according to an opinion, and do not accept universality of attachment except for what is valid that He be powerful over what is valid to be an object of power? And why did you not adhere to the doctrine of the Karāmiyya in affirming wills in His essence, because the objection to that adherence is change through originated things, and change occurs in rulings just as it occurs in meanings, since if He was not willer and then became willer according to you?
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
In this passage, al-Juwayni (speaking as the Ash‘ari spokesperson) criticizes the Mu‘tazila for claiming that their position that divine will is not in a locus is necessary. He argues that true rational necessity would eliminate all but one possibility, but here the Mu‘tazila admit that all possible positions are problematic (muḥālāt), yet they choose one without justification. He then offers several alternative positions that the Mu‘tazila could have adopted instead, implying that their claim of necessity is false. First, they could have followed al-Ka‘bī, who reduces will to knowledge and power (like they reduce 'hearing' and 'seeing' to 'knowing'). Second, they could have followed al-Najjār, who makes will a negative attribute (like 'no affliction' for hearing). Third, they could have said that God is willer by His essence, limiting the attachment of will only to what is possible. Fourth, they could have followed the Karāmiyya, who affirm multiple originated wills in God's essence, accepting change in God (which they already implicitly accept when they say God becomes willer). Thus, the Mu‘tazila's choice is arbitrary, not necessitated by reason.

Key Terms
ضرورة عقلية
كعبي
النجار
الكرمية
إرادة
صفة النفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari spokesperson is endorsing the alternative views as correct.
Correction: Al-Juwayni is not endorsing these views; he uses them rhetorically to show that the Mu'tazila's position is not uniquely necessary.

Checkpoint
Which alternative does al-Juwayni mention as reducing will to a negative attribute?
A) Al-Ka‘bī's view
B) Al-Najjār's view
C) The Karāmiyya's view
D) The Ash‘ariyya's view

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's claim that divine will cannot be in a locus is not rationally necessary, as several alternative positions exist that are equally plausible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself insisting on a particular belief about God, have you ever examined whether your certainty comes from rational necessity or from mere habit or tradition? Which of your own theological convictions might be an arbitrary choice you have never questioned?
?
Al-Juwayni shows that even scholars can hold views without genuine rational compulsion. Is there a belief you hold about religion that you have never seriously doubted? What would it cost you to admit that you might be wrong about it?
?
If you were to apply Al-Juwayni's method—listing alternative plausible positions—to your own deepest conviction, which one would be hardest to defend as rationally necessary? Will you honestly reconsider it today?

Refutation of the Infinite Regress of Wills
Sanusi refutes the infinite regress objection by asserting that will is not willed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that will is not willed, thus avoiding an infinite regress.

Core Idea
Will is not willed; it does not require its kind.

In this passage, Sanusi responds to a philosophical objection against the Ash'ari doctrine of divine will. The opponent argues that if a temporal will occurs, it must be caused by another will, leading to an infinite regress. Sanusi rejects this by pointing out that will itself is not an object of will — it does not require another will to exist. This principle is illustrated by human will, which also does not depend on a prior will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first a reference to God's speech, then the objection about will, then the response.
Pay attention to the key phrase 'الإرادة لا تراد'.
2
Identify the objection
Find the opponent's claim: that a temporal will would require another will, leading to infinite regress.
3
Understand the response
The response is that will is not willed; it does not need its kind. This stops the regress.
The example of human will shows that will does not require another will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يصرحوا بتكليم لا في محل كما صرحنا بتعظيم لا في محل غير أنهم أثبتوا كلاما مسموعا، والصفة الأزلية لم تنتقل، وسمع موسى قد امتلى بالتكليم حتى يقال: إنه سمع كجر السلسلة. وأما قولكم: إن حدثت إرادة فبإرادة أخرى تحدث ويتسلسل فغير مقبول؛ لأن الإرادة لا تراد. أليست إرادتنا لو كانت مرادة بإرادة أخرى أدت إلى التسلسل فلم تستدع الإرادة جنسها؟
They state that He speaks with a speech not in a locus, as we have stated, with a glorification not in a locus, except that they affirmed a heard speech. The eternal attribute did not transfer, and the hearing of Moses was filled with the speaking so that it is said: He heard like the drag of a chain. And as for your saying: If a will occurs, then by another will it occurs and leads to infinite regress, it is unacceptable, because will is not willed. Is not our will, if it were willed by another will, lead to infinite regress? Thus will does not require its kind.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is an argument defending the Ash'ari position on divine will against an objection of infinite regress. It begins by referencing the Ash'ari claim that God speaks with a speech not in a locus, and that this speech is heard (as by Moses). The opponent then raises an objection: if a temporal will occurs, it would require another will to cause it, leading to an infinite regress. The Ash'ari response is that the objection is invalid because 'will is not willed'—meaning will itself is not an object of another will. To illustrate, they point to human will: if our will were willed by another will, that would also lead to infinite regress, so clearly will does not require its kind (i.e., another will) to exist. Thus the regress is avoided.

Key Terms
إرادة
تسلسل
كلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a will must itself be caused by a prior will to avoid an uncaused cause.
Correction: The Ash'ari argue that will is not the kind of thing that is willed; it is a first cause and does not require a prior will.

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari response to the objection that a divine will would require an infinite regress of wills?
A) They accept infinite regress.
B) They say will is not willed.
C) They deny divine will.
D) They say will is created.

Key Takeaway
Will does not require a prior will; it is not willed, thus the infinite regress is avoided.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a decision, do you ever pause to realize that your very will to choose is itself not willed by you but given by Allah? How does that truth confront your sense of self-sufficiency?
?
If will is not willed, then your inclinations and desires are not fully under your control. Consider a time you felt proud of a good intention: did you attribute that will to yourself alone? What would change if you consciously thanked Allah for granting you even the will to do good?
?
The lesson shows that will does not require its kind—meaning Allah's will is absolutely independent, uncaused by anything. Do you truly live as if nothing constrains Allah's will? What fears or assumptions in your heart resist that absolute independence, and how can you surrender them today?

The Ash'ari Critique of the Claim of Necessity and al-Ka'bi's Reduction
Ash'ariyya's opening critique and question about al-Ka'bi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ariyya reject the opponent's claim of rational necessity and present al-Ka'bi's reduction of divine will to knowledge and power as an alternative.

Core Idea
The opponent's necessity is not rational but arbitrary.

The Ash'ariyya open their critique by challenging the very foundation of the opponent's argument: the claim of rational necessity. They argue that the opponent has posited a set of impossible alternatives and arbitrarily chosen one. To expose this arbitrariness, they cite the alternative view of al-Ka'bi, who reduces divine will to knowledge and power. This lesson examines that opening salvo.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the rhetorical structure: the Ash'ariyya first deny the necessity, then ask why the opponent didn't adopt al-Ka'bi's view.
2
Identify the two key moves
The passage makes two moves: (1) denying the opponent's necessity, and (2) presenting al-Ka'bi's reduction as a logical alternative. See how the question at the end challenges the opponent's consistency.
3
Reflect on the concept of rational necessity
Consider what 'rational necessity' means in kalam. Why is it important for the opponent to claim necessity? The Ash'ariyya argue that the necessity is not genuine but rhetorical.
This connects to the broader debate about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الأشعرية: الضرورة التي ادعيتم ليست الضرورة العقلية التي يضطر العقل إلى التزامها من إبطال قسم وتعيين قسم، فإنكم قدرتم من محال حتى ارتكبتم محالا، والأقسام كلها بزعمكم محالات، فلِمَ التزمتم هذا المحال دون مذهب الكعبي في رده الإرادة إلى العالمية والقادرية، كما رددتم كونه سميعا بصيرا إلى كونه عليما خبيرا على قول؟ وهلا التزمتم مذهب النجار في رد الإرادة إلى صفة النفي كمن أنه قال منكم: إنه سميع بصير؛ بمعنى لا آفة به، وهلا قلتم: هو مريد لنفسه كما قال النجار على رأي ولا تلتزموا عموم التعلق إلا فيما يصح أن يكون قادرا على ما يصح أن يكون مقدورا له، وهلا التزمتم مذهب الكرامية في إثبات إرادات في ذاته؛ لأن المحذور من ذلك الالتزام هو التغيير بالحوادث، والتغيير حاصل في الأحكام حسب حصوله في المعاني، إذا لم يكن مريدا فصار مريدا عندكم، فإذا لم يدل تغيير الأحكام على الحدوث لم يدل تغيير المعاني أيضا على الحدوث، أو هل قلتم: هو مريد بإرادات في محل كما قلتم: هو متكلم بكلام يخلقه في محل؟ وما الفرق بين البابين فإن الإرادة إن أوجبت حكما لمن قامت به فليوجب الكلام كذلك، وإن كان المتكلم من فعل الكلام على أصلكم فاصطلحوا على أن المريد من فعل الإرادة، ثم يرجع الحكم إلى الفاعل كما يرجع في كونه عدلا يخلق العدل، فكيف التزمتم أبعد الأقسام قبولا عن كونه معقولا؟
The Ash'ariyya said: The necessity that you claimed is not the rational necessity that forces the intellect to commit to negating one division and specifying another, for you have supposed something impossible until you committed something impossible, and all divisions according to you are impossibilities. So why did you commit to this impossibility rather than the doctrine of al-Ka'bi in reducing will to knowledge and power, just as you reduced His being hearing and seeing to His being knowing and aware according to a saying? And why did you not commit to the doctrine of al-Najjar in reducing will to a negative attribute, as he said among you: He is hearing and seeing meaning there is no defect in Him, and why did you not say: He is willing by His essence as al-Najjar said in an opinion, and you do not commit to universal relation except in that which it is correct for Him to be powerful over that which it is correct to be an object of power for Him? And why did you not commit to the doctrine of al-Karramiyya in affirming wills in His essence? Because the harm of that commitment is change by accidents, and change occurs in rulings as it occurs in meanings, since He was not willing and then became willing according to you. So if change in rulings does not indicate temporality, then change in meanings also does not indicate temporality. Or did you say: He is willing by wills in a locus just as you said: He is speaking by speech He creates in a locus? And what is the difference between the two categories? For if will necessitates a ruling for the one in whom it inheres, then let speech necessitate likewise. And if the speaker is the one who performs speech according to your principle, then you may agree that the willing one is the one who performs will. Then the ruling refers to the agent as it refers in His being just (creating justice). So how did you commit to the most remote of divisions from being acceptable to being intelligible?
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a detailed critique by the Ash'ariyya of their opponents' position on divine will. The Ash'ariyya argue that the necessity claimed by the opponent is not a true rational necessity that forces one to accept one division and reject others. They accuse the opponent of positing a set of impossible alternatives and then arbitrarily choosing one. The Ash'ariyya then survey alternative theological positions: al-Ka'bi reduced divine will to knowledge and power; al-Najjar interpreted will as a negative attribute (like 'hearing' meaning 'not deaf'); and al-Karramiyya affirmed multiple wills in God's essence. The Ash'ariyya ask why the opponent did not adopt any of these positions instead. They further point out an inconsistency: the opponent accepts change in God's rulings (acts) as not implying temporality, so change in meanings (attributes) should also not imply temporality. Finally, they question why the opponent accepts that God speaks by creating speech in a locus but does not accept that God wills by creating will in a locus, arguing that the two cases are parallel. The passage ends by denouncing the opponent's choice as the least acceptable and intelligible.

Key Terms
كعبي
عالمية
قادرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent's claim of necessity is rationally compelling.
Correction: The Ash'ariyya argue that the necessity claimed is not rational but arbitrary, as the opponent could have chosen other equally possible alternatives.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ariyya, why is the opponent's claimed necessity not true rational necessity?
A) Because it does not compel the intellect to accept one division
B) Because it is based on revelation, not reason
C) Because the opponent did not provide a proof
D) Because the opponent rejected al-Ka'bi's view

Key Takeaway
The opponent's claim of necessity is invalid because true rational necessity would force a unique conclusion, but the opponent's alternatives are equally possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief because you assumed it was the only rational option, but later realized other possibilities were equally valid? How does that affect your confidence in your current convictions about Allah's attributes?
?
The Ash'ariyya challenge the opponent's claim of necessity by pointing to an arbitrary choice. Is there an area of your faith where you demand absolute certitude, yet the evidence admits alternative interpretations? What would it cost you to admit that your position is not forced by reason but chosen?
?
Al-Ka'bi reduces divine will to knowledge and power, simplifying the divine nature. Are you ever tempted to simplify Allah's attributes to avoid complexity or mystery? How does that reflect on your trust in His transcendence versus your desire for understanding?

6 min
Al-Najjar's Negative Attribute and al-Karramiyya's Multiple Wills
Ash'ariyya present al-Najjar and al-Karramiyya alternatives and argue about change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative positions of al-Najjar and al-Karramiyya on divine will, and the Ash'ariyya's argument about change and temporality.

Core Idea
Will can be understood as a negative attribute or multiple wills without implying temporality.

The Ash'ariyya continue their critique by presenting two more alternatives that the opponent could have adopted: al-Najjar's view that will is a negative attribute (like 'hearing' meaning 'not deaf'), and al-Karramiyya's view that God has multiple wills in His essence. The opponent, however, does not accept either. The Ash'ariyya then point out a key inconsistency: if change in God's rulings (acts) does not imply temporality, then change in meanings (attributes) should also not imply temporality, undermining the opponent's objection to multiple wills.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the two alternatives
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify where al-Najjar's view is presented (will as negative attribute) and where al-Karramiyya's view is presented (multiple wills).
2
Understand the change argument
The Ash'ariyya argue that if change in rulings (like creating) does not imply that God is temporal, then change in attributes (like acquiring a new will) should also not imply temporality. This is a direct challenge to the opponent's consistency.
The opponent accepts change in actions but rejects change in attributes.
3
Compare the two alternatives
Consider how al-Najjar's view avoids the issue of change by denying a positive attribute, while al-Karramiyya's view embraces multiple wills but argues that change does not entail temporality.
This shows the range of possible positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الأشعرية: الضرورة التي ادعيتم ليست الضرورة العقلية التي يضطر العقل إلى التزامها من إبطال قسم وتعيين قسم، فإنكم قدرتم من محال حتى ارتكبتم محالا، والأقسام كلها بزعمكم محالات، فلِمَ التزمتم هذا المحال دون مذهب الكعبي في رده الإرادة إلى العالمية والقادرية، كما رددتم كونه سميعا بصيرا إلى كونه عليما خبيرا على قول؟ وهلا التزمتم مذهب النجار في رد الإرادة إلى صفة النفي كمن أنه قال منكم: إنه سميع بصير؛ بمعنى لا آفة به، وهلا قلتم: هو مريد لنفسه كما قال النجار على رأي ولا تلتزموا عموم التعلق إلا فيما يصح أن يكون قادرا على ما يصح أن يكون مقدورا له، وهلا التزمتم مذهب الكرامية في إثبات إرادات في ذاته؛ لأن المحذور من ذلك الالتزام هو التغيير بالحوادث، والتغيير حاصل في الأحكام حسب حصوله في المعاني، إذا لم يكن مريدا فصار مريدا عندكم، فإذا لم يدل تغيير الأحكام على الحدوث لم يدل تغيير المعاني أيضا على الحدوث، أو هل قلتم: هو مريد بإرادات في محل كما قلتم: هو متكلم بكلام يخلقه في محل؟ وما الفرق بين البابين فإن الإرادة إن أوجبت حكما لمن قامت به فليوجب الكلام كذلك، وإن كان المتكلم من فعل الكلام على أصلكم فاصطلحوا على أن المريد من فعل الإرادة، ثم يرجع الحكم إلى الفاعل كما يرجع في كونه عدلا يخلق العدل، فكيف التزمتم أبعد الأقسام قبولا عن كونه معقولا؟
The Ash'ariyya said: The necessity that you claimed is not the rational necessity that forces the intellect to commit to negating one division and specifying another, for you have supposed something impossible until you committed something impossible, and all divisions according to you are impossibilities. So why did you commit to this impossibility rather than the doctrine of al-Ka'bi in reducing will to knowledge and power, just as you reduced His being hearing and seeing to His being knowing and aware according to a saying? And why did you not commit to the doctrine of al-Najjar in reducing will to a negative attribute, as he said among you: He is hearing and seeing meaning there is no defect in Him, and why did you not say: He is willing by His essence as al-Najjar said in an opinion, and you do not commit to universal relation except in that which it is correct for Him to be powerful over that which it is correct to be an object of power for Him? And why did you not commit to the doctrine of al-Karramiyya in affirming wills in His essence? Because the harm of that commitment is change by accidents, and change occurs in rulings as it occurs in meanings, since He was not willing and then became willing according to you. So if change in rulings does not indicate temporality, then change in meanings also does not indicate temporality. Or did you say: He is willing by wills in a locus just as you said: He is speaking by speech He creates in a locus? And what is the difference between the two categories? For if will necessitates a ruling for the one in whom it inheres, then let speech necessitate likewise. And if the speaker is the one who performs speech according to your principle, then you may agree that the willing one is the one who performs will. Then the ruling refers to the agent as it refers in His being just (creating justice). So how did you commit to the most remote of divisions from being acceptable to being intelligible?
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a detailed critique by the Ash'ariyya of their opponents' position on divine will. The Ash'ariyya argue that the necessity claimed by the opponent is not a true rational necessity that forces one to accept one division and reject others. They accuse the opponent of positing a set of impossible alternatives and then arbitrarily choosing one. The Ash'ariyya then survey alternative theological positions: al-Ka'bi reduced divine will to knowledge and power; al-Najjar interpreted will as a negative attribute (like 'hearing' meaning 'not deaf'); and al-Karramiyya affirmed multiple wills in God's essence. The Ash'ariyya ask why the opponent did not adopt any of these positions instead. They further point out an inconsistency: the opponent accepts change in God's rulings (acts) as not implying temporality, so change in meanings (attributes) should also not imply temporality. Finally, they question why the opponent accepts that God speaks by creating speech in a locus but does not accept that God wills by creating will in a locus, arguing that the two cases are parallel. The passage ends by denouncing the opponent's choice as the least acceptable and intelligible.

Key Terms
نجار
كرامية
صفة النفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming multiple wills in God necessarily implies temporality.
Correction: The Ash'ariyya argue that if change in God's acts does not imply temporality, then change in attributes also does not, so multiple wills are not inherently temporal.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ariyya, how does change in rulings (أحكام) relate to change in meanings (معاني) regarding temporality?
A) Change in rulings implies temporality but change in meanings does not
B) If change in rulings does not imply temporality, then change in meanings also does not
C) Change in meanings implies temporality but change in rulings does not
D) Both imply temporality equally

Key Takeaway
The opponent is inconsistent: they accept change in God's actions without deeming it temporal, but reject change in attributes on the same grounds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you willing to admit that your theology might contain double standards—accepting change in God's actions while denying it in His attributes? What would it cost you to be consistent?
?
If God's will is a negative attribute, how does that challenge your personal sense of God's involvement in your life? Can you trust a God whose will is not a positive relation to you?
?
This argument forces you to examine whether your conception of God is shaped more by logical consistency or by inherited tradition. Which one has the final say in your heart?

Divine Attributes
advanced
5 min
The Parallel Between Will and Speech: A Final Critique
Ash'ariyya's argument from parallelism between will and speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ariyya's argument that will and speech are analogous, and why the opponent's rejection of created will is inconsistent.

Core Idea
If God can create speech in a locus, He can create will in a locus — the two cases are parallel.

In this final part of their critique, the Ash'ariyya draw a direct parallel between divine will and divine speech. They note that the opponent already accepts that God speaks by creating speech in a locus (e.g., the bush). So why not accept that God wills by creating will in a locus? The Ash'ariyya argue that the same logic applies, and if the opponent insists on a different rule, they must explain the difference. The passage ends with a sharp rhetorical question, denouncing the opponent's position as the least acceptable and intelligible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the parallel
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the comparison: God is said to be 'speaking by speech He creates in a locus' — similarly, could He not be 'willing by will He creates in a locus'?
2
Understand the argument from parallelism
The Ash'ariyya argue that if will necessitates a ruling for the one in whom it inheres, then speech should do the same. And if the speaker is the one who performs speech, then the willing one should be the one who performs will. This mirrors their own doctrine of acquirance.
This is a sophisticated argument that exposes a double standard.
3
Reflect on the concluding rhetorical question
The passage ends with 'So how did you commit to the most remote of divisions from being acceptable to being intelligible?' This denounces the opponent's choice as arbitrary and irrational.
This ties back to the opening critique of necessity.

Source Text

Because the harm of that commitment is change by accidents, and change occurs in rulings as it occurs in meanings, since He was not willing and then became willing according to you. So if change in rulings does not indicate temporality, then change in meanings also does not indicate temporality. Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Will and speech are fundamentally different categories and cannot be compared.
Correction: The Ash'ariyya argue that the same logical principles apply to both, so the opponent's rejection of created will while accepting created speech is inconsistent.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ariyya, what is the consequence if the speaker is defined as the one who performs speech?
A) The willing one should be defined as the one who performs will
B) The speaker must be the locus of speech
C) Speech cannot be created in a locus
D) Will must inhere in the subject

Key Takeaway
The opponent's refusal to extend the same logic from speech to will reveals an arbitrary inconsistency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you willing to accept the full implications of your own beliefs, even when they extend to areas you find uncomfortable? This lesson shows the danger of arbitrary inconsistency. Where in your own life do you hold positions that contradict the logic of other positions you hold?
?
When you defend a theological position, do you do so with the same rigor you would demand of an opponent? The Ash'ariyya here hold the opponent to their own standard. In your own discussions about faith, do you apply the same criteria to yourself that you apply to others?
?
The passage ends with a scathing question about committing to the most remote of divisions. Have you ever clung to a position simply because it was familiar or comfortable, rather than because it was rationally sound? What would it take for you to abandon an inconsistency in your own thinking?
